THE RED PAGE
Adventure Jack London (Dymock’s). The
adventure of a tall, resolute man, who
strong even in sickness, is added to
the adventure of a “slender, level-browed
girl, with romance shining out of her
grey eyes,” and a Colt’s revolver on her
hip, and they become one adventurer. The
scene is the South Seas, and the man-
ner is London’s best.
Anatol Arthur Schnitzler, paraphrased for
y* e . , l E^ l } sh sta S» by Granville Barker
.(Sidgwmk and Jackson). The love
affairs of Anatol, a gay bachelor of
Vienna, are related in seven sparkling
dialogues. One of them, “A Farewell
Supper, has been staged by Barker at
the 1 alace Theatre, "London. The others
are too slight to act well; but they have
much the same interest for the reader
as The Dolly Dialogues.
Here and Hereafter— Barry Pain (Sydney
B 9 ak .p lu b). The “hereafter” does not
suit Barry Pain; but there are suffi-
cient stories in lighter vein to make this
collection worth getting.
Impatient ■ Gris el da —Laurence North (Dy-
mock’s). This “comedy in resolved
discords’ is told vivaciously. It be-
longs to that class of clever stories
which you generally get in The Smart
Set.
Phrynette and London— Marthe Troly-Cur-
tin (Dymock’s). The manners and cus-
toms of the English are submitted to
some amusing criticism by a sprightly
young lady from Paris. Her father’s
friends were artists, and he had let her
do as she liked, and read anvthing “ex-
cept Zola and the Bible.” Her talk is
sometimes horribly “risky” to her Eng-
lish friends.
Ihe Higlit Side of Paris —E. B. D’Auvergne
(Werner Laurie). Here you are—
Paris amusing herself; the Boule Miche,
Montmarte; bohemians, apaches, demi-
mondaines ; all the nicest people in alb
the glory of gaslight—only one shilling.
Aphorisms and Reflections —T. H. Huxley
(R.P.A. reprint). A good sixpen'orth
of cold iron to\ chew.
“The Readers’ Library,”' published by
Duckworth at 2s 6d. net per volume, includes
some excellent books of comparatively recent
date: — A Commentary by John Galsworthy,
Essays tn Freedom by H. W. Nevinson, and
I rogress and other Sketches by Cunning-
hame Graham. Richard Jefferies is an
earlier and not yet forgotten writer, and in
his Bevis you have one of the best of boy’s
stories. . Amaryllis at the Fair is a novel of
provincial English, as seen by a milder and
kindlier Hardy. In it Jefferies dwells for
a page on the merits of a leg of mutton, a
deserving joint which had not been honored
by any previous writer. W. H. Hudson
is quite as fine a stylist as Jefferies, and his
Green Mansions, and The Purple 'Land,
which treat of South American scenery and
adventures, are books which leave long and
pleasant memories.
* * * * '
Macmillan and Co. are reissuing many of
their popular novels in neat little cloth-
bound volumes, which sell here at 9d. each.
Wells’s Tono-Bungay, Marion Crawford’s
Corleone and Adventures of Elizabeth (of
the German Garden) in Riigen are the latest
additions. A correspondent who asked re-
cently for an opinion as to the best of
Marion Crawford’s novels is referred to A
Cigarette Maker’s Romance, also published
in this series.
* * * * .
There is not so much “bite”"in John Gals-
worthy’s pictures of British aristocracy in
The Patrician (Dymock’s) as there was in
The Man of Property and The Country
House. The ink of the satirist becomes less
mordant as he grows older, but his lines
are as fine as ever. The principal interest
of this book is in the careful portraits of
Lord Miltoun and at least three other
members of the ancient Caradoc family.
If the Lloyd George Budget meant the be-
ginning of the patricians’ end, posterity
will get a very impartial view of the last
of the tribe in Galsworthy’s book.
The action of the story is within the souls
of two pairs of lovers. Miltoun, who is
on the verge of wrecking a brilliant future,
receives most attention, and the conflict be-
tween love and duty can be seen straining
and rending his finely-tempered nature. He
and the rest are shown as puppets in the
hands of Fate; for to Galsworthy, as to
Democritus, “character is fate.”
Galsworthy is more poetical than usual.
There, are several passages in which jiis de-
scriptions of the time and the place are shot
through with a subtle symbolism. Note
the delicate significance of “blood-red” in
this passage
Lady Casterley raised her glass, full of a
blood-red wine. “The war would save us,”
she said.
“War is no joke.”
“It would be the beginning of a better
state of things.”
“You think so?”
“We should get the lead again as a na-
tion, afid Democracy would be put back 50
years.”
The Patrician is a splendid addition to an
already notable achievement in literature.
According to Kipling, who ought to
know, when ’Omer wandered about with
his bloomin’ lyre, ’e ’eard old songs turn
up again, and in modern days old fashions
in fiction are revived as often as old
fashions in dress, thus bearing out Nietz-
sche s doctrine of the eternal return of all
things in nature. De Morgan began in the
Dickens manner as Winston Churchill in
the Thackerayan, and a newcomer from
America reverts to the style of Reade’s
The Cloister and the\Hearth, a landmark
on the highway of romance, which runs past
Dumas back to the earliest days of prose
fiction.
On The Broad Highway of Jeffery
Farnol (George Robertson and Co.) you
will find many adventures which haye hap-
pened before, and characters old in story.
In Chapter I. the family lawyer reads the
will in which £20,000 is left to one nephew,
a dashing buck and friend of the Prince
Regent—“in the fervent hope that it may
help him to the Devil within the year,”
and £lO to another nephew, a quiet, schol-
arly chap; with a special bequest of
£500,000 to whichever of the two shall,
within one year, marry Lady Sophia
Sefton, the reigning toast of the day. The
end may be foreseen. The happenings on
the way provide reasonable .entertainment
if you skip the author’s philosophising.
A
HUNTING
MORN.
That cock that is too young to kill
Crows from his heart upon the hill,
And through the hedgetop’s ragged crest
Ihe tinker’s smoke blows curling west;
.Clatters and cups make great carouse,
hull joyous at the rich man’s house.
Horns Mowing, October flowing,
They are all a-hunting going.
The spotted lurchers turn their bones
Over and over the paving stones,
While Dick the dogrnan in the yard
Kisses the cookmaid well and hard,
Till on the lawn, with high disdain,
My lady’s nag, yclept Elaine,
Viewing the vulgar scene of love,
Sighs for a pear from her mistress’s glove.
Horns blowing, October flowing,
They are all a-hunting going.
But now the merry redcoats race,'
Yip, Yip, Halloo! A madcap pace—
Out of the -house and down the stairs,
Lord John Jolly with Miss Bellairs,
The Count de l’Orme and Squire Jones,
Afire to break his new-set bones;
The parson’s pretty actress wife,
The parson too, I’ll stake my life;
The little lawyer, looking big
Beneath his teeny-weeny wig;
Gruff Major Smith, plain Mister Brown,
Hunch-a-bunch from his desk in town;
The Marchioness of Morningside,
And fat Sir Sneego with his bride:
Laughing, stumping, squeezing, thumping,
Down they come like crickets jumping.
Solitary by the table,
Drinking ale for all he’s able,
Sitteth Jeames in reverie,
While the bugles ring with glee.
Horns Mowing, October flowing,
They are all a-hunting going ;
A ll... .except poor Mister Jeames!
Part XLII. of The Forest Flora of
iV.S.W., by J. H. Maiden, contains
the usual full descriptions of Western
Beefwood, Blue Mallee, Fringed (or Sally)
Wattle, Flax-leaved Wattle, and Oliver’s
Sassafras, together with some notes on tim-
bers (particularly N.S.W.) suitable for car-
riage building.
J. H. M. Abbott begins his little book —
William Dampier (Angus and Robertson)
with a useful reminder to children that,
though the Olden Times of fairy tale are
supposed to be the only romantic times, the
present is perhaps the most romantic time
that has ever been. Adventure is still to be
had in plenty, and piracy is more profitable
than in the days of Dampier. The pirate of
to-day walks the streets in silk hat and frock
coat, and is, quite possibly, a vegetarian.
Abbott mentions that “the favorite food of
the buccaneers was raw marrow.” It needed
a diet of that kind and the life of devils
for men to fight like devils. Dampier was
a pirate by accident, and his heart was not
in the business. He was an adventurer,
one of those who must be for ever roaming
and doing things in the open air. Though
Abbott’s statement that Dampier was one
of the wisest of the adventurers could be
disproved from his own pages, he was cer-
tainly one of the Iravest, and he was
also able to observe carefully, and record ac-
curately all that he saw in language of ad-
mirable simplicity. Dampier’s Voyages is
an enjoyable book to read, and it is a pity
that there is no cheap edition of it. Abbott
has told the .story of Dampier’s life, with
liberal quotations from the Australian chap-
ters of the Voyages, in such a way as to
interest school children in one of the great
navigators who helped to rescue Australia
from the unknown. The author evidently
began his task with a genuine love of his
subject, and that went far to make it a good
piece of work.
A fair girl, plenty of horses, and an agree-
able specimeh of Australian manhood, will
commend May Forsyth’s Peter of Gunneroy
to Australian readers. The girl came out
from England as Mrs. Mitchell, leaving
somebody supposed to be her husband be-
hind. She didn’t think much of Australians
at first
‘‘lt takes a little time to get used to their
voices. I tried to talk to them, and find
out if they had any hobbies, or were keen
about anything—-but all I gathered was that
there was not time for reading in Australia,
and that I’d find out it wasn’t a climate in
which to do anything requiring exertion.”
The Bush altered her first impressions, but
the gum trees are not entitled to all the credit
for her admiration
As she sat there with her whip bent
across her knees, and her eyes looking up at
him, she realised that she had loved him
from the very beginning, and that was whv
the bush to her had been full of such extraor-
dmary significance, and the sunshine which
had burned into her very heart was only be-
cause the bush, the grandness of it, the bril-
liant golden sunshine, all meant Peter.
That, of course, is as it should be in
a love story, and this is a fresh, breezy
treatment of the old subject that never grows
stale.
From Vic Yicta, a novel by A. E. Carey
(Greenings): “The situation bristled with
dilemmas. _ The Colonel thought he had the
key to their solution in his pocket, but he
was hedged about with reticences which
shackled his efforts. There was a crop of
difficulty to be reaped. . . .”
Part 11. (omitted from the book). ‘‘Then
he heard the stealthy footprint. ‘Ha, ha,
the villain still pursues me.’ On the one
hand the conflagration was licking the
heavens with resistless fury, on the other
the flood-waters were steadily rising, behind
him the deadly fuse was fired and a yawn-
ing chasm gaped at his feet. The Colonel
murmured and, shackled though he
was, he threw himself with one bound over
the bristling hedge of dilemmas. . . . And
that is how a crop of difficulty was reaped
in the brave days of old.”
Randolph “Billy Pagan’’ began
his strenuous life in the pages of True Eyes
and the Whirlwind. There he related his
own adventures; but as his native modesty
might prevent him from doing himself jus-
tice, the author plays Dr. Watson to his
Sherlock Holmes in a further instalment of
his career —Billy Pagan : Mining Engineer
(Bookstall Co., Is.). A habit of putting
two and two together and drawing con-
clusions is only one of Billy’s gifts. What
he doesn’t know about “wild cats” and
other mines is not worth knowing. A
merry, brave, likeable chap, an Australian
of the best type, all his doings 'in this book
are interesting, and the reader hopes to
meet Billy again.
THE TOWN OF HAY.
-The town of Hay is far away,
•The town of Hay is far;
.Between its hills of green and gray
T Hs winding meadows are.
Within the quiet town of Hay
Is many a quiet glen,
And there by many a shaded way
Are homes of quiet men:
And there are many hearts alwav
That turn with longing, night and day,
Back to the town of Hay.
Within that good old town of Hay
There was no pride of birth,
And no man there pursued his way
A stranger in the earth-;
none were high and none were low,
Of golden hair or gray,
And each would grieve at other’s woe
Down in the town of Hay;
And many a world-scorned soul to-dav
Mid crowded thousands far away
Weeps for the town of Hay.
A road leads from the town of Hay
Forth to a world of din,
And winds and wanders far away,—
„ And many walked therein ;
Far in the crowds of toil and stress
, Their restless footsteps stray,—
Their souls have lost the quietness
Of that old town of Hay ;
But in some respite, of the fray,
In transient dreams they float away,
Back to the town of Hay.
Old men are in that town of Hay,
Amid its quiet trees,
Who dream of strong sons far away
Upon the stormy seas ;
Old mothers, when the twilight dew
The woodbine leaves have pearled,
Dream of their boys who wander through
The wideness of the world :
And tears fall in the'twilight gray,
And prayers go up at close of day
In that old town of Hay.
A hillside in the'town of Hay
Is slanting toward the sun,
And gathered ’neath its headstones gray
Are sleepers one by one;
And there are tears in distant lands,
And grief too deep for tears,
And farewells waved from phantom hands
Across the gulf of years:
And when they place that headstone gray
It crushes hearts so far away
From that old town of Hay.
SAM WALTER FOSS.
WHEN A MAN’S OUT OF A JOB.
All Nature is sick from her heels to her hair,
W’en a feller is out of a job;
She is all out of kilter and out of repair,
W’en a feller is out of a job;
Ain’t no juice in the earth an’ no salt in the
sea, ,
Ain’t no ginger in life'in this land of the
free, /
An’ the Universe ain’t what it’s cracked up
to be, - ' . •
W’en a feller is out of. a job.
W’at’s the good of blue skies, an’ of blossom-
ing trees, 1 „ V,'* *. *
„ W’en a feller is "out of a job?
W’en your boy hez large patches on both of
his knees, .
An’ a feller is out of a job?
Them patches, I say, look so big in your eye
That they shut out the lan’Scape an’ cover
the sky, i : .
An’ the sun can’t shine through ’em the best
,it can. try, ■ , ' , > • ■ -
W’en a feller is out of a job.
W’en a man has no part in the work of the
earth, -
W’en a feller is out of a job,
He feels the whole blundering mistake of his
birth,
W’en a feller is out of a job;
He feels he’s no share in the whole of the
plan,
That he’s got the mitten from Natur’s own
ban’,
That he’s a rejected and left-over man,
W’en a feller is out of a job.
For you’ve jest lost your holt with the rest
of the crowd, •
W’en a feller is out of a job;
An’ you feel like d dead man with nary a
shroud, .
W’en a feller is out of a job.
You’re crawling around, but you’re out of
the game ;
You may hustle about, but you’re dead just
the same— l '
You’re dead with no tombstone to puff up
your name,
W’en a feller is out of a job.
SAM WALTER FOSS.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Revelation.
Beyond the uttermost ways of soaring dream
I sought for God, but found myself alone.
My heart within my breast sank as a stone
Set round with ice; but still by field and stream,
In chasm and waste, I followed that faint gleam,
And found Him not; till, all my faith o’erthrown
Into a mire of doubt, I could but moan
My soul out in a misery extreme.
Then as I lay, a light hand touched my brow,
And sweet, soft lips besought me. For a space
I lay unmoved, too desolate to rise.
But soon I wept in thankfulness, for now,
The while I praised God for His crowning grace,
I saw him smiling in my daughter’s eyes.
MAORILAND.
PRANK MORTON.
The Road to St. Helena.
How far is St. Helena from a little child at
play ? — Kipling, “A St. Helena Lullaby.”
Drink and rave and bluster, but why should we
prevail?
The Road to St. Helena is a road that many sail!
The cliffs of St. Helena, oh, saddest cliffs they be,
Where the Ruined M&n at sunset stand a-gaziny out
to sea!
Oh, the boy with soul in torture thinking of the
Present Time!
Oh, the young man in his glory, and the strong man
in his prime!
Oh, the old man in his wisdom, where the naked
idols fall,
And the Road to St. Helena lieth plain for one and
all!
You may make a little money, you may build a
little fame,
Be it War, or Art or Letters, it is evermore the
same;
You may sail alone and bitter, you may take the
one last friend;
But the Road to St. Helena, you shall sail it in
the end.
When your life-long work is finished and the
“World” turns down the page,
And you sail for St. Helena by the Cape of Middle
Age,
When the Good-bye Coast is fading faster than all
other scenes,
And the sailing wind is fairest—oh, you’ll know
then what it means!
Ah! the Road to St. Helena is a lonely road and
sore,
Where the track runs up behind you and the track
runs down before;
And you see the end as plainly as high Heaven could
reveal,
Or the stolid, silent steersman smoking calmly by
the wheel.
Mother, father, toiling ever, working late and
struggling hard;
Worn-out granny in her corner, grand-dad pottering
round the yard—
But we have no time to listen, while the old folk
croak the tale
How the Road to St. Helena is a road that all must
sail.
Rushing down the road to Fifty on the track of
No Return—
Oh, the withered hands of women where the hot
tears fall and burn !
Oh, the old maid or old mother (all pretend and no
one cares) —
Say! how far is St. Helena from the dot who says
her prayers ?
From the highest to the lowest we must sail it for
the sake
Of the France that claims Napoleon, or the Devon
that claims Drake !
Bear the poor bones back, ye nations. Raise your
monuments and shout;
But the roads you sent your saviours you shall
never wipe them out.
We may drink and rave and bluster, but say can we
prevail,
While Roads to St. Helena are the roads that all
must sail?
As with men, so with the nations, all must stand and
gaze again
From the heights of St. Helena on the Past made very
plain.
N.S.W.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Rev. Wowser.
Arrayed in garb of weird design,
He walks the Path of Peace to-day;
Which, with unconscious humor, has
Been aptly styled “The Narrow Way.”
He shows how wealth may win for us
Admittance at St. Peter’s gate—•
Since man to reach his halo down
Must stand on a collection plate!
While-round him throng the hungry ones
(Pale wrecks on whom no fortune smiles)
He sends his missions to destroy
The happiness of heathen isles.
When, ’neath oppression’s cruel might,
The weaker ones on “life’s dark way”
Come seeking succor at his hands,
He kneels and murmurs: “Let us pray!”
He teaches Hate, though preaching Love;
He bids us ev’ry morn arise,
And walk the day in terror of
The God he cannot realise!
God’s Love is all unknown to him,
Governed by Creed his ev’ry mood.
He scorns the poor, he bows to rank,
And lives a lifelong platitude!
N.S.W.
RIVERINA.
The Victor Wind.
Last night, when all the land was locked in sleep,
A wind came rushing from the restless deep.
Scalping the waves, and wet with bitter brine,
It scoured the beach and grappled with a pine.
Hour after hour that weird and lonely fight
Raged on and on throughout the livelong night.
Sea-voices raised a farewell chant, a knell;
A tangled mass, the riven pine-tree fell.
Shouting loud triumph, wreck and death behind,
Throughout the land rushed on the victor-wind.
N.S.W.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty-
Six Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin

Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communica-
tions to be addressed to William Macleod, Manag-
ing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Sfjecial Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address ie specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, MAY 4, 1911.
THE REFERENDA-AND AFTER.
The Referenda campaign was like nothing
else that Australia had ever seen. George
Reid’s Anti-Socialistic humbug was not
taken seriously by Vested Interests; any-
how, not seriously enough to cause the mon-
eyed classes to disburse campaign funds
with a free hand. The Fusion policy was
so colorless that the majority even of Anti-
Laborites spoke of it with open scorn.
Prior to the introduction of the class issue
by Reid, Capital subsidised and worked for
the Freetrade or the Protectionist parties,
according as it chanced to be an importer
or a manufacturer. The Referenda strug-
gle on the other hand, found practically
every considerable employer of labor in the
Commonwealth solid in. opposition to the
imminent menace of raised wages a con-
crete Tiger, whose actuality none of them
Questioned. It was inevitable that there
should be a block “No” vote from those
whose wage-sheets stood to be swollen either
bv the operation of New Protection, or by
a Common Rule of the Federal Arbitration
Court It was inevitable, likewise, that
every shareholder in Melbourne Gas or
Tramway companies, and in Sydney Sugar
or Tobacco or Coal and Shipping combines
should get together to vote down the Gov-
ernment’s proposals in regard to monopolies
The shareholders in Australian I rusts and
the wealthy employers of labor are, it is
true, a negligible quantity numerically.
But they were reinforced, as they mostly
are, by a herd of LAZARUS-like persons, who
voted their ticket, partly owing to a natur-
ally servile habit of mind, partly from grati-
tude for the crumbs that come their way.
The back-blocks attorney, who once ad-
vised Brown-Smith, M.L.C., in his dog-
license trouble; the small dentist who gets
a tooth-repairing job, now and then, from
the Adipose family; the struggling doctors
and auctioneers who occasionally come into
professional contact with the affluent —all
these tend to vote the way their richest
patrons vote. It is a mule-headed proceed-
ing, seeing that the small professional man’s
best hope of prosperity lies not in aggrand-
ising the infrequent big man, but in better-
ing the lot of the frequent little one. Still, a
tragically common form of British snob-
bishness debars the typical brass-plate ex-
hibitor from taking that view, and Toryism
benefits accordingly.
* * * *
Anti-Labor, therefore, began the late
struggle with a sizeable ready-made vote, m
its pocket. And it spared no efforts, legiti-
mate or otherwise, to improve the advantage.
Old electioneering hands were astonished at
the reckless way the allegedly Right Think-
ing party spent its money. .Struggling jour-
nals, which had never previously discovered
the possession of “Liberal” sympathies, be-
came suddenly aghast at the prospect ot
Australia’s benign monopolies being inter-
fered with, and the New Protection shocked
them for the first time in their lives. House-
holders were swamped with leaflets and car-
toons, and alcoholic strangers clamored at
them to vote “No” if they wished to avert an
unspeakable catastrophe. Elderly citizens
of good repute—the Bruce Smiths and
Solemn Lees of the community—rushed
about, threatening that if the verdict went
against them they would drag the torch or
bloody revolution through the land. After
the first week the “Liberal” sheets dumped
Truth into her well, clamped the lid down,
and announced variously that a “Yes” vote
would mean the ruin of the N.S.W. working
man by the levelling-down of his wages to
the miserable Victorian standard, and like-
wise the ruin of the Victorian toiler by the
levelling-down of his wages to the deplorable
N.S.W. figure. Financial chaos and the re-
fusal of Cohen to advance another shilling
was predicted from the raising of State rail-
way men’s wages; also financial disaster to
railway employees by the wholesale lowering
of their wages. The poor widow—-the identical
old widow who was going to be empaupered
by Federation —was dragged into the contro-
versy. C. G. Wade limned her as a Sydney
Ferries shareholder, who would be driven
on the cold, hard streets if the Government
were empowered to acquire her property —
and this despite the fact that, at the worst,
the Government only proposed to do it “on
just terms.” Speaking with a thousand dif-
ferent voices, the folks who had everything
to lose by a “Yes” v<)te besought those who
had everything to gain by it to vote “No.”
The howl was so loud, concerted and auda-
cious that it was bound to carry weight.
* * * *
While Anti-Labor was unprecedentedly solid,
the Labor party was split for the first time
in its history. In some States the combines
found a priceless ally in certain State Labor
members. This astounding variety of demo-
crat put in his time fighting quietly, but
hard, for the low-wage cause. He saw his
own job imperilled, or, at any rate, dimin-
ished in importance by the Federal Govern-
ment’s proposals, and resolutely placed what
he conceived to be his interests before those
of the people. Since the average citizen
takes time to become disillusioned with men
he has trusted for years, the interested re-
presentations of State Labor members lured
many thousands of Labor voters into the
enemy’s camp.
* * * *
So the Government’s proposals were de-
feated by Vested Interests, their immediate
hangers-on, and the factor which in the U.S.
is termed Graft or Boodle, acting in con-
cert with State Labor. Yet the voting
strength of that weirdly unnatural alliance
was inconsiderable. Less than 45 per cent,
of electors on the roll voted —the smallest
poll Australia has known. Fifty-five per
cent, of the people were so little impressed
by the horrific warnings that had been
shouted in their ears that they refused to
avert Doom by making a couple of crosses
on two slips of paper. It is likely
that a large proportion of non-voters under-
stood nothing about the proposals. Cer-
tainly they were intricate enough to baffle a
normal layman. Thus, to appreciate the
relation of the suggested Trade and Com-
merce clause to the rest of the amendments
required an understanding of the fine points
of half a dozen involved High Court deci-
sions. The extent of the popular ignor-
ance on the subject, even in the closing
stages of the fray, might be gauged by a
study of the smaller Tory sheets. There
was* something epic in the utter futility,
not to say imbecility, of their final pro-
nouncements. If the public educators in
charge of these journals knew nothing ot
the question at issue, it seems a fair thing
to assume that the bulk of their readers
knew little more.
* * * *
The task that faces the Nationalist party
is, therefore, mainly educative. The thick-
and-thin supporters of the State Labor
member must be brought round to the view
that the interests of the wage-earners m
particular, and Australia in general, stand,
after all, before the personal ambitions of
any individual Labor politician. The citi-
zen who runs a shop or farm, or who doc-
tors or stops teeth, or expounds the Word,
has to be convinced that his interests are
not those of the price-raising monopolists
who bulldozed him into voting “No last
week Those who hesitated need to be
shown that the “Yes” side of the fence is
the Australian side, and the safe side as
The job may take time, but it is
unthinkable that it should fail. The present
intolerable and absurd position is that a
mass of legislation, which the country au-
thorised as far back as six years ago, and
which members of both Federal parties have
at different times approved, is rendered
nugatory. Without that legislation the
manufacturer may draw all the profit which
results from the tariff, and return none ot
it to his employees in the shape of decent
wages. In addition, he may penalise the
consumer as he pleases in the matter of
raised prices. The Trust curse, whose
steady development in timber, rabbits,
coal fruit, and so forth, is daily chronicled
in the press, is at liberty to expand un-
checked. W. A. Holman, of N.S.W.,
whose amazing attitude helped so materi-
ally to defeat the proposals, talks blandly
of “an agreement between the State Gov-
ernments* to voluntarily hand over certain
powers to the Commonwealth.” It is a
feeble bluff, put up with the evident inten-
tion of placating scandalised Nationalists.
Even assuming that the rabidly provincial
State Governments were willing to forfeit
the pow T ers required—and the question of,
sav, a Denham Government so much as
considering the matter, is too ridiculous for
discussion- —there are the Legislative Coun-
cils. Ninety per cent, of their members
regard the New Protection as “an infamy,”
and legislative control of monopolies as
“confiscatory Socialism.” As things stand,
Australia is hopelessly in the toils. It will
stay there just as long as the wage-earners,
which is to say four-fifths of the people,
continue by indifference, inaction or other-
wise to play into the political hands of the
small clique whose interests are directly op-
posed to theirs.
SOME STATE WRONGS.
Now that the Sydney daily papers are full
of wails about “harbor congestion” and “the
block on the wharves,” it is time to mention
a few State Wrongs which are lost sight of
in the wild turmoil about States Rights. A
Commission appointed by Wade, the cham-
pion full-back of the State Righters, sat in
190 S-9 and discovered, after a year’s labor,
that the conveniences of the port of Sydney
were inadequate to the increased demands of
the rapidly-growing trade, and that conse-
quently the whole State was handicapped,
lined and incommoded thereby. It also
found :
(1) That the trade of the port and the country
was rendered more costly in the handling because it
had to struggle against the railway bottle-necks and
dead-ends, congested wharf approaches, and inade-
quate wharf accommodation;
(2) Narrow streets, with hills up and down which
merchandise had to be dragged;
(3) Want of co-ordination between the two great
handling departments, the Harbor Trust and the
llailway Commissioners;
(4) The absence of rail connection between the
main wharves and the railways, while even the cen-
tral railway station wasn’t really central;
(5) The want of broadways around the wharves,
and the need for the removal of the Australian Gas
bight Co.’s plant, so that the wharfage plans of the
Harbor Trust in Darling Harbor might be carried
through ;
(6) The need of bridges and tunnels connecting
the north and south shores of the harbor in order
{inter alia) to bring Newcastle traffic into direct
link with Sydney, instead of compelling it to go
through the bottle-neck both ways.
At the time the Commission reported,
Full-back Wade was so extremely busy in-
structing the Commonwealth Parliament how
to govern Australia, and so preoccupied in
conserving State Rights by resisting the
claims of some State servants to a square
deal, that he was forced to put the report in-
to a pigeon-hole, and he allowed it to stay
there. The problem of Sydney’s wharf and
harbor congestion—which means trade conges-
tion for the whole of N.S.W. from Ulladulla
to Tibooburra —has been intensified by two
years’ rapid growth, and the report still re-
mains untouched.
* * * *
In the course of the Commission’s investi-
gations it found that the conditions of
N.S.W. trade had vitally changed during the
last few years. Its wool, which was for-
merly consigned from the station to London
and sold there, is now consigned to agents
in Sydney and sold there. London is no
longer the wool market for N.S.W.; Sydney
is. The buyer comes here; we do not send
our wool to him. Consequently, in the last
few years colossal warehouses have been
raised in Pyrmont and elsewhere, into which
the wool is carried on lorries up and down
various hills, stored, sold, and then carted out
again at vast expense to the ship’s side. The
Railway Commissioners had an “ideal state
of affairs” in contemplation to prevent all
this handling, for which, of course, in the
last resort, the producer has to pay. The
whole of the warehouses were to be in “one
great central station.” Asked why the
scheme was not carried out, the Railway
Commissioners replied that “vested interests
were so strong that the idea was aban-
doned.”
* * * *
Then, again, there is a double handling of
imported and exported goods, for which the
citizens of N.S.W. pay. Said this same Com-
mission : —-
Owing to the fact that Sydney is the distributing
centre for the whole of the State, about 90 per cent,
of the goods received from oversea vessels are carted
from the ship’s side to the Sydney warehouses, and
then distributed over the State. . .Similarly, a large
proportion of the products of the State intended for
export is delivered into the Sydney warehouses,
and sold to an exporter for shipment abroad.
This state of things is leading daily to greater con-
gestion of traffic in our narrow streets, and it is
apparent that, unless prompt measures are taken to
cope with the difficulty, it will, in the near future,
become almost intolerable.
The “near future” is here, and so are the
difficulties. The Harbor Trust still asks
for the land on which the Australian Gas
Light Co.’s plant stands, in order that a
broadway may be made to give access to the
splendid wharves it has built at Miller s
Point and Darling Harbor, and to connect
these with Circular Quay. But again vested
interests have prevailed, and the co. is still
there. The inward and outward merchan-
dise of N.S.W. still crawls up hills and
down dales from the train or the ship to the
warehouse or to the lighters, with double and
treble and quadruple handling. The little
fingers of the Sydney storemen, the Gas
Company, and the carriers are apparently
thicker than the thighs of the State Govern-
ment, and the back of vested interest is
stronger than the fist of the two great State
departments, the Railway Commissioners
and the Harbor Trust. And these are the
people who perpetually cry about State
Rights! Has the State no rights against
these vested interests —interests which oper-
ate detrimentally to the biggest spending de-
partments of the State, to the citizens ot
Sydney, and in concentric circles to the
people of N.S.W. generally? Has the lag-
ging State only rights as against the Com-
monwealth Government, which is struggling
to acquire power to do something for the
protection of the people of the States against
trusts and monopolies?
Finally, the city and the State are penal-
ised and State Wrongs created by the fact
that some of its great departments are work-
ing independently of, and in some cases an-
tagonistically to, each other. The Commis-
sion’s report showed the amazing fact that,
while the Harbor Trust had elaborated a
scheme of wharfage in Darling Harbor, on
the assumption that the Railway Commis-
sioners would bring the railway down so as
to take the goods straight off the ship, and
save all the up-hill weariness and expense
and unnecessary lighterage, the Railway
Commissioners had elaborated a counter-
scheme of bringing the railway along to
Glebe Island, in the full conviction that the
Harbor Trust would build wharves there!
Take a couple of extracts from the evidence
of Mr. Hakpeis, then traffic manager, now
Commissioner: —
Mr. Hickson (of the Harbor Trust) : Do you know
that the Harbor Trust Commissioners have a scheme
before them now for about 14 additional over-sea
berths between the gasworks and Dawes Point?
Mr. Hakper: No.
Mr. Hickson: The large over-sea traffic is gradu-
ally going away from Circular Quay; and Woolloo-
mooloo Bay is filled up. It (the over-sea traffic)
will be concentrated botweeen Dawes and Miller’s
Points. We are providing very fine accommodation,
with good stores and a good road behind them.
Under those circumstances, 1 ask you whether you
think it would be justifiable to recommend that a
railway should be brought down to the new wharves?
Mr. Harper : I do not think so.
Such want of co-ordination between two
great State Departments is surely a State
Wrong of a most grievous nature, when it
causes the frustration of costly plans.
While State Ministers are splitting straws
about the right of the Federal Government
to do things which the States as units can-
not do effectively, Vested Interests are bat-
tening on the necessities of the people of
the whole State. Great changes rendered
necessary by the country’s growth de-
velopment are neglected because the State
rulers are too busy fighting for theories and
losing the substance. Progressive cities in
Europe and America not nearly the size of
Sydney have spent their millions cheerfully
to meet their increasing trade, and spent
them most profitably. Cities like Melbourne
and Adelaide, Auckland and Wellington have
conquered such problems. But Sydney is
content to potter along, bringing its mer-
chandise into dead-ends and through bottle-
necks, and up steep hills and down into
deep valleys, carrying vast loads in trol-
leys through crooked, narrow streets, because
its rulers are too busy worrying about imag-
inary State Rights to redress real State
Wrongs.
THE AUSTRALIAN VANDAL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The country did not make him ; his vandal-
ism is the result of his mainly British origin;
of his descent from a people that had no
public estate in a country where all the land
and many of the opportunities had been
stolen centuries before, and that conse-
quently had no respect for the public owner-
ship suddenly thrust upon it in a new land.
* * * *
Out of the greedy exclusiveness of the
British freeholder grew the landless man’s
idea that anything held in common belonged
to nobody, and might therefore be destroyed
without any risk of punishment. Men who
would not steal a shilling from an indi-
vidual calmly defraud Customs or Land Tax
Commissioner, or any other branch of Gov-
ernment ; women who regard the pilfering
housemaid with horror will smuggle shame-
lessly, and then wipe their lips and say that
they have done no sin. That is general
enough in most countries; but aimless van-
dalism with regard to public property is
most often the attribute of the Anglo-Saxon,
or whatever other thing the mongrel British
nation has become by now. England (and Aus-
tralia has followed the fetich of the British
closely) maintains the old Wall around the
private garden, the old rails and spikes
around the allegedly public park, and the old
“keep off the grass” attitude; while France
for more than a century has had the open,
unfeneed country-side, the only barrier being
a row of poplars-—the poplar that makes the
man who sees it think always of Normandy. It
has communal orchards—apple and pear
trees lining the roads—owned by all and by
all respected. Public monuments and statues
are not linked round by chains and barriers;
for these people have some of the artist and
much of the real citizen in them, and respect
the beautiful and their own. Consider how
many centuries would the Arch of Titus in
the Roman Forum have lasted in London,
unguarded,. and without rails and spikes !
War and invasion have passed over it and
only scarred it; a mere century or so of the
ruffians who take great pains to climb the
pediments and chisel away the noses and
cheeks of the statues in the Fitzroy Gardens
of Melbourne would have brought it to the
ground. The open lawns in Florence keep
their roses till the season ends; in the Fitz-
roy Gardens they have to be ringed with
spikes and rails, and even then the vandals
pass the barriers and grub the plants to
carry them away, roots and all.
. * * *
Down at Calvi, in Corsica, when I called
at one of the birthplaces of Columbus, a
hen was sitting on 11 eggs in the room Avhere
the navigator was alleged to have been boro,
and the populace used the house as a latrine;
but it was never anything but an ugly little
house, and this degradation of a thing of
public interest is so rare as to be conspic-
uous. But in England there must always
be a barrier and a chain, and side by side
with it the commission of all the offences it
is intended to prevent. And England sent
out all that these things represent to Aus-
tralia —the ignorance, the want of culture,
the small brain that could discern no beauty
in anything that was not a habit.
* * * *
They found the country clean and sweet
and free of disease, and were not satisfied
till they had filled it with pests. The rab-
bit and the stinkwort, sectarianism and the
sweetbriar, the blackberry and tlm thorn,
the gorse and the whin that ate up rat land
by the thousands of acres, the thistle and
the horehound, the starling and the sparrow
and the stinking fox—all these they brougnt
into a clean country, and with them the
British habits and prejudices. And _ they
brought their vandalism into the politics ot
the new land. Wentworth, attempting to
grab Maoriland and make himself Earl of


Woolloomooloo, was a vandal; every man
who ever tried to supersede Australian sense
of greatness with some little Briticism or
other was a vandal, and a great portion of
Australians are vandals to-day. The rail-
ways are the people’s own; yet every day
some of the proprietors slash leather seats,
cut off upholstery buttons, and destroy
rubber window-buffers and metal fittings.
They climb statues and chisel off the faces,
expectorate on pavements, jostle rudely in
crowds. Only a generation or two removed
from the despotism of squire and parson,
their new-found liberty has become rude
license, and courtesy is felt by many of
them to be a surrender of independence.
That is the average city Australian —not
the man of the bush nor the picked Aus-
tralian. The picked Australian is a better,
finer man than the picked Britisher ; bigger-
brained, bigger-hearted, better educated and
more capable. And the main complaint
against the average city Australian is that
lie is too English, which is to say, too small
and thick-headed. Forty years of State
education have bettered him so much above
his earlier type that, vandal as he is, we
can only press for forty years’ more educa-
tion and discipline.
**' # *
The best discipline he can have is from
conscription. Even if invasion should never
come—-and it will be the quickest chance
for the Australian’s salvation if it does —
he would gain so much, not only physically,
but in being taught mental control, that his
term of service (which the clay-brained
commercial man regards as being lost time)
would be time put to its best use. Other-
wise let his vandalism increase—the van-
dalism of a strong-headed creature filled
with meat —and he may be almost as de-
structive to Australia as a foreign enemy
would be. He tears down millions of feet
of fine timber, and gives it to the fire in
order that his children’s lives may be made
wretched with the slavery of a struggling
dairy farm; and in that his vandalism
amounts to murder, since it destroys, for
little result, the youth of the children who
are to be the men who come after. The
drunken man lighting his pipe with five-
pound notes —that is the average Austra-
lian. See him in his pleasure, dirtying the
green and gold of his peerless country ; find-
ing Arcadie and vulgarising it to Hampstead
Heath. In a day he destroys the work of a
century, and is rather proud of it, just as
England used to be proud of the theatri-
cality of one W. E. Gladstone, who de-
lighted in cutting down trees.
I have in mind one of the cruellest, as it
is one of the most often repeated, acts of
vandalism I know of where no economic
reason can be urged. Along the shores of
Port Phillip Bay grows the classic ti-tree
(and you can quarrel over the spelling).
It grows slowly, and has centuries of life
in it. South wind and west wind and north
wind have beaten vitality into it in pain
and stress —great gnarled and rimose trunks,
that were battling before Captain Cook
saw Botany Bay, lean landward. Brighton,
which has a municipal council of sorts, has
torn it down, leaving the cliffs bare; and
now the vandalism of picnickers is tearing
it out at Hampton and Sandringham, and
the beauty that has taken a century to make
falls in a day.
* ■* * #
It was the third good season, and myriads
of little metallic blue and green beetles,
six-legged and bronze of belly and shard,
crowded the tree bloom, waiting for the
time to come to attack the onion gardens.
Every three successive good seasons give
them a chance to strip the ti-tree of its
bloom. They rose from the ti-tree in irides-
cent clouds, getting into flight directly from
the leaf, and their metallic shards shone in
the sunlight as they attacked the bloom and
the pine-apple shaped awns of the nodding
grasses.
* * * *
Upon the cliffs the bloom showed its petals
on the glassy yellow bloom of the capeweed;
the fluffy gold of the wattle overarched the
white pathway; and, all around, gold of
the bottle-brush honeysuckle, flute of magpie,
lilt of blackbird, chatter of little melodists
the size of your thumb. It was a tent of
green and white, with the fluted columns of
the ti-tree for the king poles; a cathedral
of leaf, with white blossoms showering at
the spring of the dome. The sea was haze
and softened blue of steel; the capeweed a
lawn of saffron stars fallen to the earth ; the
arc of the breakwater tense drawn like a
white bow upon the shield of ruffled steel
that is the sea. The long half circle of the
bay sweeps round to Brighton, ugly in her
nakedness ; a bee in every black-centred daisy
of the capeweed; a skylark singing high
above the nest; a lusher green in the quiet
gloom under the ti-tree —a gloom striped
and dappled with light; the ti-tree fluted as
those marble columns in the cloisters of
Milan; each capeweed a saffron star, edged
white around its little pollen bags for the
bee, and around its seed-cases for the earth
that next spring may blow again with
beauty. Little silken stars of forget-me-not
blue, to whom death must soon come with the
summer heats, although they bloom as if
they would live for ever. Quiet, and calm,
and green and gold, and blossom; a glade
of ti-tree beaten over and growing at right
angles to their roots, but still a-bloom and
glad with life because from stress they
have won strength. And on the gleaming
carpet of grass little children playing, their
poppy-tinted faces yellowed by the pollen of
the capeweeds.
And then to the Eden come rapine and
death: the Australian vandal in couples, or
the more horrific forms of picnics, staining
the beauty, tearing down in a minute the
trees that have taken a century to grow, and
that, apart from their beauty, deserve life
for the brave fight they have made against
the elements. Bottles and newspapers and
the remains of food litter the new carpet of
the spring; in that sandy garden the vandals
take great pains to break bottles against the
tree trunks, so that Paradise shall look like
the East End of London —where they rightly
belong. Shrieks in the clumsy love chase
which follows on the unspeakable female
throwing pieces of food at the unspeakable
male as an invitation to dalliance; noises
as of monkeys drunken in a clean wood;
the sacred grove insulted with noise and dirt
and mental besottedness, so that all the
Dryads flee away. Winter hides the fouling
agents; spring breaks clean on a new earth;
and the summer brings the vandals again
to the sanctuary —larrikin vandals, Salva-
tion Army vandals, revivalist vandals; all
makes of noise and dirt, and doers of unclean
things. Pitiful!
* * * *
Here is one of the many sanctuaries —
places made holy by tree and blossom and
bird and sunlight—and the vandals are in-
vading it and leaving it poorer every sum-
mer. It belongs not to these, nor to the
handful of apathetic people who live about
it always, and to whom its beauty is cheap
because it is not new ; it belongs to the na-
tion, destined to leave some mark on some
parts of the national character —good mark
if preserved, and bad mark if destroyed. The
artist and the poet who can express them-
selves shall bless or curse us as we deal by it;
the great army of men and women who are
blind artists and dumb poets shall be sorry
for its destruction, even if they know not
why they are sorry. And the smallest ar-
tist and the smallest poet are of more value
to the nation than the largest people whose
lives end in making much money out of pork
or drapery or fish-oil. The municipal
authorities are usually people of fish-oil
or pork or drapery ideas, and even if their
vested interests did not cause them to wel-
come the vandal, they are unfit to be trusted
with the work of guarding the shrine. That
work must be given to some central au-
thority-—-an authority that will be so proud
and jealous of its trust as to make it penalise
the vandal out of his Britishness, and either
make a good Australian of him, or suppress
him utterly.
RANDOLPH BEFORD.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The “Class Legislation” Cant.
Of all the cants ever canted in a canting
world, the cant of the “class legislation”
cry is the most canting. Ever since man be-
gan to make laws, class legislation has been
the rule. The highway of progress is strewn
with the bones of men who have protested
against it. The Wat Tylers and Jack Cades
paid with their lives for opposing it. Yet
those who gabble this parrot talk of “class
legislation” turn up their noses at the men-
tion of Jacic Cade, the man who was on
their side. Cade, according to their calen-
dar, was a mere fellow without a stake in
the country. That is to say he was “no
class.” For a thousand years, more or less,
the lawmakers made laws conserving their
“rights” ; the sole right of Jack and the “no-
class” people was to obey them, or be hanged.
Laws for the rich were game laws ; laws for
the poor consisted of Thou Shalt Nots, with
appendices. Now that the people are begin-
ning to rub the dust out of their eyes, the
troglodytes who believe that in the beginning
Herbert Spencer created the heavens and
the earth, profess to be alarmed, and shout
that class legislation is bad. They are right.
It is bad. Therefore bury it, burn it! It
has been a stumbling-block in the way of
Democracy, and its death and burial are due
and overdue. But the funds of Fat and Co.
may be depended upon to prolong its malig-
nant existence yet a while, and the people
who hold up hands in holiest horror at the
dreadful thing will struggle the most desper-
ately against the dreadful thing’s destruc-
tion.
Justice Brown, of the Court of Appeal.
Last session the Victorian Government
passed an amending Crimes Act, by which
the Governor-in-Council, which practically
means the Attorney-General, may release
any prisoner on recognisances to be of good
behavior. This might be' a sensible piece
of legislation—in the hands of a sensible
Attorney-General. But, as it happens, the
Victorian Attorney-General is Brown—
Drysdale Brown. Last year this Mr.
Brown, apparently on his own initiative,
ordered the prosecution of Robert Harper
and four other persons on a charge of con-
spiracy, which prosecution was speedily and
conclusively shown to have been quite un-
justifiable. On the comments of Supreme
Court Judges in that and the previous action
of Ronald v. Harper and other trials re-
sulting therefrom, the Crown prosecuted
seven persons, including various disreputable
degenerates, and juries found them guilty of
a series of utterly' malicious acts of per-
jury. One can find some excuse for the man
who lies to save himself or a friend, or even
out of lust of revenge or hate ; but for the
waster who deliberately swears away a
strange man’s character for the sake of a
misty promise of a billet and the price of a
couple of pints, no respectable country has
any use. From some points of view, he is
the worst internal enemy it can have. No-
thing matters quite so much as the mainten-
ance of the purity of the Courts and the ad-
ministration of justice; and this sort of man
pollutes the fountains, and poisons the wells.
When, therefore, Judge Chomley sentenced
these scoundrels to two years’ imprisonment
—the maximum being seven years—no one
accused him of severity. But the other day,
after nine months of the term had been
served, the new Appeal Judge ordered the
release of the seven deadly sins on their
giving recognisances to be of good behavior
for 15 years—and they were forthwith dis-
charged from custody! Possibly Mr. Brown
had reasons for his action, though, so far,
none have been given. In fact, the whole
episode has been shrouded in mystery, and
there is no reasonable expectation that the
shroud will be torn off. Under the circum-
stances it seems to be necessary to make a
still further amendment of the law. In
most States the railways cannot charge a
customer less than the fixed rate without
reporting the matter to Parliament, and set-
ting out the reasons for the variation. In
this much more important matter it seems
necessary—in view of the possibility of
more Browns— that the same principle
should be applied to reductions of penalties
fixed in open Court by Judges; and in every
case the report of the Judge who fixed the
penalty should be attached to the report of
the Minister. Why, however, there shouldn’t
be a real Court of Appeal instead of appeal
to a man who has probably had no judicial
training, but is a party politician, dependent
on votes, is even more niysterious than Mr.
Brown’s action in the particular case under
review.

The Limejuice that Isn’t.
The Verran Government is quite resolved
that South Australia, at any rate, shall put
into its Little Mary just what it intends,
instead of any old substitute that Private
Enterprise may find it cheaper to introduce.
P. E. is frankly amazed at the idea. Long
years of steady and unsteady adulteration
have given it an idea that this sort of swind-
ling is a “custom of the trade,” not to be
interfered with, and its opinion of the new
Food and Drugs Act varies from ridicule to
abuse. Opposition-leader Peake recently
made fun of Verran for having mentioned
that Mrs. Verran had found flour and pol-
lard in the household pepper. Verran added
that Commerce was “a rogue.” Thereupon
there was a howl of indignation; and the
Chamber of Commerce prescribed boiling oil
for the vile slanderer. The Premier is fre-
quently accused of talking too much. This
time he didn’t talk. He simply called on
the Central Board of Health, and promptly
the Chief Inspector provided a report of
local food-adulteration. The disclosures con-
tain things like this: Limejuice (so-called) ;
Nine samples analysed: eight “merely con-
coctions of citric acid and water” ; the other
tartaric acid and water. Mustard, one
sample, 75 per cent, flour. Pepper, three out
of six pure; the others from one-third to one-
half pollard. Liquorice powder (largely
used as a medicine for infants) : Two
samples, each half starch. Similar mean
frauds were detected in connection with
baking-powder and chicory. The latter, it-
self an adulterant for coffee, was loaded with
as much as 85 per cent, of roasted grain!
The report concluded grimly:—
These samples were all purchased from well-
known grocers and chemists, still doing business in
the city of Adelaide. Legal proceedings were com-
menced against every one of them, but the first
ease that came on for hearing was dismissed on a
legal technicality, and -it was useless to go any
further with the matter till more effective legisla-
tion could be passed. This legislation we now
have, as witness the milk prosecutions. Milk is
the only article dealt with so far.
The Bulletin has already given an account
of the new South Australian Food and Drugs
Act, and its way of using the axe on milk-
vendors —45 samples analysed, 21 con-
demned ; adulteration up to one-third water.
The “so far” suggests that the turn of the
others is about to come. It is, of course, for
the good of all honest traders that it should
come quickly. For instance, S. Aus. makes
olive-oil that is possibly the finest in the
world, and there is a demand within the
Commonwealth alone for more than it can
produce. Already, however, cotton-seed oil
is mysteriously finding its way into the olive-
juice ; and a very little of that kind of thing
will get the industry ja bad name that might
smash it for years, and ruin the honest pro-
ducer. Probably, in the coming pepper-and-
mustard prosecutions a few innocent re-
tailers will suffer; but they will have been,
at any rate, careless, and will soon learn to
put the blame where it belongs, and to deal
only with manufacturers who are beyond
suspicion. Nor is there the least reason to
believe that other communities are cleaner,
because they have not yet started sweeping
and kicked up the dust. Sydney and Mel-
bourne and Perth and Brisbane and Hobart
are all, beyond reasonable doubt, swallowing
drugs when they think they are drinking
limejuice, and starching their babies’ insides
with the mistaken idea of administering
liquorice. Adelaide has merely—as in so
many other useful reforms —got the axe to
work earliest. They can all follow. There
is no shortage of axes.

Gentleman and Buccaneer.
A terror of modern life is the “speculative
solicitor.” He is one of the most persistent
and shameless blackmailers in the com-
munity ; yet practically there are no means of
dealing with him. In the present state of
the Australian libel law, no newspaper, how-
ever cautiously run, is safe from him. He
notes, for example, that Jones, an unfinan-
cial bar-acquaintance, has been referred to
in the press in a way that may be twisted
into a misdescription. He draws the atten-
tion of Jones to the glad fact, and men-
tions gently that there is money in it. An ar-
rangement is thus arrived at, whereby Jones
shall proceed against the sheet for damages,
and the speculative attorney conduct the
proceedings on his behalf. If the suit fails,
no injury is done to the plaintiff’s side, since
the gent, one’s time is his own, when not
absorbed by cases of this sort; while Jones
has nothing to lose. If, as may happen, a
goodly purse of damages accrues, Jones
knows the joy of one who unexpectedly
strikes it rich; and the attorney takes his
costs and a lump sum for his “risk.” This
howling injustice is not confined to libel and
slander cases. A citizen cannot bump a
bicycle, or sulky, or trip over a carelessly-
disposed garden hose, or get sacked, or
pushed out of a theatre for being drunk and
disorderly, or fall out of a cab, or off a
wharf or ladder, without a covey of legal
vultures swarming round and sooling him
into a court of law. The prospective de-
fendant is, in nine cases out of ten, blame-
less in the matter, and knows it. But even
if he wins he must pay all his costs —since
the speculative attorney’s client is always
or nearly always, stone-broke—while if he
loses, the bill is liable to be monstrous. It
is not strange, in the circumstances, that
for every such blackmailing action that is
started, a score or more are settled out of
court. Every barrister knows of the exist-
ence of this scandal. Yet the furthest that
even the more reputable of them are pre-
pared to go, in the way of protest against it,
is to lay down a rule that, before having
anything to do with cases in which notori-
ously “speculative” gentlemen one are con-
cerned, they must be paid their fees. It is
painfully, inadequate, regarded as a check on
a harassing abuse of the Law’s processes.
The Victorian case of Grace Bayne v.
Blake and Riggall, where plaintiff's solici-
tor .was ordered to pay all the costs of the
action, indicates that even the Bench, which
is invariably the last to get official knowledge
of legal outrages, is beginning to wake up
and take notice, and resolve that the evil
must be suppressed. But the possibility of
occasional judicial action of the sort —it has
been recently adopted both in England and
in Maoriland —is a mild deterrent at best.
What is needed is a tribunal, specially em-
powered to take evidence regarding suspic-
ious conduct on the part of attorneys—to de-
cide whether the latter’s conduct of any given
matter was or was not rotten and dishonest,
and in the former event to punish the solici-
tor as a racing club tribunal punishes a
crooked owner or trainer. But, pending
that tribunal, the judicial practice lately
adopted in the Blake and Riggall and
other cases is worthy the earnest attention of
all Judges who desire to preserve the reputa-
tion of the “officers” of their courts.
Pursuing a Judge with Intent.
At the recent conference of M.L. trades and
labor councils, held at Christchurch, the bub-
bling resentment against Justice Sim, of the
Arbitration Court, boiled over. A clause
which the Court has attached to some
awards, holding a union to blame for a strike
of only two or three of its members, and can-
celling its general award, was the confer-
ence’s chief worry. By that clause a
double-barrelled penalty is provided—the
statutory penalty against strikers, as laid
down by the Act, and the withdrawal of the
benefits of the Court’s award. Delegates
representing councils from Auckland to
Dunedin, rose in turn and denounced Sim
and his judgments. He was told that his
job was to settle industrial disputes, and
not to legislate for the country; that, al-
though no Judge of the Arbitration Court
had given entire satisfaction, he was the
worst to date; and, finally, that there was
nothing else to be done but to wish him a
safe and speedy departure from his billet.
Instead, however, of resolving “that this con-
ference disagree with the action of Judge
Sim, and recommend that he be relieved,”
a motion to the effect was rejected in favor
of another to protest to the Government
against recent decisions of the Arbitration
Court. So the Ward Government, which is
most friendly, obliging and civil to every-
body who doesn’t talk to it with the serious
end of a piclthandle, will probably go cheer-
fully to work and tinker up the Arbitration
Act some more, to make similar decisions im-
possible. Which always looks inexpensive,
since the public foots the bill; and it can be
done in such a way that everybody is tem-
porarily placated or bewildered with a new
toy, while the Government has a bit of a
rest, or raises another loan. Also, it adds
variety to the tune to be played at election
time. And Labor has got to take it that
way whether lousepii sugarcoats it or not,
because Maoriland Labor is divided wide
enough for the hosts of Israel to walk
through.

A Labor Government came into power in
N. S. Wales in October; and, as a Federal
Labor Government is also in office, the whole
business of government in the Ma State has
been in the hands of the tigerish Socialists
for the past six months. The Labor people
denied that they were ravening monsters, of
course, but their opponents scoffed at the
denial, and said that “Labor domination”
meant Ruin. Even the man wiio didn’t join
in the lie about the wholesale bursting of
the wedding-ring had no hesitation in saying
that there was going to be a wholesale flight
of capital from the country. The one test
on this subject of flying capital is supplied
by the banking returns ; and a complete quar-
ter’s figures have just been made available.
For the March quarter of 1910 the average
amount of coin and bullion in the banks in
N. S. Wales was £12,119,000; for the past
three months it has increased to £15,739,000.
Even the 1 hly Telegraph, the chief dis-
tributor of the flying-capital lie, had to ad-
mit —“The banking averages for the March
quarter are record-breakers in the current
accounts, in the interest-bearing deposits and
in the cash.” So another Anti-Labor lie is
nailed. It appears to be nearer the truth
that, so far from the foreign capitalist mis-
trusting the Labor party, he has been im-
pressed with its practical proposals. In
that connection he remembers, anyhow, that
his heaviest losses occurred at a time when
the most virulent Anti-Laborites were in
power, 20 years or so back.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The most revolutionary of parties are not nearly
so dangerous when they feel the responsibilities of
office. —Tory paper.
When the fight is fought, shall the battle
stand,
A truce of words as your guerdon won?
Shall a nation waiting with empty hand
Call for the deeds that the strong men
shun ?
Once ye were ready to march and dare,
Shoulder to shoulder, a fearless few,
Proud the contempt of the fool to wear
As a badge of the work ye were called to
do.
Never a pause in the onward sweep;
Your drums that beat were the soldier’s
call.
Though the march be long, and the way be
steep,
Will ye halt ere the banners crown the
wall?
Not by the fights that ye fought of old,
But by the faith that ye keep to-day,
Shall a people’s sceptre be yours to hold,
And a nation’s heart be yours to sway.
For deep in the scorn and contempt of years
Are the men who once to a triumph won,
But, conquered at last by their own small
fears,
Betrayed their trust with a task half done.
N.S.W.

Heartless memo, received from the Census
office: —
Oil comparing: tlie ages of a number of persons, mostly
ladies, with the Birth registers, it has been found that
the ages have been wrongly given in the Census cards.
All persons who have been discovered so far, have been
written to and told that they are liable to a penalty of
£5O. It has clearly become necessary to ascertain how
far this practice has been common. The average amount
of misstatement so far is 8 years. All persons who have
misstated their age are advised to forward their correct
age to the Commonwealth Statistician, Melbourne, with,
out delay, in order to avoid the penalties prescribed by
the Act. .

During the Referenda struggle the Age and
the Argus held up daily, for public admira-
tion, a Prahran workman named J. T.
Packer. Packer was a member of the
Prahran branch of the Political Labor
Council; yet he stood on the same platform
as Judkins, and endorsed many of Jud-
kins’s remarks, and made a speech that won
for him the cordial apnrobation of the
Argus. Naturally the Labor Council asked
him if he didn’t think it was time to send in
his resignation. Then the two papers
raised an uproar in the name of “freedom of
speech,” and the puzzled public was given to
understand that it must stand by “Packer
and liberty.” And nobody asked what, would
have happened supposing Packer had been a
leader-writer on the Argus, and had in-
sisted on his “right” to tell the puoiic that
Deakin was a political fraud, and the whole
Referendum campaign a heavily-loaded at-
tempt to bulldoze the workers. Probably
nobody asked, because everybody knew that
the Argus bosses would, so to speak, lead
that writer by the ear to the front door,
and tell him that his place was in some other
kind of office, and on some other sort of rag.
Or if some canvasser for one of the Argus’s
Fat friends of “the Lane” boosted the fluff-
ware of Brown while he was supposed to be
representing Jones, nobody asked whether
the Argus would not say that the sooner
the disgraceful drummer did the honest
thing, and transferred himself to Brown
the better. And if the drummer talked
about “liberty,” everybody knew that the
Argus would reply with the Argus equiva-
lent for “Rats !”
♦
The Wonthaggi (Vic.) coalminers gave
unionism a lift the other night. One of the
little worries of pretty well every coalfield is
the army of wheelers —youngsters who are
liable to knock off work at any moment, for
almost any cause, and throw the whole con-
cern idle. At the State mine miners and
wheelers are in the same union; but when
one of the wheelers was sacked the other
day, the crowd of wheelers went on strike
without even mentioning the matter to the
organisation or its executive. That night
there was a mass meeting, and, after they
had listened to the remarks of various union
officers for about 15 minutes, the youngsters
began to wonder what was biting them.
They had got a half-baked idea that they
were budding heroes and champions of union-
ism : and when the old hands told them that
they were an undisciplined crowd, and only
about two removes from blacklegs', they be-
gan to think things. The blackleg professes
to ignore the union; these enemies of union-
ism had disregarded it—and there isn’t much
difference between the two. It was a con-
siderably chastened crowd which crept back
to work a few hours later.

The “Black German” s.s. Hanau, bound
from Hamburg to Australian ports, has
arrived at Cape Town with a gaping hole in
her hull. The steamer is said to have struck
a submerged object off the South African
coast. Eight weeks ago the big British
tramp Palatina was hurriedly beached near
East London. She, too, had struck some-
thing where “no bottom” is charted. The
two accidents suggest one of two things—
either the steamers struck the sunken Wara-
tah, or portions of her, or what they struck
the Waratah had struck first. On the facts
given, both theories no doubt have holes in
them; but, all the same, most people will think
that Avhen the accidents are accounted for,
the Waratah will figure somewhere in the ex-
planation. They certainly ought to be ac-
counted for; and, considering the volume of
Australian traffic round the Cape of Good
Hope, it is up to the British Admiralty to
give some of the warships a job in getting to
the bottom of the mystery.
The Age and the Argus were one in word,
deed and thought against the Referenda;
but the harmoniousness of their leaders of
the 27th was almost miraculous. The
Argus anticipated “a gross and insulting
explanation that the decisive defeat of the
Caucus proposals is due to the bribery of the
electors by monopolies and rings.” The Age
prophesied: “The defeated side will com-
plain that it had to fight imaginary trusts
and monopolies.. .It will be said that the
arts of corruption were exercised as they
were never used before.” Both papers went
on to protest heatedly that these presumptive
explanations would be preposterous, insult-
•ng and unworthy. “Where has the money
gone? Who has received it?” demanded the
Argus angrily. Surely these two elderly ladies
do protest too much ! It would almost appear
that they were acting sympathetically on the
principle of “Don’t prophesy unless you
know,” and were inspired with a fear that
the other fellow did know'.

“Jack and the Beanstalk” is still at Her
Majesty’s, Sydney. Audiences have caught
on to Jack Cannot’s chorus song, and howl
it on the scantiest encouragement; the
Pantzers are well, likewise the Giant’s legs,
and generally things are booming.
** * *
At last J.C.W. makes a small change at
the Sydney Royal—he takes off his Hollar
Queen and puts on the Dollar Princess in-
stead. The Queen, whose other name is
Miss G —bbs (The Bulletin has sworn not
to mention her again at less than £237 per
letter), will go on Wednesday, May 10, and
the Princess is due on the lucky l3th. Flor-
ence Young, Olive Godwin, Roberts and the
others will have their old parts.
#* * *
At Sydney Criterion, the infamous career
of “The Woman in the Case” is drawing to
a close. Those who have not enjoyed the
luxury of scandalised amazement which is
induced by this female’s machinations, are
hereby warned that they have but 10 days
to repair the omission.
** * *
During the past three weeks “The Bad
Girl of the Family” has been doing her
worst before about 50,000 people (on the
manager’s affidavit) at the Adelphi, Sydney.
George Marlow says he can’t reform her
while such regrettable encouragement is
given her by the public; but as soon as the
public sees the error of its conduct, the
young woman will be discharged. Probably
she will be too old to work by then.
« Dt « *
R. A. Roberts, the quick-change artist,
will make his final appearances as six people
and a pirate at Sydney Tivoli this (Thurs-
day) evening. Variety is the spice of life,
and the quick-changer is the man who is best
able to supply it. Violent revolutionaries
like Bruce Smith well know how many lives
and homes are wrecked by plain, dull mon-
otony. The wife who has watched the same
old husband pottering about for 10 or 15
years, and the plain brown husband who has
observed the same neutral-tinted wife out
of the corner of his eye for a decade or two,
frequently yearn in secret that the other
partner could be a bit different occasionally.
If married couples would take lessons from
talented actors, and learn thoroughly the art
of making complete and artistic changes, the
“till-death-do-us-part” clause in the marriage
contract would have less terrors for a lot
of people who doubt their staying capacity.
Peggy Pryde, Delino, Tom Dawson, Black
and White, and Vaude and Verne will be the
performers mainly responsible for the bill
after Thursday; but reinforcements are ex-
pected.
“ THE WOMAN IN THE CASE ,” AT
SYDNEY CRITERION.
The doubting lawyer (Hugh Buckler) and the

Prince Alfred Park (Sydney) will have a
vacancy for a circus after Saturday night,
when Wirths’ big show will end its season,
and the blood-perspiring hippo., the Flying
Jordans, the shapely wire-walkers, the horses
and other appurtenances will get into line
behind the band waggon and hit the trail.
Business has been so good during the long-
season that a permanent circus looks a pay-
able speculation, but the Wirths say they
owe a duty to the country, and they are go-
ing to pay it and collect the change.
** * *
West’s Pictures will shortly be putting on
the magnificent production of “Henry VIII.,”
which was played for the English branch of
the world-wide picture firm by Sir Herbert
Tree and his famous company. The film is
now in Sydney, but the date of the first
night is not yet fixed. At present West’s are
booming in Sydney at the Glaciarium and the
Palace Theatre, and on Saturday the Bijou,
remodelled, beautified and enlarged, will be
opened as the Princess. The star item of
the present programme is the Grand Na-
tional of 1911, when at Aintree, England,
out of a record field, only one horse did not
fall. And no doubt the cine-man who
turned the handle reflected, as he “took”
that thrilling race, that it’s a slippery course
that brings good luck to no one.
*# * *
At Sydney Lyceum “The Fatal Wedding”
is still being celebrated, and the Spencerian
staff has risen to the occasion. The door-
way is powdex*ed with confetti, and, up
above, some patent machinery reproduces the
ringing of the wedding-bells. Inside, the
tin-can band makes its fearful music while
the figures flit life-like across the screen, and
it is hard to believe that the shadows on the
canvas do not make the commotion.

Miss Lily Mackenzie Seton’s farewell con-
cert, under Mrs. Quesnel’s direction, comes
off on the 9th May in the Y.M.C.A. Hall.
Miss Seton will later on pack up her soprano
voice for Europe, and depart in peace by
Orient mail boat on May 27. Mrs. Alex.
Gordon is vigorously assaulting the sale-of-
tickets problem for Miss Seton’s good-bye.
Alfred Hill has written an orchestral ac-
companiment (for this concert) to his
“Maori Love Song,” and the members of an
invisible chorus will blend their voices in the
refrain.
