The awkwardly-titled emotional drama,
“The Dawn of a To-morrow,” produced at
Melbourne Princess’s on Saturnight, is a
sermon in action —a sermon in which,
as usual, sensibility is substituted for
common-sense. This was to be expect-
ed from Mrs. Frances Hodgson Bur-
nett, authoress of that famous ex-
cursion in domestic bathos, “Little Lord
Fauntleroy.” It would appear that Mrs.
Burnett is an exponent of the art of mental
healing. She philosophises in four acts on
the power of faith to overcome all the ills
to which man is heir-presumptive, and to
make woman’s smile eternal, even in the
absence of a gold tooth and a new tube
skirt. The preacher-in-chief of her gospel
of hard wishing and patient waiting is a
small, vulgar, gutter-bred optimist, with a
violently red head, a mixed costume of odds
and ends gleaned from the raw material of a
shoddy mill, and a habitation and a name at
Apple Blossom Court, in a particularly dis-
agreeable and unmannerly London slum.
* * * *
Glad has picked up her rudimentary know-
ledge of the scientific principles of sainted
Mrs. Eddy in hospital, while suffering from
broken legs, and sets all the female popula-
tion of Apple Blossom Court praying fer-
vently for the things its soul craves for,
“ostridge fevvers” and beer chiefly. To
Glad in a fog comes Sir Oliver Holt, a man
with insupportable masses of money and a
naughty nephew. Sir Oliver is disguised as
an honest man in old clothes and a crying
need of a bath. His pleasant intention is to
dodge irrevocable madness by blowing out
his brains in a noisome quarter, and leaving
his shattered remains on the parish to be dis-
posed of as unclaimed goods. His exact
object in going to all this trouble is not
convincingly set forth. However, Glad
takes hold of Sir Oliver Holt, and begins to
get in her good work. Incidentally she is
praying herself out of a peck of trouble.
Her best boy, one Dandy, is earnestly sought
after by Scotland Yard, because of the sud-
den and painless death of an old gentleman
who had thrust his head in the way of a
burglar’s implements of trade. Out of the
manner in which the faith of the young
woman removes mountains and shifts ills,
Mrs. Frances Hodgson Burnett makes her
play. Glad strikes impressive attitudes,
wishes fervently, and waits with a frigid ex-
pression ; then slowly the gates of. circum-
stance unfold, and the desired thing comes to
pass. To provide temporarily effective situa-
tions in this way is easy; but you’ve got to
pull the long leg of coincidence till the limb
is painfully out of joint. Mrs. B. pulls with a
traction engine. Glad prays her Dandy out
of danger, and into a line of holy living; she
starts the bedevilled millionaire in a new
career of expensive and promiscuous philan-
thropy ; she saves the immortal soul of all
Apple Blossom Court.
* * * *
Miss Katherine Grey’s performance as
Glad is a series of effective bits. At odd
moments she is fine, but the sweep is not
continuous, and Glad would have to be acted
in a sort of unrelaxing artistic frenzy to carry
her through triumphantly in defiance of the
captious worldliness of a sceptical genera-
tion. Winton Hall’s Sir Oliver Holt is gently
done and well, but the part is littered with
discrepancies, and has no distinction. Wil-
liam Desmond’s Dandy is tuned to the right
pitch, and has some value and force as a
human document Cyril Mackay presents a
conventional dissipated nephew in an ac-
ceptable way, naught extenuating, and set-
ting down nothing in malice. Quite a num-
ber of capable actors and actresses are figur-
ing in incidental characters. Miss Susie
Vaughan casts a faint glow of humor in the
gloom of Apple Blossom Court as a slum wife
with a perennial black eye. Atholwood is a
distinguished physician; but a distinguished
physician in a drama cannot have more
brains than the dramatist, and it’s Athol-
wood’s misfortune to have to impersonate a
celebrated scientist who talks like Marie
Corelli —or Mrs. Hodgson Burnett. Miss
Alma Vaughan, Miss Flo Gleeson, Leslie
Victor and Boyd Irwin are good in little.
There were various indications on Saturday
night that the drama had run in hurriedly
out of the wet, but no doubt it is very much
the better for wear these later evenings.

For a few more nights at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) “The Merry Widow” will supply
its three fat courses of haunting music, with
erotic entrees and saccharine tablets. The
present edition of “The Widow,” like its
predecessors, is proving financially success-
ful. The company is also generally satis-
factory, though none of the males are suffi-
ciently delightful in their amorous moments.
They want nourishing on a diet of soft-soap
and glucose.
* * * -*
At the Melbourne Royal Raymond de St.
Gautier is still pursuing “The Belle of Brit-
tany,” what time pa discourses on gentle
sausages. The old noble is instructive and
verbose on those little mysteries, but he is
cruel in suggesting that they serve their full
purpose as common edibles. As a matter of
fact, sausages are only in their infancy as
regards their uses. Mayors, for instance,
will employ them in the form of official
necklets, so soon as they realise that don-
ning frank forts is a way of putting on dog.

At the Melbourne King’s to-morrow even-
ing The Arion Society should finish its brief
appearance in “La Mascotte,” which was
announced to commence last Tuesday night.
Then W. Anderson will resurrect “The
Squatter’s Daughter” on Saturday, instead
of the threatened “Sorrows of Satan,”
it being understood that Satan is slightly
backward in his training. It is suspected
that he has been spending time in Referen-
duming instead of in Rehearsing.
-& * * *
In mentioning the Opera House bill this
week one requires no load of buttered super-
latives. The programme is good to middling,
with some patches of cleverness supplied by
the Zanfrellas, Australian Dartos and Yetta,
the wire performer. Miss Cromer Challis,
mentioned last week, is at present warbling
a ditty regarding George V. The Bulle-
tin was specially requested to hear this
song, and, having heard it, says fervently,
“God Save the King!”
** * *
The gloom of Melbourne Athenaeum has
been dispersed by the arrival of the Royal
Hawaiians. Ernest Kaai is the leader of
the group, and his mandolin and ukulele
solos are precious little items of verve and
vivacity. R. Kaawa is heard in “Moki-
liana,” which seems to contain an incanta-
tion to a dusky Cupid. J. Kamakani uses
his bass to good effect, and the sweet voice
of Miss Iveala makes clever excursions up
the higher reaches of the stave. Also, there
is Miss Etela’s hula dance, with about one
motion and several Oriental meanings. The
item is strange, but the costume of the lady
is stranger. It appears to consist of scarlet,
with an overskirt of straw, plus a pair of
nobby doormats to keep the draught off her
ankles.

beloved, circles about a sinful crew who try

She, however, resists all the persuasions,
and is finally rescued from the gang by Vir-
tue in foreign whiskerings. The sermon
takes about an hour to deliver, but it doesn’t
seem half that long, for time goes quickly
when one is being reformed by the view of

Motor Street Cleaners.
The Renault Co. of Paris appear to be always
leading the world in automobile matters, and
their latest achievement is the supplying to the
city of Paris a number of motor-driven street
sweepers. The experiment is a distinct success,
and our city council would do well to enquire into
such an invention.*
an abduction, some fights, and a thrilling
escape upon the housetops.
** * *
“Jean and the Waif,” turned on at Mel-
bourne Olympia last Saturday night, tells
of a ragged youngster who goes for
a mother in a wonderful imitation of a Yan-
kee blizzard. Soon she happens on a dwell-
ing where the only suggestion of a family is
a petted tike snoozing in a cot in canine
pyjamas. The infant snuggles up to the
hairy sleeper, and is in the middle of a large
and friendly hug, when she is snatched up
and adopted by the animal’s owners. Alto-
gether the picture has many points that will
appeal to the multitude, though this paper
cordially disapproves of the insanitary fond-
ling of the cherished hound.
*****
At the Melbourne Masonic last Friday
evening Mis Maud Grantham accompanied
Alice through the Wonderland of Lewis Car-
roll. The trip occupied something over two
hours, and Miss Grantham’s humorous de-
scriptions on the journey were received with
a polite chorus of titters in the reserved sec-
tions of the hall. As on other occasions, the
lady showed herself an engaging elocutionist,
with a charming aptitude for wearing nice
clothes. Incidentally she obtained full vo-
cal am from a good nuartette, which sang
Lehmann’s cycle of “Nonsense Songs.”
** * *
The two Lillies, making their first Austra-
lian appearance at Adelaide Tivoli, prove
to be the usual step-dancing sisters, plus
a bright idea. That the machine-like exact-
ness of step may be the more manifest, they
do it one in front of the other. The first
Lillie floats gracefully about an empty stage,
then draws aside a curtain, and is, appar-
ently, reflected in a mirror. When she
faces the audience, and the reflection conse-
quently has its back to them and her, the
absolute identity of movement is a delicate
matter. Cooper and Lait have moved on,
but a request is hereby sent after the male
partner to drop saying, or half saying, a
word that is familiar in street rows, but out
of place on the stage. True, he is skilful
enough to gain laughter by this trick ; but
it really isn’t worth while. The William-
son juveniles have finished their “Florodora”
season at the Royal. The rank-and-file

“Flinderino” describes a Northern show: —
Cairns has lately been having- its old-established
annual water frolics on a large scale. The programme
opened with a cyclone, in which iron-roofing, out-
houses and building material gave a display of aero-
planics. A heavy rain-turn followed, and for several
days the ordinary-sized rain-gauge had to be emptied
every few hours to keep up with the performance.
Uncertain as to whether another cyclone might be on
the programme as an extra, the audience secured
the lids of its houses by passing ropes and wires over
the roofs and weighting each end with big logs, old
stoves, or anything heavy. In a rapid transforma-
tion-scene the street gutters appeared as rivers and
hid the earth, and the Pacific Ocean at every high
tide walked up the lower streets and hung its skirts
on the top rail of the fences. Diving contests, off
verandah and balcony railings, were indulged in;
also swimming races to the summer-house and back.
A party of “sports” boarded a “flatty,” and rowed
through the town. In the main street they entered
the side door of an hotel, tied their craft up to the
bar, drank, and then navigated the boat out through
the front door amid loud and prolonged cheers.
AT POVERTY POINT.
Ernest Wunderlich, good friend of Sydney artists,
is leaving for Europe, where he contemplates a long
stay; so the professional musicians “smoked” him
at their club-rooms on Sunday night. A feature of
the concert consisted of four of the compositions of
the guest. The first was a song, “By the Willows”
(words by Fred J. Broomfield), most sympathetically
sung by brother Alfred Wunderlich. The three other
pieces were set to English words from the German
by Rudolph Baumbach. “The Stars in the Sky
Above Me” was a stirring work, also admirably ren-
dered by. brother Alfred; while “’Tis May That’s
Approaching” and “Good Night” were well sung by
Emil Sussmilch.
A yarn of Yankee leg-pulling. As the last echo
of a Sousa overture died away the U.S. General
Schofield once stepped up and greeted the con-
ductor. “Beautiful, beautiful!” said the General
warmly. “But I’m astonished that you get such
good results with so little discipline.” Sousa, who
worships Discipline in the same temple as he does
Music, was staggered, and said so. “You say you’ve
never noticed it?” said the General affably. “Of
course not! But it’s true all the same. The mo-
ment you turn your back on one side of your band to
shake your stick at the other, the blighters on vour
blind side all quit playing. Of course you don’t see
it!”

The cast is small—six adults and three children—
but the play grips all the same. The play has been
nearly 40 years in reaching Australia. The explana-
tion is that Irving, pere, always kept his fist on it
in case he should_ make an Australian tour. He had
so few novelties in his repertoire that he could not
afford to sub-let any he had. On his first visit to
America it was “Charles I.” that assured the success
of his New York season. But it was left to son H.
B. to introduce the play to Australia.
“Blue Mask”: Mrs. “Trilby” Irving is far from the
days when she could rattle through a hocky or tennis
match, or ride a-wheel with the best. About the only
outdoor recreation she indulges in to-day is motor-
ing; and even then the speed has to be slow, and the
distance not more than 30 miles, or her strength is
overtaxed.
Drama, with an Australian setting, and quite in-
nocent of either bushrangers or squatters, gets its
chance at Princess’s Theatre, Melbourne, next July,
when a string of Randolph Bedford’s plays get their
chance. Long ago, in old Pluck-up, outside of
Broken Hill, and ante-dating Broken Hill as a silver
discovery, there was one woman on the field, and all
the men were in love with her, more or less, and in
different ways. The First Lady had a tragic ending.
Bedford has taken the romantic yarn and turned it
about, and trimmed it with the character-studies of a
new rush, never before used by an Australian, "and
probably never before used with the right knowledge.
“Pluck-up” begins the season, and either “New
Chum Gold” or “The Love Child” (more drama by
Randolph) follow it.
“Sinnicuss”: The Williamson management has de-
cided to make a donation to the Sydney Hospital.
That Js to say, for the last night of “Our Miss
Gibbs” —a function to which every worshipper of
coryphees and “Society” aspirant in the- town is
bound to crowd—the ordinary charge for Stalls and
circle seats will be raised to 10s., and half the pro-
ceeds will be handed to the distressed institution.
It is one of the most delightfully Caledonian bits
of charity that has come within my purview.
“Hamer”: With a desperate desire to keep its
functions select, a dancing club advertises: “Ad-
mission—Gents., Is.; ladies on approval.” The in-
dividual who has to give the approval is up against
a pretty bad time, if he approves too generally he
will have to meet the inspired objections of Bill
Jones, whose girl works at a hat-factory, and who
objects to rubbing shoulders with Jack Johnson’s
girl, who toils in a pickle-factory. And if he with-
holds his approval he will meet—well, he will pro-
bably meet it at the first corner. The job of ap-
prover should be a nice lively occupation, and it
ought to be worth £5O a week.
Consul 11., the famous “educated” ape, recently
broke away from his cage at the Lafayette Theatre,
New York. Once free, the animal dropped his semi-
human gentility, and, with a growl of anger, lum-
bered on to the stage and seized the most tasty-
looking soubrette available, ’[’lie lady naturally ob-
jected ; the audience also objected ; and, as the lady
is a good draw, so did the management. The oppo-
sition, however, did not deter the simian, which
began by biting the lady’s face. At latest accounts,
both were feeling poorly.
“Stony”: A “college of music” advertises—“No
sharps, flats, scales or exercises to learn; tunes first
lesson. Fee, one guinea.” No flats to learn? But
how many to teach?
“Tempo”: Adelaide’s Mrs. F. H. Pollock, who until
recently was the only woman-lessee of a theatre in
Australia, said “I will” once again a few days ago,
to the consternation of the Holy City. The bride is
not so young as she was, and the bridegroom reached
the age of indiscretion about the middle of last cen-
tury. Still it is the happy ending of an old love.
“Blue Mask”: Twenty-seven years ago William-
son, Garner and Musgrove were running a “Jack
and the Beanstalk” pantomime at the Sydney Royal,
with Nellie Stewart as “Jack.” I merely mention
this to contrast The Bulletin’s criticism of the
1884 show with that of the Beanstalk now sprout-
mg at Her Majesty’s. Bulletin (13/4/’ll) says:
Year by year pantomime gets nearer and nearer
to musical comedy, inasmuch as they approximate
more closely to vaudeville. The show
k? a ?" s ,, a U, recor ds in the matter of sparseness of
plot. The Bulletin critic of the 1884 panto, re-
portea that it was doing solid business “despite
the feeblest libretto an audience was ever cursed
with. Pantomime is dead. The show at the Royal
is a burlesque—a well-made pastry pudding (bar the
aforesaid libretto) of jingle, breakdown, whimsy-
fantastical, limelight, and the nearest approach to
the naked truth in the' human form divine we ever
blushed at. Anybody remember when there was a
real pantomime in Sydney? By the way, what is
real pantomime ?
Snell”: Every time I see an imported dancer

drawing. value of a striking dance, to mark what
little skill is devoted to the devising of the goods.
Australian girls are astonishingly clever on their toes.

“Borob”: During: the . recent rains in Northern
Bananaland, St. Leon s circus was held up for seven
weeks cm the banks of the Burdekin—unable to
cross. This stream drains a big patch of country,
and, unlike other trickles, which go down quickly it
recedes at the rate of about an inch a day. When
the troupe eventually managed to cross, the bands-
men promptly decamped. There’s more skittles than
beer about a circus fife in North Queensland.
“St. S.”:G. P. Huntley’s peculiar humor is all his
own, and it doesn’t matter whether his stage is
theatre or music-hall—he takes possession of it all
the same. He has just drifted into London Pavilion
where he plays in a duologue, “Buying a Gun ” The
f£ ene 3 , 5 , a S'™ sh °P> and he chatters on about the fee
the caddy will ask for carrying the weapon: and the
proper place to hit a bird; and the necessity for
having guns to match his socks, and so on, all in
the inconsequent but immensely popular way which
Australia remembers in “Three Little Maids.”
Judging by the evidence at the coal vend
trial, one witness was so indignant at the news he
had received of a “job” in which certain

Dutton Green, who died in Adelaide the
other day, was a member of the Assembly for two
yea,rs in the days when to be reputed rich and to
be in “the swim” was enough to secure a man
election if he chose to seek a Parliamentary seat.
Except to look pretty—he was an Adonis—he did
nothing in Parliament.
The driver of the team shown in George
W. Lambert’s “Across the Black Soil Plains”
was drowned in the Barwon a little while ago
while swimming horses across to get some feed.
His name was Jack McGowen. He was drowned
a fortnight before the date fixed for his marriage.
Died of typhoid, W. N. Dove, N.S.W.
stipendiary magistrate. In 1909 the Broken Hill
miners were on strike, and the Government took
several precautions to preserve the peace. Among
them it sent up the appropriately named Dove as
a temporary S.M. When the troubles subsided
his appointment was made a permanent one.
Lately he contracted typhoid, and a fatal heart
trouble supervened.
The members of the State Legislature in
S. Aus. are sad because the referendum concern-
ing the £lOO a year increase in their salaries was
taken on the same day as the Federal plebiscite.
All the Federal “Noes” went down on the State
papers, while many of the “Yes ” brigade tossed
the State ballot slips away without marking them.
The result was a two-to-one majority against the
desired rise in screw. The present pay is £2OO a
year.
A layman, writing on the eternal Sydney
Anglican ecclesiastical-vestments question, re-
marks, apropos the quoted dictum of Archbishop
Wright that the use of vestments is “contrary to
the Constitution of the Church as we have it ” :
His Grace has recently been pleased to appear in St.
Andrew’s Cathedral, for the first time in its history, with
a scarlet train and train-bearers, for all the world like a
Cardinal without his hat. So that we are all proud to
think that we are almost as grand as they are at St.
Mary’s.
On the night when the Referendum poll
was declared, the Geelong (Vic.) correspondent of
the Argus, full of unholy joy, relaxed his hold on
the weazand of Truth for a few moments. In
consequence, the following words were printed in
the next morning’s paper :
All bodies affiliated to the Council of the Leagues have
worked admirably for the past two months, especially the
Women’s National League. The result was manifest
to-day in the capital system for bringing voters to the
booths. Dozens of motor-cars and other vehicles, bearing
streamers of blue and white, the colors of the party, flitted
everywhere, and few voters were overlooked.
Anti-Socialism naked and unashamed !
A German surgeon last week described to
a gathering of spectacled brother man-repairers
how he had constructed a new gullet out of an in-
testine and some outer skin, for a suicidal frau
who had burnt out her original food-and-drink
canal with a draught of sulphuric acid ; some-
where else a man had had his hip repaired with a
flake taken from a corpse ; and in America some
hospital doctors are just finishing the job of put-
ting a new face on an unfortunate railway worker,
whose countenance was ruined by falling into a
set of revolving cogs. It makes the reader rise
and cheer, till he remembers that'gout has not
been abolished, and that rheumatism, neuralgia,
catarrh, cold-in-the-head and a few hundred
other things still worry himself and the neighbors,
despite the brass plates and the bills. Medical
science seems to be able to patch and sew and do
freak-jobs well enough; but a simple ache or
sniffle makes it look like a fool.
More capital, it seems, has been driven
out of the country ; and John Murray is the guilty
party this time. Though compulsory voting is
not law in Victoria, people who refuse to give
their names and other necessary particulars to the
police who do the electoral canvass are liable to
prosecution. Now, a community of 30 of the
Chosen People had established themselves at
Kyneton ; and when the roll-collector called he
was told that the followers of tfrr-Qne True Light
had conscientious scruples against both enrolment
and military service, wherefore they would give
no information whatever. Then John Murray
took a hand, and ordered that the Chosen People
should be enrolled or prosecuted. The Law went
back ; but half of the sect, having got wind of the
“persecution,” had left the country, and the other
half said it was going as soon as it‘had packed up
its wings. So John Murray decided not to take
further action—which is what John loves doing
better than anything else in the world.
The arrival in Sydney of the Japanese
Antarctic exploring schooner Kainan Maru last
Monday is satisfactory evidence that the partv
never intended to try for the South Pole after
sailing from Wellington (M.L.)in February, or
that it was singularly ignorant of the weather
conditions at the feet of the world. With no
ponies, only sufficient dogs for one eight-dog
sledge, and a general appearance of poor equip-
ment, the little schooner sailed toolatein the season
to get through the ice—provided it was through
the ice she was to be steered, which isn’t at all
certain. In fact, nothing is clear about the expedi-
tion, except that in Wellington (M.L.) its mem-
bers couldn’t speak English, German or Chinese,
but in Sydney they can speak English with ease.’
In one breath they explain that they are going to
dash for the Pole ; in another, they say they are
merely a southern scientific outfit. One thing
alone is certain—that Lieut. Shirase is here with
his small ship and big sword and spurs. His
announced intention is to remain here for six
months—“ waiting for the spring.” What spring
isn’t mentioned.
AN AUSTRALIAN CHORUS SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is stated that the trusts and combines of Aus-
tralia spent £lOO,OOO to defeat the recent Referenda
proposals. This works out at sixpence per head of
population.
Chorus of Fat Men:
We care not a dump for the Federal crew,
The national dreams are dead ;
Our waistcoats swell, and the skies are blue,
The mob are blind to the things we do—
We bought ’em at sixpence a head !
Chorus of Thin Men:
What are you backing at the races?
Let’s have a ticket on the sweep!
George-street’s full of pretty faces,
And old Bill Smith is still asleep!
Chorus of Fat Men:
We gather the rents, and we stow the swag,
On plunder our souls are fed,
There are plenty of fish in the nets we drag,
And the people have never a tongue to wag—-
We bought ’em at sixpence a head!
Chorus of Thin Men:
Who was it mentioned Referenda?
Wake us when the thing is getting near !
Tell us when you think it’s time to send a
Billy round the corner for a beer!
Chorus of Fat Men:
This is a glorious land and free,
Let never a tear be shed,
The New Protection is up in a tree,
And Australia’s citizens all agree
To serve us for sixpence a head!
Chorus of Thin Men:
Who’s this bloke that’s come to wake us?
’Ere, take your carcase off my chest!
Strike me pink ! It’s Fat who’s come to take us
Back to dig his garden with the rest!
Grand Chorus of Fat Men:
The wool shall grow in our trusty land,
Where glorious mutton is bred,
Our hopes are high, and our faith is grand,
There is nothing Australian fools won’t stand,
We "bought ’em at sixpence a head!
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

PENFOLD'S
WINES
A curious anomaly was dug up out of the
dust in S. Aus. the other day. A sailor-man,
having been allotted £7 and costs, or two months,
for resisting the police in a Saturday night street
riot, reached over the rail at the Port Adelaide
Police Court and “passed” the constable in charge
a heavv left. He was about to he charged with
contempt of court, when someone discovered that
you cannot contemn a mere police court more than
a month’s-worth, whereas it is worth two to hit a
policeman. So assault it was, with two months
more.
A THREAT.
DISGUSTED SPORT: “ Well, if they don't bring

“ The Vagabond : The
sudden upheaval concerning the
methods of medical examination,
etc., on hoard over-seas boats
ought to bring to light a lot of
interesting items which are com-
monplaces to the man who has
done much deep-sea journeying.
The possibility of all the Plagues
of Egypt and other countries en
route escaping into Australia by
the Adelaide front-door is no new
thing. Two or three years ago
we passengers to Sydney were
tickled by the joke that several
of our friends landed themselves,
their goods and gear at Adelaide,
and trekked overland to Sydney,
without any bothersome medical
inspection ; while at Melbourne
and Sydney we all had our
tempers, but not our tempera-
tures, taken, and our tongues
listened to but not looked at.
We had to turn out and “fall in’’
aloirg the deck, and were kept
waiting there till the Health
Officer came along and counted
noses. He evidently found one
short in the tally, so we stood
some more on one foot and rested
the other while he went into a
long discussion with the purser.
It turned out that on the pas-
senger list was the name of a
man who had missed the boat in
London, and we stood on the
other foot and rested the first
one while this was explained at
length to the doctor, who seemed
to accept the excuse with re-
luctance and to be deeply sus-
picious of our having the man
concealed in our pockets. But
the passengers landed at Adelaide
were never even alluded to.
James Edmond sends a cry from the
Macedonia :
We are roaming l through the Red Sea in a dead, dead
calm. It is a wonderful sea in a historical sense. I got
up early this morning and looked out on the Egyptian
shore. In the distance I saw the left wheel of Pharaoh s
chariot. The tyre was burst, but otherwise it was in
good preservation. On the other shore lay the skeleton
of the whale which swallowed Jonah. With my glasses
I could distinctly see a Hebrew prayer book lying anions:
tbe bones. I believe Jonah left it behind him when be
quitted his lodgings. Further on is the humble residence
of Solomon’s 000th mother-in-law. She is very aged now
and lets lodgings. An Arabic card in the window says
that there are apartments for single Arabs. Just as I
had noted these facts, the last remnant of the rock that
was struck by Moses got in my eye, and I had to retire.
The case of Moses begins to interest me since X got into
these parts. It strikes me (1) that Moses was a very
plucky man to hit anything so much larger than himself ,
(2) that the whole course of the world’s history might
have been altered if the rock had got in first and struck
I have seen the noble Arab at Aden. He is just a brown
beggar like any other brown beggar. His native town is
the same old tangled, dirty horror. There is a series of
wonderful stone tanks (ascribed to Solomon) some four
miles from the town. They are supposed to be for the
supply of the town ; but their catchment area is almost
nil and the rainfall is almost nil, and the blessed tanks
are so much divided up by great stone walls that even if
they were full they wouldn’t supply Sydney for a day.
And there was just a spoonful of greenish water at the
bottom of them. Aden lives on condensed water and
precarious wells. It is surrounded by the driest, brown-
est, craggiest mountains on record. Some of the roads
dodge through deep cuttings and tunnels—dimly-lighted
tunnels as much as half a mile long, and so narrow that
no two* vehicles can pass. A chesty black policeman
regulates the traffic at each end.
God knows why His humble creature Man ever decided
to dwell at Aden. There is neither tree nor grass. The
harbor is really a poor sort of roadstead. The local
camel that drags the local cart is a gaunt beast soured by
misfortune, and his lower lip hangs down like an open
carpet bag in perdition. Sometimes he is brown ; some-
times he is grey ; and sometimes he seems to be covered
by a greenish fungus. Mostly he has a tail, but some-
times that useful addendum has dried up and fallen away.
Then there is the occasional small bullock and the
occasional almost invisible ass. The cab-horse, of whom
there is manv, is the oest-ventilated cab-horse in creation,
for the wind blow's right through him. He lives almost
entirely on his own caudal appendage. The other pro-
ducts of the place are cemeteries and thieves. The old
thieves are buried in the cemeteries, and the young ones
run behind the cabs crying out for backsheesh, and
hang on in such numbers that the well-ventilated horse
goes back as often as he goes forward.
It is a place of clamor and row. There is ho noise on
earth like the noise of all Asia bellowing about 2d. When
you try to buy something worth Is. in a local shop you
have the proprietor yelling at you, also his son, his grand-
son, his remote ancestor, his six assistants, and ten loafers
who have come in from the street. And the local
deformity—a person who walks on his hands and has
one leg doubled under him and one leg cocked up behind
his ear—takes up a resolute stand in the doonvay, and
vou have either to leap over him as you go out or else
pav him to go away. A marvellous creature is the local
deformity with one leg doubled under him and one leg
cocked up behind his ear. If he has a third leg he pro-
bably wears it in the middle of his back. These things
are his stock in trade.
* * * *
The sly-grog purveyor in Maoriland is
contriving to make ends meet despite the constant
drain of heavy fines. One of him was shown in a
recent prosecution to have traded 149 dozen
bottles of beer in a week at about 200 per cent,
above market price, and as it was in a metro-
politan centre the probabilities are that the bulk
of the cargo was unloaded on a Sunday. It is all
very disheartening for the Wowser; still, he
hasn’t lost hope that he will yet hear the echo of
the last cork being drawn and the crash of the
final dead-marine falling into the Past. And on
that day the pterodactyl will again inhabit the
earth.

“Adolphus the Penman ” discovers another
interference with Private Enterprise :
Unless some meddlesome scientists don’t take a pull, it
soon won’t be safe to trifle with another gentleman’s sig-
nature even for 2£d. The finger-print nuisance was bad
enough, this latest is worse. Dr. Lindsay Johnson,
of London, is the prime meddler. His idea was that in
many diseases, particularly of the heart and kidneys, the
patient’s pulse-beats are individual, and no one can con-
trol, even momentarily, the frequency, or irregularities,
of his heart’s action, as shown by a chart recording his
pulsation. So he proceeded to obtain a chart by means
of a “ sphvmograph an instrument fitted to the
patient’s wrist and equipped with a needle, which records
the pulse-beats on prepared paper. Following up the idea
other busybodies applied the test to handwriting. Magni-
fied and projected on a screen, it exhibits the turns and
qtiiverscausedbv the writer's peculiar pulsation. As a test,
a number of hospital patients were set to write a few lines,
and in every case the individual pulsations were dis-
tinctly visible when the magnified reproductions of the
MS. were thrown on the screen. The handwriting of
healthy persons, it seems, doesn’t reveal the pulse-beats ;
so where a document purporting to he written by any
person contains the beats, and his acknowledged writing
doesn’t, or vice versa, forgery may be taken as proved.
It appears, then, that in the future it won’t be safe for a
gentleman of my profession to do business unless he is
certain that he is perfectly healthy, and that the capi-
talist he proposes to do business with is healthy likewise.
And so far as I can see there are no healthy capitalists.

a u’eek, shinv elbows, and being-a-gentleman, aie
as dust and ashes compared with £2 and being a
mere —ah—laborer, as it were.
In its eagerness to get a case finished, the
Supreme Court in S. Aus. actually sat on Easter
Saturday. But it drew the line at Good Friday.
A sitting on that day has not been attempted
anywhere since the time of the English Judge
Mansfield, 150 years ago. He sat* once on Holy
Friday, in face of a protest from the indignant
Bar that there was no precedent for it since that
of Pontius Pilate !

The Oldest Inhabitant on the Oldest
Hospital:—
Sydney Hospital is about to start a Centenary Fund,
towards which J. C. Williamson will give one-half the
gross proceeds of the last night of “ Our Miss Gibbs” in
Sydney. Governor Macquarie laid the foundation stone
of the old “Rum Hospital” on October 23, 1811. The
contractors for the buildings were D’Arcy Wentworth,
Garnham Blaxcell and Alexander Riley. The remunera-
tion has been variously stated. Macquarie said that, in
payment, they had permission to import into the colon}'
45,000 gallons of spirits, with ,six months’grace to pay
the duty of 3s. per gallon. They also had the use of 20
draught bullocks from the Government herd. But Dr.
Lang, in his earlier histories, stated that the contractors
had the right to import 60,000 gallons. The Secretary of
State severely criticised Macquarie’s action in giving the
contractors a monopoly of importing spirits for public
consumption ; but his protest came too late. The profits
must have been enormous. Lang states that the contrac-
tors bought the spirits at 3s. per gallon, paid 3s. duty,
and sold at 40s. per gallon. Two of the buildings
erected under this contract still stand, the Parliament
House and the Mint.

Truth will out sometimes — by accident.
The other day a big Sydney drapery firm in its
catalogue proudly proclaimed that a certain
much-boomed make of flannelette was “war-
ranted inflammable.” Then someone produced
the office dictionary, and the offending page was
cut out of the catalogue.
“Exile” writes from Noiseville about
some lost Australian antiquities :
Being in Slumland to-day, I sauntered down White-
chapel past some fine old family residences now occupied
by Doukobohrs, Finns, Slavs and “To Lets,” and up
Cambridge-road to the Bethnal Green Museum, the only
public gallery of any importance in East London, the
place where they kill policemen. The chief gallery was
filled with a loan collection of Eastern art objects lent
by Lord Curzon of Kedleston, and bought by his wife,
the daughter of Solomon Leiter, a Chicago wheat-buyer,
who amassed a huge fortune by keeping an “honorable
understanding ” with other wheat kings to squeeze the
Minnesota and Michigan farmers as hard as possible, just
as the wheat-buyers used to do in Adelaide. Here also is
stuffed away a weird collection of tools used by the Duke
of Edinburgh for laying foundation stones in Australia.
There were 38 trowels of gold, silver and bronze, pre-
sented to Alfred by Mayors and chairmen whose names
were invariably emblazoned on the implements. A few
may be remembered by some of the people who helped to
pay for them : East Oollingwood Mechanics’ In-
stitute (Vic.) ; Sydney Town Hall; Alfred Graving Dock ;
Williamstown (Vic.); Central Arch, G.P.0., Sydney;
Prince Alfred College, Adelaide; Sailors’ Home, Port
Adelaide ; Victoria Tower, G.P.0., Adelaide ; Town Hall,
Melbourne ; and Temperance Hall, Ballarat. I had no
time to copy the whole list, as I was anxious to work my
way back to civilisation —meaning, of course, the Bank
of England. There were emu eggs, silver kangaroos,
gold emus, silver boxes, gold “Galateas” and enough
boomerangs with snobs’ names engraven on silver shields
to fill a safe deposit. The antiquities represented oceans
of liquor and mountains of gush and snobbery, and here
they will rest till some future Goths sack London and
remove the trowels and emus and kangaroos to a smell-
ing pot. The “treasures” are “guarded” by two old
and obese gentlemen who had been on the premises for
half a century or so, and were mildly astonished when 1
asked if the'Duke of Edinburgh had ever been thpre.
There 1 Why should he go there ! In fact, it seemed to
surprise them that anybody should go there.

THE PRIVATE ENTERPRISE DEVIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Speaking at a temperance demonstration, Rev. Henry
Worrail declared that the Federal Government proposed
to nationalise the drink traffic. It might, he said, as well
attempt to nationalise his Satanic Majesty. He would
be no party to a scheme that would create such a rotten
national foundation, and would certainly vote “No” at
the Referendum.— Melbourne Aon.
I wandered down into Hell last night;
The place was filled with a glow of light;
Old Harry was there and working hard ;
With furnace-burns was his old tail scarred ;
And the molten metal around me hissed ;
But there was something in Hell I missed.
No devils around me shoved and jammed ;
I heard no howls from the freshly-damned ;
About their business tame friends went,
Or tended machines on jobs intent.
The noises there in the busy gloom
Were those of a well-kept engine-room.
I listened long to the click and whir,
The clank and rattle, and then said : “Sir,
Could you tell me, please, if this is Hell ?”
Old Harry laughed as he answered : “ Well,
I say it is when I'm feeling bad ;
But why do you want to know, my lad ?”
I told him Worrail had voted “ No.”
“ Why ?” asked Old Harry. I told him. “Oh!”
A humorous gleam stole in his eye,
As he softly laughed. “ i’ll tell you why !
We put small jobs in each other’s way ;
We work together, as you might say.
“ Down here—this place is a private mint—
The Wages of Sin we stamp and print.
You bet if the Labor party tries
To meddle with Private Enterprise,
And say of Hell: “ This shall have an end,”
I’ll get a hand from my Reverend friend.
“No,” said the Devil, “it would not do !
If 1 went out of it he'd go too !
You bet he’ll keep these paying flames
From their grasping Socialistic games.
‘ Hell for the Devil,’ our motto is,
For competition’s the soul of biz.”
N.S.W.

The other Commonwealth—that of Crom-
well s time—would not have taken so meekly
impulsive “ Damn it! Let me tell
you ! ’ when he was interrupted before he could
reply to a question in Melbourne. The
chief of all Wowsing Parliaments in 1050 fixed a
careful scale of fines for “ language.” The mini-
mum, for a Bloke, was 3s. 4d.; the maximum, for
a Duke, 30s. For ladies there was the same tariff,
according to the rank of husband or father.
Children same price, and, if under 12, a whipping
instead of the stocks on default of payment.
Double fine for everybody after the first offence.
It would have cost Nesbit, assuming that this was
his first offence, either 6s. Bd. or 10s.—it depends
on whether a K.C. is an Esquire or a Gentleman.

One VV. D. B. Creagh the other day backed
his cart into a Sydney paper and unloaded him-
self as follows:
I have been a fair traveller iri this world, but I have
never seen so much drunkenness in a big city as I did in
Sydney in the last few days.
On Good Friday groups of men drunk and semi-drunk
were to be seen around nearly every corner, especially
near hotels, drinking out of bottles that had been sup-
plied to them from the back door. No moral or self-
respecting people should allow this to continue. I
counted no less than 15 men at one hotel, nearly every
one intoxicated. Their language was terrible, and little
children were waiting, and even fighting at the very feet
of the men, to gain possession of the empty bottles.
And, as in the case of the Three Holy Children
who saw the mob of small boys reeling drunk from
“ one of the brightest lighted hotels in Sydney,”
no name, street, locality or even compass point is
mentioned, no policeman was told about the
Terrible Language of the intoxicated, and no corn-
inunication was sent to the Inspector-General of
Police that would enable him to make a move
against even one publican. All is vague and in-
definite, like the oyster pie at a Dago’s food
factory.
“ Gil Gunnia” : The Waldschules (Forest
schools) of Germany might be imitated by Aus-
tralian Education Departments with benefit
to the youth of the country. These institu-
tions are in the woods, on the outskirts
of the big cities. Electric cars take the
children to and fro daily. All the work
and play is done in the open, though
roofed-in shelters are provided for
wet weather. The Government looks
after the tuckering of the kiddies,
and also pays for their schooling
and fares if their parents can’t pay.
What about a few forest schools
round our cities where the kids
from (say) the Sydney Rocks and
similarly congested areas could go
and be schooled in the summer
amidst the vales of Middle Harbor
or Hornsby, instead of being cooped
up in black holes, and having to
stretch their limbs in play-grounds
no bigger than a. moderate-sized
suburban back yard ?
-There seems to be no doubt that
Dutch New Guinea shelters some people
who are still living right back in the
Stone Age. The Dutch expedition which
lately got into the Snow Mountains came
iri touch with them ; and now letters come
from A. S. Meek, the Australian naturalist
and collector, who certainly has never had a
chance to hear or read of the other people’s dis-
coveries, very briefly describing the same primitive
barbarians. A Sydney ethnologist was particularly
anxious to ascertain if stone “ nose-bars ” were in
use. Mostly these nose “ornaments’’are made of
shell; but Meek luckily found one that was broken,
and thus discovered that it was of stone. The
accounts of the two expeditions differ very widely
in one respect. Naturalist Younghusband six
weeks ago wrote in London Timks about these
stone-age men, and said that they were ravenous for
iron and steel tools. Meek, from whom only pri-
vate letters have so far been received, says that
they didn’t want his iron and steel tools. For 20
years he has been roaming among the Islands,
particularly among the natives of New Guinea’
and he had always before found them eager to
trade ; but these stone-age people had apparently
never traded in their lives, and he made hardly
any progress with them.
“H.E.R.”: Westralian immigration is a
great scheme—for the immigrant! The Govern-
ment assists him to come out and go on the land
—and he never intends to go near the land. I
was speaking to one the other day, who looked as
if he’d just been transplanted from the
Thames Embankment. He offered to sell
me his German liner ticket to Sydney for
a trifle of 50 s. A few questions brought
these facts to light. He was nominated, and
planked down £5 for his passage. On landing
in Westralia, the Government refunded him £3,
but did not demand his ticket, which entitles him
to stay in Westralia for a year. Then, if he likes
(there is nothing to stop him), he may decamp
eastwards. No doubt Westralia is a most hospit-
able State, and its immigration-visitors are on a
good wicket.
AUSTRALLANS ABROAD.
MRS. BROWN-SMITH: “The brazen hussy-
how tranquilly she wears 'em, too!"
The other night, near Auckland, Maori-
land missed a big railway smash by about three
chains. Otahuhu is a small station on the main
trunk line, where a local passenger train is side-
tracked to allow the through express to Welling-
ton to pass ; but on this particular night it wasn’t
tied to the water trough, or the anchor wasn’t
down, or something, and the carriages sneaked
out of the yard while the crew was temporarily
absent, and started careering down the hill just
when the big main-trunker was expected to tear
out of the darkness on the same set of rails.
The odds on a collision were 50 to 1, when R.
Claude, employed at the local saleyards, looked
out upon the night and observed the two trains
preparing to fly at each other’s throat. Like the
central figure in a hellowdrama, he leaped the
railway fence, swung on the last car of the
escapee, applied the Westinghouse (of which be
had gained a knowledge), stopped the “local,”
raced to the front with a red light warning the
unconscious passengers as he went, and held up
the express just where he could reach out and
shake hands with it. Now a fund has been
started to show Claude that he can’t do things
like that with impunity, and the name of Gover-
nor Islington is expected to be handy on the list.
He was on the express.
“Boswell ’: “ Johanna,” in the Melbourne
Woman’s Letter (B. 20/4/11), remarks: “The
circle was packed with those who love to be near
a Governor.” It reminds one of other things.
As Dick Sleath and P. Bowling, Labor leaders,
could tell you, in the great gloomy gaol at Goul-
burn (N.S.W.) the various wings converge on a
space called “The Circle.” Delinquents
who scratch the paint on the cast-iron “regula-
lations ” are there mustered each morning, pre-
paratory to being hustled before the Governor to
receive their medicine. I don’t fancy that
“circle” has ever Den “ packed with those who
love to be near a Governor.’’ Evidently there
are “circles” and “circles” and Governors and
Governors.
“ Table Salt ” sends a hail from the boat
deck :
I have gazed down from high-level bridges and cathe-
dral roofs and felt no misgivings because I knew the
perches were firmly rooted in the 75-mile-thick crust of
the earth. Last week I inspected the remote hats of a
large crowd on a wharf from the boat deck of a modern
liner; and when I recollected that the steel mountain
only had a thirty-foot grip on the ever-moving sea, and
that the winds had a sort of G.P.O. to shove against, I
hastily clambered dow'n several iron ladders and won-
dered how voyages are completed. Some person was in-
cautious enough to advance the opinion that the cyclone
had got under the \ ongala’s long decks and tilted the
lost ship till it went over like an aeroplane. The
theory was promptly hooted—by the people who own
or are interested in the running of skyscraping ships.
These are the days of fresh air, pyramids of deck cabins
—and mysterious losses. Every second, boat is piled
almost to the funnel-tops with layers of airy sleeping
apartments, to which leg-weary passengers are hoisted in
lifts after they have absorbed much food and drink in
the dining-hall two or three storeys down. The naval archi-
tects who design the sea skyscrapers presumably know'
what they are doing ; and they are steadily going up—and
their ships are going down. When the ‘25-deck ship
with the same old 30ft. draught peers over the horizon, I
shall write some more on this subject. Meanwhile, I am
craning my neck and admiring the facades of our present
sea-pubs.
“Smith”: A Minister for Grace and
Elegance may he all right; but I want to instal a
Minister for Truth and Fairplay. We ought to
have had him as from January 1, 1911 There
never was, surely, in the history of any country,
a period of two months during which so many
lies, misrepresentations and half-truths were pub-
lished as we have just passed through. In addi-
tl0 j i to i he regular Press, millions of pamphlets
and leaflets were printed, in which even worse
lies and misrepresentations were placed before
the electors The heated and diseased imagina-
tion of the Tones soared to hitherto undreamed-of
heights. It seemed to run absolutely wild; it;
appeared to consider any old lie to be fit and
proper matter with which to deceive the voter.
Under the Russian system, the editor of a paper,,
or the publisher of any political pamphlet, has ter
rush around to the Minister for Truth with
a proof of his rag’s political matter. If
it isn t approved of, it cannot appear.,
sometimes the Minister for Truth runs;
his blue pencil through a few lines, andl
sometimes the whole article is thrown out,
Of about 30 pamphlets and leaflets that I have
come across lately, at least 25 would not have
been printed at all under that system. And iff
Iruth mattered, they wouldn’t have been printed .
Wherefore, as Truth ought to matter, I want it.
given a Minister whose one job shall be to guardl
it. I believe it is almost as well worth protect-
ing and encouraging as scenery or schnapper.

THE SUN’S STEADY JOBS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An interesting address on solar energy was given on
Saturday by Sir Joseph Thomson, Professor of Physics at
the Royal Institution, London. In the course of his re-
marks, he said ; “ The sun on a clear day transmits power
equivalent to 7000-h.p. per acre. If it were harnessed the
power required for the world’s work could be obtained at
the cost of lowering the temperature by a degree or two.
—j ßecent cable.
The good old sun we worship so pours down his wealth of
light,
Like some old spendthrift on this earth, from morning
until night.
Without a thought of vears to come, he throws his bars
of gold
And recks not of the time when he will be used up and
old.
The limelight man who runs the moon and works the
shadowed tides,
Irom all commercial bondage free, through skies of safety
rides;
Wave-motors, bobbing here and there, run little lighting
plan ts,
But at most jobs of any size some servile engine pants.
The river and the cataract, hemmed in by earthen banks,
Have long been harnessed up and work for something
less than thanks ;
And men who used to toil like slaves now sit and take
their ease.
Or tend the humming dynamoes in spotless dungarees.
But sweat still drips from many brows to wrin" from
Earth its yield,
While power wastes upon the coasts and spills upon the
field ;
The man who guides the jolting plough behind the plod-
ding horse
Emits a curse, and hungers for the great unharnessed
force.
Friends, if we could let work go hang and sit by summer
seas,
What would it matter if we lost a couple of degrees?
Our blazing friend who beams and burns has heat enough
to spare—
There is no need to work or freeze while he is smiling
there. "
When all the jobs are his at last, and winter brings its
clouds
w?if' Ve f u e 5, . ,n a holiday, employers stout in crow'ds
Will gather in their boardrooms dark, and reverentlv
speak
Of happy days when men worked hard for Two-Pounds-
odd per week.

ABORIGIN
ALITIES
“Montalex” : About Australian tree-heights.
The 302-footer mentioned by “Condot” (B.
20/4/’ 11) isn’t “in it” with some of the Titans of
the Dandenong Ranges, in Vic. There is—-or was—-
in that locality a colossus which, by a rough calcu-
lation, I made out to be 460 ft. high. I was laughed
at. “Why,” said one of the cachinnators, “that
would make it nearly 100 ft. higher than the tallest
of the Californian Wellingtonias, and everybody
knows they are the loftiest trees in the world.”
Then, in 1891, came along Professor Semon, who,
using accurate scientific methods, gave the tree’s
altitude as 480 ft—-easily a world’s record. I quote
him (“The Australian Bush,” p, .30.): “A specimen
of Eucalyptus amygdalina, in Dandenong Mountains,
near Melbourne, measured 480 ft. in height, thus over-
topping even the celebrated mammoth trees, Welling-
tonia gigantea (370 ft.), of Yoseinite Valley, the Stras-
bourg Cathedral, Saint Peter’s, and the Cheops Pyra-
mids, and equalling in height the Cologne Cathe-
dral.”
A SECRET PAGE OF
HISTORY.
The King pledges the regalia.
“Jimmie Pannikin” : Folks in the wonder-
fully wild sandhills Baek-o’-Kensington tell a tale
about the self-reliance of the Urban Outbacker, A.
William-Goat (or is it A. William-Cann ?). It ap-
pears that A. W., etc., was but walking in the Ken-
sington Desert, when he spied a huge snake. There
was no stick handy, but A. W. etc.’s resourceful-
ness rose to the occasion. He pulled out his strongly-
scented handkerchief, and threw it in front of the
snake; then he dropped one of his photographs on
the handkerchief and went off to look for a weapon.
Returning with a few rods of fencing, he threw
them at the snake in sections. The reptile did not
budge, though some of the post and rails fell quite
close to it. Investigation proved that the fascina-
tion of the photograph had.been fatal to the snake,
which had died s without a struggle. The acumen and
the enterprise of the Urban Outbacker are absolutely
astounding.
* * * *
“Jimmie Pannikin”: Why do sheepmen
differ so much in various parts of the Ma State?
During my three years of Outback exile, no sheep-
man (nor any man) ever raised his hat to me or
to his employer in my presence; but during my five
years’ soujourn Closer-In, the mere sight of my
driving gloves was sufficient to cause many men to
doff their hats. I maintain that Outback expands
men s souls, but Closer-In contracts them.
“Jack Jay”: There are exceptions to
the rule of Maori hospitality. For instance: A
miner, with his wife, child and pa-in-law was a
while ago on the way to Whangamata; wife and
child riding, men walking, and pack-horse ahead
with the outfit. Near an isolated Maori settlement
the pack-horse disappeared, and could not be found,
mainly owing to the fact that the Maoris had planted
him in the scrub. In due course the natives “found”
the animal and demanded £1 as a reward; and as
the amount was not in the party’s purse, the two
men were held in pawn while the woman was sent
on with the horses to try to raise the wind. Luckily
I had got bushed the night before by a wrong turn-
ing, and appeared at the Maori village for break-
fast. As soon as 1 heard the story of the two host-
ages, I said a speech to Cannibal Brother that made
him feel the cold wind from the open gaol blowing
down bis spine, and before he could warm his feet
again I had handed him a useless I 0 U for eighteen-
pence, and removed the pledges.

“Montalex” : There is much that I agree
with in Randolph Bedford’s recent shriek for re-
bellion against imported British traditions and cus-
toms; but, in the cause of accuracy, let us pull up
some of his wild assertions. He says of the English
oak in Australia: “Every successive winter finds it
postponing the falling of the leaf ; assimilating itself
to the Australian habit; becoming gradually ever-
green.” Now, when I arrived in Melbourne I ob-
served that the Fitzroy Garden oaks retained their
leaves about a month longer, and renewed them
about a month sooner, than would have been the
case in Britain. That was 43 years ago ; so that if,
as the Reckless asserts, the trees have been ever since
gradually shortening the leafless interval, they should
long ere now have become perfect evergreens. And
even more so should this be the case with the much
older oaks in Parramatta Park (N.S.W.). But no;
after more than a century of Australian existence,
these trees show no more alteration in leaf-period
than is to be expected from the milder local winter
conditions. Also, if “southern environment is
strong enough to alter types always for the better,”
how is it that the timber of the Australian-grown
oak is practically worthless? Such trifles stick not
in a Bedford’s memory. Again, Randolph spreads
himself over the vigor of the eucalypts he saw in
Corsica. Well, passing over my own observations,
let me quote from Professor Semon’s “Australian
Bush” (p. 30) : “The blue-gams reach an immense
height, and the dwarfish specimens of these trees
which we find in Italy, do not give any impression of
the stately and proud character of their Australian
parents.” Once more from Bedford: “This is a
country that teaches its struggling life new ways to
live. The rifle-fish earns his living by the accuracy
of his aim in shooting,” etc. Why, the rifle-fish is
of an essentially Old-World type, and is of great an-
cestral antiquity. Go to, Bedford! Thou hast a
lovely theory, and so many facts support it that it
is a pity thy bounding rhetoric should bring thee
into disastrous collision with other facts which
don’t 1
“Curse o’ Moses”: Scientific men are
great grubbers after facts; but when they come to
build a theory on the information they have gath-
ered, their logic is not always to be trusted. Pro-
fessor Keith, rooting up the floor of Essex, recently
discovered a 4000-year-old skeleton. It was that of
an ancient Briton, or other Freetrader, of short
stature, well-developed head of modern shape, and
sharp, small features. In describing the corpse the
man of lecture-rooms is on safe ground; but mark
what follows. The Professor argues that the right
scapula, by its abnormal development, shows that
the right hand was very often lifted up and down,
and, therefore, the deceased was most likely a cob-
bler I Hoot awa’, Professor! Hae ye never heard
mon, o’ the effect on the bones an’ muscles o’ liftin’
a wee drappie ower aften tae the lips? Ask the
opinion of any Aboliar on such a cadaver found in
Australian tertiary deposits, and he will give it in
one convincing word—“Shicker.”
WHEN THE BOOM BROKE.
WARDER : “ Visitor waitin' for you in the reception room , Ninety-nine."
NINETY-MINE (ex-company promoter, suspiciously): “ Visitor? Tell him I'm not at home."
“A William-Cann” continues his ro-
mances :
Bulletin readers, both Outback and Right-in, are
continually pestering me with inquiries as to where
and when I first met “Jimmie Pannikin.” In no
spirit of vulgar egoism, but for the sake of peace, I
will tell them. “Pannikin” and I were at Oxford
together—that is to say, when I was a blithe, young
undergraduate at “the House” James was employed
at Vincent’s Club.
Well do I recall my first evening at the wine club
which flourished then —-as no doubt it does still —in
B.N.C. “Pannikin’s” was the arm which guided my
reeling form from the assembly of garishly clad
youths to the cool air of the quad, outside; “Pan-
nikin’s” the brain which gathered from my involved
hiccupings that my lodgings were in King Edward-
street; and “Pannikin’s” the hands which undressed
me. He then left, having another case to attend to.
Towards the end of my second year I took defi-
nitely to drink. “Pannikin” about the same time
became a hopeless slave to alliteration. A self-
respecting University was demonstrably no place for
either of us. We were asked to leave.
I had frequently noticed a Sydney daily paper in
the college Common Room ; the sheet was posted to
an Australian in residence. A study of it convinced
me that the Commonwealth was par excellence the
place for us. In this far land, I learned, the majority
of the inhabitants were Anarchists, Atheists, Robbers
and Bursters of Homes, while a powerful political
party advocated rapine, piracy, and the confiscation
of people’s property. In such an environment, I
reasoned, the mere dipso, would appear innocuous,
and the victim qf alliteration and braggadocio would
be a pleasing social circumstance.. I put my view to
the distracted “Pannikin.” He begged, tearfully, to
be taken as myr aide-de-camp. “I want none of this
Man Friday business,” I replied, raising him from the
ground. “Besides, from what I can learn, free-born
Britons who go to Australia under;agreement to work
are immediately gaoled. I shall pay your passage,
and you must then carve out a career for yourself
among these lawless and demented revolutionaries.”
In the evening of my life I hope once more, as
“Pannikin” would put it, to traverse the Turl, cross
the Commarket, perambulate Pembroke, vegetate in
Vincent’s and meander musingly through Magdalen.
While doing so, rest assured that my thoughts will
often fly to “Pannikin”—one of the most knowledg-
able bushmen that ever learnt his craft in a British
pantry.
“Blackboy”: Some of the prettiest and
best brumbies in Australia are to be found on the
Great Southern line between Albany and Perth
(W.A.). They are small, certainly, the average
height being not more than 11 hands; but when
once yarded and tamed they make good workers,
and are worth anything up to £2O a-piece. The
best time to hunt them is after winter rains, when
the grass is young. They are then weak and not too
hard to catch; but when they are in nick, nothing
but the best horses can stay with them. I take it
that these ponies (which are mostly bay, with a
sprinkling of grey and iron-grey) are the property
of the State, but numbers are trapped by private
individuals, who make a good thing out of the
business.
“Leo Lear’’: Why the obloquy attached to
the cuckoo? The bird is not of necessity one that
does not build its own nest, or that places its egg
in the residence of another bird. For instance, the
pheasant coucal of Queensland is labelled “cuckoo,”
yet it builds its own nest in the grass, and the mother
tends her young like any other respectable woman.
For ; at least one of the class I have actual admira-
tion. That is the channel-bill cuckoo, which passes
its maternal duties, on to the crow; and anything
that takes in a crow deserves honorable mention.
These channel-bills—large, grey birds—get their
name from a peculiar groove in the side of a long
bill. They are the most discordant and loudest-
voiced of birds, and fly over the country, pursued
by an army of crows, like lost souls escaping from
the seventh inferno, with a squad of under-devils at
their heels.

NIGHT THOUGHTS AT RYDE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The first beer ever harvested in Australia was
grown at Ryde.
The tramway twinkles down the night,
The gum-trees sweep and sway,
And up the darkness to the right
The old road winds its way ;
Some common fruits are grown out here
Where once were fields of waving beer!
Faint pubs on hidden corners stand,
Large storage tanks arise,
Wide wisps of wind the clouds have fanned
Across the lustrous skies;
Australia’s sacred place is near,
Where grew the first brown blooms of beer.
Long, long ago the mug was filled,
What time the world was strong
The air along these wastes was thrilled
With patriotic song—-
Oh, glad was all the land to hear
The gurgling voice of malted beer!
The pubs are closed, the midnight hour
Falls on the sleeping land ;
Ah, days when pewters foamed in flower
In ‘each forgotten hand—
Shall memory leave without a tear
The earliest home of Austral beer ?
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

“J.C.C.”: Concerning Black Sister in the
Solomons:—-
The Malaitan girl starts work in the tribal garden
when she is about 10 years old, and stays there until
a young man buys her from her father, and, by
right of purchase, becomes her husband. There is
no marriage rite or ceremony. Values vary con-
siderably, but, as a general rule, £6 in native
money or goods will buy a likely-looking damsel.
After marriage she goes to live in the women’s
house and work in her husband’s garden; also she
prepares his food, but does not eat with him. The
women eat by themselves. Polygamy is common.
If a man is rich, or a chief, he will buy as many
wives as he can afford. The more wives he has the
more ground he can cultivate. He builds a house
for his women, each of whom has a separate room ;
but he does not sleep in the house. That is “tam-
bo,” i.e., prohibited. The Malaitans are extremely
moral; the young girls are carefully watched, and
misconduct means death or mutilation. Divorce is
looked on by the woman as a disgrace, and if it
happens she generally wanders into the bush and
hangs herself. Then, if the suicide’s relations are
stronger than the husband’s, there’s a fight. As a
rule, though, the trouble is settled by payment of
pigs or food and money by the husband. Families
are only fairly numerous, infanticide being practised
more or less all over the island. Maternal love is
certainly shown in many cases, and very deeply too;
but on the other hand, maternity is avoided as far
as possible. Women about to give birth to children
make themselves a lean-to of leaves in the bush,
and remain there until after the event. It is death
to look upon a mother and child until three days
after the birth. One peculiar thing about the
cannibal tribes is that, though they very rarely eat
the flesh of a woman, they invariably eat the bodies
of young girls. They are cheerful devils.

“Billy B.” : There are a few things which
even the Maorilander can teach the Australian, and
one of them is the art of cadging beer artistically.
I told this to the Reverend the other night, and was
promptly referred to Prov. 12, 22; but when I of-
fered to secure five and a-half drinks for Is. he
began to take a more Christian view. Also, he pro-
duced the shilling, which was uncommonly civil of
him. The rest was easy. We turned into the front
bar of the Blue Garter, ordered two pints and pro-
ceeded to masticate them. When three-quarters of
mine had gone the way of all beer, I took out my
little matchbox, selected one of the large, defunct
flies which I carry round for the purpose, and sur-
reptitiously dropped it into the mug. Then I called
up the bar lady, and shudderingly drew her atten-
tion to the corpse. “Oh, I’m so sorry,” she ex-
plained, and splashed the remains into the heel-tap
barrel, where the pick-me-ups come from; “the
dirty brutes get up the pipes, I think. There! I
don’t think you’ll find any in that one.” She was
quite right; but peculiarly enough we found one in
the Rev.'s mug at the Swan with the Scorched Tail.
Over a fire of twigs prehistoric man grilled
his steak. A most wasteful and uncomfortable
method. Yet Phillips Ormonde (266 Flinders-
st., Melb.) wonders why with open fire we still
cook the cook as well as the food. Should not
all cooking be accomplished by hot air? Will
not the griller of the future contain provision
for its circulation? After use in the hot-air
cooking range, could it not be re-employed for
heating the house instead of the chimney?
Practical, patentable solutions are material-
ising ou this interesting subject,*

FRESHAIR FRED : “ Yes, mum, I do. But me eyesight ain't so good as it useter be.”
“Ague”: Clarence approached a farm
towards evening and entered. In answer to his re-
quest for “employment,” the cocky asked: “Do you
know anything about grubbing?” “Yes,” said
Clarence, manfully, and was sent up to have a feed.
Next morning the cocky gave him a spade, a mat-
tock and an axe, and Clarence thought of the honor
of old England, and determined that whatever an
Australian could do he would. It was half a mile
to the ground to be grubbed, and on the way the
immigrant’s thirst for information was his downfall.
“Excuse me,” he said ; “but will we need all these
tools to kill them with?” Then the cocky showed
him out, to face once more Australia’s magnificent
distances.

of the house addressed me: “Y’ mus’ be feelin’ dull.
Come up th’ paddock, and I’ll show yer the place
where father committed sooiside.”

“Austra,” from a distance: —
To “Vagabond” and other seekers for snake-bite
cure, let me recommend perusal of an article in
Queensland Agricultural Journal for March, 1911.
The theories and experiences
of more than one scientific
and professional man are
given; also information as
to how to distinguish poison-
ous snakes and their bite
(after the bite) from non-
poisonous. Some alleged cures
are supplied; but it is stated
that it is doubtful if there is
any universal cure, each par-
ticular breed of crawler re-
quiring a special dope of its
own. The ligature is unani-
mously recommended when
the bite is in certain parts of
the human frame, but is
classed as useless for others.
When used it must be loosen-
ed each half-hour to let in
some fresh blood. Failure to
do this may mean mortifica-
tion and amputation. Imme-
diate bleeding and thorough
sucking, which is stated to be
quite innocuous to the sucker,
is the best first aid. Per-
manganate and strychnine are
made to appear nearly as
deadly as the snake-juice.
Patients have been known to
die through unscientific dosing with these goods,
as well as from fear, after being bitten by a
strictly non-venomous reptile. Vinegar is recom-
mended for injection to the cut. It is some conso-
lation to read that far more snakes than usually sup-
posed are not deadly in their bite. The worst of it
is that “Vagabond” and a lot of other estimable
denizens may be bitten by something harmless, and
killed by the deadly remedy before this can see print.
“Eddyson” : The shy Australian suitor is
a numerous class. When Rowe died at Crow’s, he
left a little fat widow of 28, and Daley, her nearest
neighbor, suddenly developed an interest in the lone
woman, which he manifested by standing afar off,
and gazing at her house. He indulged in this form
of fervent love-making for hours at a time. On
occasions he ventured to the back door, grinned
into the kitchen, made a breathless Observation
about the weather, and fled again. One Thursday
Mrs. Rowe met Daley on the Wheatfield-road. He
stopped her with the usual meteorological items,
crumpled his hat, pawed the road with his large
feet, and said: “Well, I s’pose I gotter be goin’.
There’s a letter to post.” Daley walked 11 miles to
Wheatfield and 11 miles home again, and the butcher
brought the letter back to Mrs. Rowe at Crow’s next
day. It was from Daley, and contained the laconic
proposal, “Have us?” The widow was agreeable,
but Daley had gone into hiding, and after the third
day Mrs. Rowe stuck her acceptance on his hut
door: “Notice to James Daley. Mrs. Rowe is will-
ing.” This drove the sensitive James into deeper
cover. After a fortnight the widow received another
letter: “If you wanter marry me, you’ll have to
get the Hogans to hold me.” The Hogan brothers
were appealed to, and generously undertook to per-
form the friendly office of roping and holding. James
Daley was married in a very dim light, with one
Hogan actually holding him to the scratch, and two
other Hogans guarding the exits in case of a break-
away
* * * *
“Jack”: Let me tell “Kowhai” (B.
23/3/’11) that if “he knows what he knows,” I
know that which he doesn’t know. I have been
through Maori pa s, from Dunedin, in the South, to
Whangaroa, in the Far North, and my circle of
white friends includes, several doctors and chemists,
and when “Kowhai” infers that the Maori lady
uses race-suicide methods to prevent the appearance
of the once-frequent half-caste (why half-caste?), he
is wrong. Can “Kowhai” point out to me one in-
quest where the verdict showed attempted child-
murder as the cause ? I can show him plenty among
the whites. And let “Kowhai” remember that it
was the pale ha who brought the unmentionable
diseases to M.L. As to dirty “niggers’ ” paws: In
the poorer districts, where Henare is not in pos-
session of a motor-car, there is plenty of “digging
in the dish,” I admit. In other parts, especially in
the South Island and the Wairarapa, there is an evi-
dence of cleanliness which would be a credit to any
white. Some . Henares employ white cooks. Know
of one Mere whose kitchen floor is clean enough to
eat your shark from. Not far away is a white
Mary, who comes from an “old English family.”
A Maori dog wouldn’t eat bones from her table.
* * * *
A magistrate at Gawler (S.A.) lias been
called on to settle a point that he admitted was be-
yond him. The claim was for damages for trespass
on plaintiff’s stud heifer by an inferior bull. De-
fendant said the bull was an infant of six months,
running with its mother, and he called a vet., who
swore that it could not propagate its species under
12 months old; but practical farmers swore they
knew of cases under six months. The Bench coyly
declined to be accepted as a judge on that point, but
remarked acutely that Time would show. Meanwhile
it gave small damages for the “trespass.”
“Bush Botanist”: In answer to the pa-
thetic appeal of “F.C.” (B. 30/3/’ll) for a forti-
fication against flies, I provide the remedy. A chain
belt of castor-oil trees, mixed with Cupstia gluti-
nosa, Siline glutinosa, Nicotiana glacicum and ta-
baccum, is all that is necessary, and the plants can
be obtained in Australia.
“Eavesdropper” describes an evening at
Sam Slavin’s: —-
“Go and tell yer mother it’s near yer bedtime;
clear out now', and stop yer noise. . . . Yes, as I
was say in’, the labor question is gettin’ a fair ter-
ror ; carn’t keep a milk-boy a week; thinks ’e
ortent to do no more than ’is boss. I give ’em 12
bob a week too. Last chap I ’ad used to stop ’is
graft every time a neighbor come near the yard.
I’d get talkin’, yer know, about one thing and an-
other, and thinkin’ the milkin’ was goin’ on all
right. Blarst me if he wouldn’t be sittin’ at the
same cow' when I’d ’ad me talk out, though the
missus ’ud be jobbin’ ’im all the time to ’urry up.
She w'ould have nine or 10 milked herself, and the
young crawler loafin’ ”
“Dad, Mum says she ain’t through the separatin’
yet, and to chuck a bit of wood on the fire, and see
the kettle is boilin’ against she’s done.”
“All right! Maggie, bring me a bit of w'ood, and
’ere’s the kettle; fill it up and you and Sammy
bring it. I’ll ’ang it on. Now, wheel the baby;
’e’s wakin’ up, and keep the kids quiet. Jim an’
me carn’t ’ardly ’ear ourselves speak. . . . Wot
was it I was sayin’, Jim? Oh, yes; about them
young loafers gettin’ 12 bob”; —
“Dad, Mum says will yer give ’er a lift with the
big vat?”
“All right. Suse ain’t as strong since she got
knocked over with the roan bull larst winter.
Used to be able to lift it ’erself, but women carn’t
stand much roughin’, yer know. Still, she’s
a middlin’ good worker—good with the milk-
in’, and separatin’ and feedin’ the pod-
dies and pigs. Not much at fixin’ up a fence,
though. I laughed at ’er the way she fixed it once,
when the cows broke into the maize. Well, I must
go and give ’er a lift. Wait and have a drink of
tea; she’ll be done soon. .. . Well, where’s the
vat, Suse? Jim’s gone; wouldn’t wait for a drink
pf tea ; the kids too noisy I s’pose. Wot made yer
so long to-night at the separatin’?—it’s near 10
o’clock.”

“Pick”: “Tarboy” (B. 30/3/’ll) notwith-
standing, the tallies of the men in the recent cham-
pionship bartering match at Toowoomba were not
much to get excited about; and unless Zimmerle can
go considerably better when pushed, his reign as
top-dog will be short. If I remember rightly, Zim-
merle’s record panned out equal to about 128 sheep
for eight hours’ work. How would the performance
stand against that of Volney Day, who, beside me a
couple of years ago, at Bibbenluke (N.S.W.), where
the sheep are high-class merinos, and admittedly
hard to shear, cut out 644 in two and a-half days?
“Tarboy’s” yap about sheep that are a disgrace to
the pure merino is away from the question, for now-
adays that class of sheep does not exist on any big
station where notable tallies are made, and only
very rarely among the small owners. The sheep-
men of Australia have long since recognised that
grass is too valuable to be eaten by inferior wool-
lies, and well-bred rams are, in these days, cheap
and easily obtainable by those who want them.
THE DREAM BEAUTIFUL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last night I had a dream of drunks. The casement
opened wide,
And on a sea of crayfish red I sailed away outside !
The streets ran amber-brown with beer, as full as
any tick,
And loaded trams came lurching past, half-tight, and
breathing “Hie!”
The street was drunk, the pave was drunk, and
drunk as drunk was 1:
I sailed round forty thousand curves before my goal
drew nigh.
Then sweet it broke upon my sight—l tried to rise
and cheer —
The office of The Bulletin, a-wash with floods of
beer!
Yea, yellow Beer and glorious poured over me in
sprays,
As through the door I swept in state upon a tide
of “crays” !
Straight to the Cashier’s room I rolled, and there
did instant biz —-
He paid me for one beery verse with forty casks of
fizz!
Small time had I to “hie” my thanks, for swift I
swept from shore,
And straight along and up the stairs to see the
Ed-i-tor,
Who sat within a bath of stout, the foam about his
chin,
To write a leaderette on “Drink” beneath a shower
of gin.
“Hie, sir!” said ho. I answered “Hie!” when
swift, from “off” the scenes,
Ihe beery staff arose and drove me thence witli dead
marines.
1 floated on past office boys, all loaded to the brim.
Till wading through the fragrant ooze there strode
a figure grim.
There was no joy upon ,his face, no bottle in his
hand;
He did not lurch, or breathe of beer; outrageous!
He could stand!
He drew me from the streaming crays ! He propped
me ’gainst the wall,
lie spoke to me of Temperance, he sorrowed o’er
my “Fall.”
I gazed upon the face. ’Twas he! In shouts of ire
I broke —-
A Poet, yes, and sober!— then I knew I dreamed,
and woke!
N.S.W.
AUGUSTUS-HIC-BLOWFLY.
THE SONG OF THE BAIN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
List to the rain !
It sings a song:
Of cattle at play where the grass is long,
Of watercourses,
And galloping horses,
And laboring bees the blooms among;
Of herbage spreading across the plain,
And waving grain!
And in the lay
The rain-drops trill.
There are notes of joy for the tanks that fill,
For the nesting plover
That circles over
The salt-bush flats beside the hill;
And over the restless flood-streams grey
That sweep away.
But though the rain
Sings soft and clear,
There’s a note of warning for all to hear;
For the streams are flowing,
Unchecked, and going,
Though the land may faint with a drought next
year.
“Beware the drought!” sings the falling rain.
“ ’Twill come again !”
N.S.W.

THE
RIGHT DRINK
On a Sea Voyage.

It has been found by many people that one of the
best drinks aboard ship is the non-alcoholic O.T.
This beverage is a stomachic of undoubted merit,
whose action tones the stomach, aids digestion
and prevents the unpleasant sensations of nausea
which accompany sea-sickness.
For the tropical regions O.T. has proved a par-
ticularly safe drink, as it derives beneficial pro-
perties from certain ingredients much used by the
inhabitants of hot climates to resist fevers, dysen-
tery and other inflammatory complaints. O.T.
carries the endorsement of the highest medical
and analytical authorities, and is now obtainable
in any part of the world.

dering what title John Murray, of Victoria, will
