Gauze.
Dave Dunsmore, sen., who, on the N. S.
Wales South Coast, was reckoned to be the best
judge of a horse in this hemisphere, recently
pulled up suddenly at the 78th post. In better
days his colors were carried ahead at many a race
meeting, and up to the time of his death he
claimed to be the head, tail and body of the Ber-
magui Jockey Club, he having, for past good
services, been made a life member of that once
flourishing, but now defunct, organisation.
PROFESSOR WALTER THOMAS
MILLS, M.A., Socialist, Author
and Lecturer.
The annual meeting of the Melbourne
Chinese Inland Mission has just been held. This
extraordinary organisation was formed bv a des-
perate fanatic named Whitridge about 15 years
ago. He was a man of fiery energy, with all the
spirit of Peter the Hermit or Michael Davitt, the
latter of whom he much resembled in appearance.
He had observed the success of Cheok Chong and
others in setting the “missies” to work on the
Chinese in Lillie Buk-street. So says Whitridge:
“ This is the thing for China. The girls will be
like Samson’s foxes, with the firebrands at their
tails, let loose in the cornfield.” So he got to
work on his mania, and he and his successors
must have sent over a hundred young women to
China. Money for the futile job has been raked
up in a remarkable way; for though Whitridge
had none of his own, he was an amazing success
at getting it from others.
The “Dickens centenary stamp”
scheme, recently described in The Bulle-
tin, was very nearly blown up by one of
the descendants. Henry Fielding Dickens,
ICC., wrote to London Times declaring, in
effect, that the allegation that some of
the novelist’s descendants had to
tighten their belts was grosslv exag-
gerated. He denied that the" 14 de-
seendants generally” were in
necessitous circumstances, and
said that the exaggerations
made the testimonial open to
misconception. The testi-
monial committee replied
stating that it had always
deprecated undue insist-
ence upon the family’s
pecuniary circumstances,
and that the affair was
not a chari table scheme,
but “ a small payment
on account of some
deferred royalties,
remedying to some
extent the injustice
done by the copyright
laws.” Mollified by
this explanation, Henry
Fielding Dickens grace-
fully said that the mat-
ter had been thoroughly
cleared up, and de-
clared that the scheme
was a great and unique
recognition of a glorious
ancestor. So things are
as they were before—
except that Henry Field-
ing Dickens, K.C., has got
an advt. out of the
scheme.

Died recently, on the Burdekin (Q.), Hero,
an ancient aboriginal who was one of the six
black trackers taken from North Queensland to
Victoria to follow the Kelly bushranging gang.
The yarn about that business kept him in beer
and ’bacca the rest of his life.
Alex. F. Chomley, who died suddenly the
other day on an inter-State steamer, left a big
crowd of Victorian friends behind him. He had
been for years prominent in tennis squares and
golfing circles, and professionally was also known
in many angles as chief surveyor to the Metro-
politan Board of Shirks. Privately he was 44,
and a son of H. M. Chomley, once in command of
the Victorian police.
Victoria’s departing Carmichael gave the
gushing Madden a dig in the ribs the other day.
His Ex. has been summed up as a decent sort of
Scotchman, an eloquent bazaar-opener, and a
good judge of cattle, beetles and other feats of
nature; and he patiently stood all the panegyrics
on these counts. But when Madden poured out
the grease promiscuously at a ’Varsity function
lately, Carmike quietly rose and told the ap-
plauders that the greatest flattery he ever got was
when he unexpectedly found himself mentioned
in Mrs. Creighton’s life of Bishop Creighton. He
had studied under the divine, and the mention he
got was: “Carmichael knows his own limita-
tions.”

South Aus. has a bush and river parson
named “Moss” Gabb (it’s not his fault). Sent
to Penola, he soon struck strong opposition. The
hall wherein he preached put up the rent and the
salvation of souls was getting expensive; so
“Moss” set to work and with his own hands
built a church. After it was finished some young
scoffers stood in front and kicked up a row. Gabb
set the congregation singing a hymn, left the
pulpit, got out the back way, and knocked one out.
“Now, quickly,” he said, “you other fellows; are
we going to have any more argument? They’re
finishing the hymn, and I must get back.”
It was a real old warrior that answered
the final roll-call in Adelaide last week. Alex-
ander Aird —he does not seem to have been any
relation to the late millionaire of London and
Egypt, whose brother also lives in Adelaide—
was one of the few remaining Mutiny veterans.
A boy when the Crimean war started, he was in
the Scotch Highlanders at 18 before it finished,
and started for Sebastopol. That was an event-
ful voyage. The regiment was sent back to
Malta without landing, and then ordered to China.
That meant, in those days, going all round
Africa, and at Capetown the troops were hastily
switched off to Calcutta. It was by giving that
order, by the way, that George Grey always
claimed to have saved India—though some his-
torians deny him the credit of it. Aird was in
time for all the fighting that ended in the relief
of Lucknow. He was a Scotch constable after
that, and a good Australian for the last 28 years.
Being a member of the S.A. Corps of Veterans,
he was buried with full military honors. This is
a delicate attention that is much appreciated—in
anticipation ; it was the late Wybert Reeve who
secured it for the old warriors.

Though the big, snow-crowned figure has
caught many another eye, the Scythe-bearer has,
so far, happily overlooked Peter Kerr, F. R.I. B. A.,
architect of the Parliament Houses in Melbourne,
the Law Courts, G.P.O. and other buildings.
The fine old man first looked on a dour and misty
world in Aberdeen on April 21, 91 years ago.
A young Maorilander named B. Bryant
recently sat for the Law Society’s examinations
in London, and gripped two of the five prizes
offered. He was one of four who secured first-
class honors, was awarded the John Mackrell
prize as the tj>est practical lawyer of 150 entrants,
and collected the Kent Law Society’s prize.
Still, the 149 home-grown British students cut up
three prizes amongst them, so they get the odd
trick—which, of course, shows that Foster Fraser
was right when he referred to the decadence of
the British stock when transplated to these
southern parts.
“Australia’s Governor” is in Sydney—
the man the Commonwealth appoints to repre-
sent it in Papua. He is J. H. P. Murray, Judge
and Colonel, but Australian first of all. After
five years’pioneering service, first as Judge and
then as Lieut.-Governor, he left on furlough for
Europe, and is now spending a month in Sydney
before taking up his work again. The Murrays
have a way of doing things. The father was,
first, Speaker and then President of the Legisla-
tive Council in N. S. Wales. Of the two sons
one went to England, and became Professor
Murray, the foremost authority on Greek litera-
ture in Britain. The other studied law, and was
for years Parliamentary draughtsman in N. S.
Wales, and afterwards Crown Prosecutor and
Acting Judge. In his spare moments he
hammered away at Defence, and, practising
what he preached, worked up to a colonelcy. In
Papua they don’t wait for him to die before
speaking of him with admiration. He is the sort
that appeals to the tough pioneer—a six-footer, in
his day a champion boxer and swordsman, a man
who never looks to see what sort of coat a man is
wearing, and who never imagines that it is neces-
sary to hang frills on himself in order to be
dignified.
Among the Mooltan’s quarantined pas-
sengers was Charles Burnett, K.C.8., one of the
three Australians who have risen to be generals
in Britain’s army, the other two being Baker
Russell and Reginald Gipps. Burnett was born
in Woolloomooloo in ’43, spent his boyhood in
St. Kilda (Vic.), and at 14 was taken to England
to be educated. Last year he retired from the
Western command in the Cold Land, and slipped
away to see his native land and his old home.
The house, in Burnett-street, St. Kilda, still
stands, though little else remains of Melbourne as
he knew it. The intervening years were spent
largely in India, though Ashanti and Afghan
campaigns—the siege of Kandahar being in-
cluded in the latter —took up a fair slice,
and left his breast covered with medals.
More important to Australians, he was British
attache with the Jap army in the Russo-Jap war.
He hasn’t yet opened his mouth to tell what he
knows of the Monkey and his tricks, but he has
already said this one sensible thing: “I haven’t
been here long, but I am told you have a Labor
Government that is going to play the deuce. I
don’t believe it. lam certain your working men,
like those in the Old Country, are patriotic. . . .
It is evident your Labor men care very much for
the defence of Australia.” Then, with a chuckle :
“ They look after its health, anyhow. It is 50
years since I left, and the first thing my country-
men did when they laid hands on me was to clap
me in quarantine and vaccinate me. But I for-
give them. It was necessary.” Burnett will have
•a look round and then go on with his wife to visit
his brother in Maori land.
The yarn is related with great joy by a
N.S.W. Judge’s wife. One afternoon, while Ins
Honor, dressed in his oldest clothes, was working
in his garden, a man put his head over the fence
with: “Say, mate, any chance of a job here?’
The person in the ancient togs was thinking hard
at the time, and snapped out, after the manner of
husbands, “ Better ask up at the house.” At the
house the man called for “ the missus.” She told
him they already had a gardener. “Yes, I
know,” he replied. “ I’ve been watching him.
He’s rooking you, missus; he’s no gardener.
You better give me a show.” But the Judge still
keeps his billet.
Justinian Oxenham is the new permanent
head of the Commonwealth’s Postal Department.
When Barton formed his first Federal Ministry
Drake, of Queensland, was made Postmaster-
General, and had the job of organising the depart-
ment. Until he stepped out of State into Federal
politics he had been Postmaster-General inPhilp’s
Bananaland Ministry, so he naturally took along
some of the men he knew to help him lick the
department into shape. And that’s how, first,
Scott and now Oxenham got to the top. The
latter started at- Warwick, the little township
which has a habit of sending out youngsters who
do things.
Now that Australia’s first famous native-
born portrait painter, John Longstaff, is again in
Australia, why not try to catch him and instal
him as art director and instructor in painting at
N.S.W. National Gallery, the queen of which is
surely his spirituelle and charming “Lady in
Black”? To quote the instances of his power,
there are his masterful portrait of Henry Lawson,
painted in a couple of hours, and that beautiful
canvas, “The Painter’s Wife,” in Bendigo Gal.
lerv. Mr. Longstaff, who has just been commis-
sioned for portraits of Professors Anderson
Stuart and MacCallum, of Sydney University, has
a personality which has always strongly com-
mended itself to students, and under him the
national art school would probably prosper.
The Socialistic Professor Mills, now study-
ing Australia and throwing in an occasional lec-
ture, was born in a hunter’s cabin in the Adiron-
dacks. He earned wages when only eight years
old, and was permanently away from home, as a
farm hand, at 13. By his own exertions he
climbed to the degree of Master of Arts. He
got his first lecturing experience as a temperance
lecturer; but that platform he soon found much
too narrow. He went into the bigger question of
economics, and his text-book on “The Science of
Politics ” has gone through 14 editions. In 1904
he was called in as an expert and drafted the ad-
vanced provisions in the Oklahoma State Consti-
tution, and they were adopted. The English
Ambassador, Professor Bryce, said it was the best
document of the sort ever adopted by a State.
Mills had a few words to say in the recent
Referenda campaign. He didn’t profess to have
made a. complete study of Australian politics, but
on his experience of the working of the American
Federal Constitution he had no hesitation what-
ever in advocating two Yeses.
SENATOR GUTHRIE, of S.A., the
sailor’s politician.
Goulburn district (N.S.W.) is a laud of
large families and many cricketers. Recently a
local team, whose members were all named Tick-
ner, played and defeated an eleven named
Jeffery. The winners are now out after a third
eleven named Horton, while the losers want to
take it out of a crowd called Blake. Some years
ago a team of brothers named Henderson, which
included also umpire and scorer, issued a chal-
lenge to play any similar team in the world.
They heard of no acceptors.
“FAT IS GRATEFUL.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Squatter Sam McOaughey recently paid in a sum of
£17,000 land-tax, and expressed himself as being glad to
do so much for the country that had done so much for
him.—Press Item.
Send the tidings flashing round
To our land’s remotest hound,
Till the dullest of our dullards gets his pate-ful,
Of the latest we have heard
(Quite incredibly absurd ! )
That, at last, we’ve found a case where Fat is Grateful.
Other years have passed away
Which (as hist’ry writers say)
Brought the nation into view of matters fateful ;
But a novel era breaks,
When the nation wildly wakes
And discovers—is it dreaming? Fat is Grateful.
In the meagre and the lean
Mostly gratitude is seen
When they kiss the hand of Kindness for her plateful;
But a case occurs at last—
Let it peal upon the blast!—
Where, in spite of all his blessings , Fat is Grateful!
If the fashion would but spread,
It may be the lifted head
Of the wealthy would not raise our passions hateful ;
We should grudge good fortune less
If the Lucky learned to bless
And to show their Luckless Brethren—Fat is Grateful.
QUEENSLAND.

£150,000 of reserves capitalised .. 20,186
Mar., 1911.. 207,428 10 p.c. = 150,000 57,428 77,614
There probably never was a time when this co. was
told a cheerful tale at its half-yearly meeting". The
bigger the profits, the more dismal the lamentations,
as a rule. The future is always full of gloom. “As
it is certain that the mills now working in Queens-
land and N. S. Wales can easily make all the sugar
needed in Australia, during the next four years at
any rate, some loss may be expected to follow the
over-development of the industry,” says the chair-
man, alluding to the Queensland Government’s de-
termination to help in establishing more sugar mills.
Still, “only one-third of the loss can fall on us, this
being our share of the present production of raw
sugar in the Commonwealth.” Nevertheless, The
Bulletin hasn’t any expectation of seeing the co.’s
profits diminished by £65,000 or so per half-year.
True, the co. may produce only one-third of the raw
sugar, but practically all the producers of raw sugar
have to send their output to this co. for refining, and
if the co. can’t make its 10 p.c. (with a bit up its
sleeve) one way, no doubt it will in another. As to
that 10 p.c., note that it is only nominally 10 p.c.
During the past 3J years alone no less than £575,000
of profit has been capitalised. That is to say, though
the shareholders put up only £2,425,000, they get
the dividend on £3,000,000; so the 10 p;c. is really
12 i p.c. And that is on the basis of the capital
paid up in 1907, and on the assumption that all the
capital up to that time had been bona-fide paid up
by shareholders. As a matter of fact, though, there
had been previous bonus distributions, so that the
dividend is actually more than 124 p.c. And what
it could pay, if it divided all its profit, there is no
telling. The plain fact is that the bonus distribu-
tions alone during the past four years have averaged
over 5J p.c. p.a.
The other day Acting-Prime Minister Hughes,
after reciting some facts and alleged facts, declared
that the co. was “making at least 20 p.c. profit,”
and added —“It is not a fair return. It is an unfair
return. It comes from the pockets of the people.”
The chairman referred to Hughes’s speech, and
asked —“Now, what are the facts?” And he pro-
ceeded to show that, in some respects, Hughes was
wrong, technically, at any rate. But he did not, and
he cannot, say that even 15 p.c. is a fair thing. That
is the whole point. The co., splendidly managed in
many respects, is too greedy. Sugar is a modern
necessity; and this co. is making Australia pay
anything from £lOO,OOO to £150,000 a year more
for this necessity than it should have to pay. That
greediness isn’t going to pay in the long run. It is
simply inviting attack. So far, admittedly, the at-
tack has been repelled—as it was last week. But, of
course, last week is not going to end the matter. It
is simply a question of a little more agitation. That
is the plain situation that the investor has to face.

But that doesn’t cover the part of the latest report
and accounts that shareholders will glue their eyes
to. The directors break the news in this cautious
way: “The policy pursued by the co., while pro-
gressive, has always been conservative, and neces-
sarily so, as sums much in excess of the amounts
available for dividends have had to be regularly de-
voted towards developmental work and in providing
new and additional steamer tonnage. To keep pace
with the demands of the continued rapid expansion
of trade, the directors have now decided upon the
adoption of a forward policy, by which the co.’s
smaller steamers will gradually be replaced by new
vessels of a larger tonnage and increased speed. This
will shortly necessitate an increase of both the
authorised and paid-up capital.” The present paid
capital is £300,000; there are 250,000 shares fully
paid to £1 and 100,000 £1 shares paid to 10s.,
and the co. has power to issue another 150,000
shares. While the directors are about it they pro-
pose to do something worth doing, and allow for all
the expansion they can even dream of. They will
ask, therefore, that the nominal capital be raised
from £500,000 to £2,000,000. It is further pro-
posed that for every £1 share the co. shall issue
another £1 share, or 250,000 altogether, and that
the 100,000 10s. shares shall be fully paid up to £l,
the £300,000 required for the two transactions being
drawn from the reserves. That will make 600,000
shares. At the same time a further 100,000 shares
will be issued; the holders of the 600,000 shares
will be entitled to apply for these pro rata. Also,
the applicants for these new shares will receive one
free share (to be also paid for out of the reserves)
for every two they pay for. The payments for the
100,000 shares will be spread over 12 months.
Boiled down, then, the holder of, say, 300 present
fully-paid shares will get 350 shares for nothing,
and will be entitled to apply and pay par for 100
shares, while the holder of 300 10s. paid shares will
have his shares paid to £l, and will then have the
right to apply and pay for 50 shares, while getting
25 for nothing. For a few years past the co. has
been paying 10 p.c.; previous to that it paid 74 or
8 p.c. The co. has 29 years behind it; in 14 years
it has paid 10 p.c., in 12 years 8 p.c., in two years
7$ p.c., and once 12 p.c. For the past five years an
aggregate profit of £330,396 has been shown; and
of that a total of £150,000 has been divided. On
the present capital ( £300,000), plus the watered
capital ( £350,000), the profit would thus average
about £lO 3s. p.c. p.a. From the aggregate profit,
however, large sums have been written off. Assum-
ing, though, that they need not have been written
off-—assuming that sufficient depreciation had been
charged direct to Profit and Loss —it is plain that
the co. could not conveniently pay a 10 p.c. div. on
the enlarged capital out of earnings equal to only
£lO 3s. p.c.—and managing director James Burns
certainly would not pay it. A 3s. p.c. margin isn’t
enough for him. Further, though it is quite possible
that the co. has made a good deal more profit during
the past few years than it has shown, those last few
years have been an unusually fat period. The co.
may pay 10 p.c. on the enlarged capital; but the
investor who relies upon it will very likely be sadly
disappointed. The management, in a memo, to share-
holders, certainly holds out no such hope. “For
some years past,” says this memo., “the co. has
paid 10 p.c. p.a. div. . . . and in the ordinary
course of events the same amount ( £30,000 p.a.)
should be payable upon the [enlarged] capital, thus
establishing the paid-up capital upon a 5 p.c. divi-
dend basis.” Not that this management will be satis-
fied to pay 5 p.c. perpetually. It may get back to
10 p.c. But meantime the management no doubt
sees quite well that a co. which nominally pays a 5
or 6, or even 7 p.c., can make a stronger appeal for
concessions, or to be even left alone, than one which
nominally pays 10 p.c. Which is no doubt the real
reason for the present tranfer of reserves.

There is a biggish increase in ship property. The
co. is diving deeper into the Java and Singapore
trade, and that means larger and more expensive
steamers. It is very doubtful, though, whether
these can show anything like the profits that the co.
has been accustomed to make on its capital in the
Island trade. Book debts are, as usual, very heavy.
Apparently the Islands are a region of long credit.
And long credit generally means large losses. Still,
the last thing the co. is at all likely to be charged
with is a reckless habit of giving away credit—or
anything else, for that matter. Cash and investments
stand' at £94,262. Some of the' co.’s investments
are known to have turned out remarkably well—
the Queensland Insurance Co., for instance —and a
man probably wouldn’t beggar his widow by giving
£150,000 for the lot.
How does a Burns-Philp investment pan out at
current prices? Paid shares have run up to 555.
Sixty shares at 555.= £165. Twenty shares at £l=
£2o._ Seventy bonus shares. Total 150 shares=
£lB5. If the co. pays 5 p.c. on the re-formed
capitals £7 10s. p.a.=:£4 Is. p.c. If it pays 6
p.c.— £9 p.a.=:£4 17s. 6d. p.c. If it pays 7 p.c.
= £lO 10s. p.a.= £5 13s. 6d. p.c. So the co. will
have to pay over 6 p.c. on the re-formed capital to
return 5 p.c. to the investor who pays 555. for the
present £1 paid shares.
There has been much talk about 8., P.’s 10s. shares
and the basis on which they come into the scheme.
Naturally the holders of these shares would prefer
to pay up the other 10s. and then come in on the
same floor as the others. The holder of 60 partly-
paid shares in that case would have his holding
brought up to 150 at a cost of £5O. Under the
directors’ scheme, he gets only 90 shares at a cost
of £20 —and the other 60 shares would be very
cheap at a cost of £3O. It is a nice point; should
the directors have called up the 10s. share and then
treated all alike, or 1 should they have done what
they are doing, using the reserves to pay up the
unpaid 10s. per share? The crux of the matter
appears to be —What was each shareholder’s interest
in the reserve ? Suppose there had been a liquida-
tion, how would they have shared? Obviously the
fully-paid would have got just twice as much as the
contributing. Two men—A and B—have each 60
shares —one lot paid, the other contributing. One
(A) will get 60 bonus shares+2o to be paid for+
10 of the second bonus shares. The other (B) will, first,
be entitled to 30 bonus shares. That is right. But
in this critic’s view he ought to be entitled then to
take up just as many new shares as the other man,
because, if he had only paid 10s., he had accepted
the liability for another 10s. So this person’s view
is that B should be entitled to 30 bonus shares+2o
to be paid for+five of the second bonus shares. If
he cared to pay up the other 10s. on his present
holding, he would then have 115 fully-paid shares
at a cost of £5O. In other words, this writer be-
lieves that the directors are perfectly right in saying
that the holders of the 10s. paid shares are entitled
to only half the number of bonus shares that the
others are entitled to, because that represents their
proper proportion of the assets; but it seems that
they should be entitled to apply and pay par for
just as. many new shares as if they held fully-paid
shares. The latter concession is, however, not worth
very much. The only difference, in fact, between
the directors’ scheme and what this writer judges
to be the theoretically perfect scheme, is that the
former allow the 10s. paid shares to apply and pay
for one new share where this writer would allow
them to apply and pay for two.

The last half-year’s profit is shown in the table
after writing £5OOO off premises and transferring
£2OOO to the officers’ provident fund. Looking
down the first column in the table it will be seen
that the disclosed profits have been simply bounding
along. It may be that, in the earlier terms, the
bank was making profits which it did not disclose. •
It is suspected that the National, like a good many
others, did not have a thorough cleaning up after
the burst of the great Melbourne boom, and for
many years afterwards was putting aside profits and
writing down assets. In other words, assets which
probably it had supposed to be worth £lOOO were
found to be really worth only £BOO, so that £2OO
had to be made up out of profits; and as the £2OO
became available, the asset was gradually written
down to its true value. It is quite possible that a
good deal of undisclosed profit went that way—to
wipe out old bad debts. But it is also quite pos-
sible that assets which seven or eight years ago
were thus written down to £BOO, have since been
sold for more than £800; and in that case the sur-
plus has been available either for internal reserves
or for profits. In the former case they wouldn’t, of
course, be disclosed; in the latter they would be
reckoned in with profits in the ordinary way. This
may explain why the visible profits are so much
greater than the visible profits of a few years ago.
But there have been other pumps at work increas-
ing the flow into the treasury. Take a 10 years’
comparison:—
Liabilities— 1901. 1911.
Notes £335,688 £340,101

Other properties .. 429,204 48,165
Thus advances—the things out of which a bank
makes most of its money—have increased over 80
p.c. in 10 years; so, other things being equal, the
profits from that source should have increased at
least 80 p.c., too—probably more, because expenses
don’t usually increase in proportion to increase in
the turnover. “Other properties”—assets on hand
from the boom-time —have been reduced to almost
nothing. That means that values have risen to a
level which has induced the bank to sell. But the
bank has been letting out very large sums of money
at the same time; and if it has accepted the same
basis of valuation, it may possibly find itself left
with another big batch of depreciated securities
when the tide runs out. This isn’t inevitable, how-
ever. Theoretically a bank shouldn’t make any bad
debts or losses. It should always be covered. But
when competition for advances is keen, as it has been
lately, and always is when money is plentiful, the
inclination is to offer to advance just a little bit
more on a security than the next-door neighbor. So
far the seasons have favored the bank wonderfully.
It began its vigorous policy five or six years ago,
and, so far as the earlier advances are concerned, it
ought to have made itself very safe. But its ad-
vance business is still extending so rapidly that its
risks must still be very substantial indeed, unless,
of course, it lias managed to get the business in
spite of insisting upon a large margin between the
advance and the present value of the security.
The chief figures have moved in 12 months this
way:—
Mar., TO. Mar.,’ll.
Not,esin circulation.. £330,986 £340,101 Inc. £9,115
Bills, etc 1,523,994 1,598,836 Inc. 74,842
Free deposits .. 3,797,760 4,228,316 Inc. 430,556
Deposits at interest 5,850,823 6,326,410 Inc. 475,587
Liquid assets.. .. 4,794,750 4,178,296 Dec. 616,454
Advances .. .. 8,017,862 9,672,564 Inc. 1,654,702
Premises, etc. ... 479,156 468,321 Dec. 10,835
The bank thus owes £12,493,663, and has liquid
assets totalling £4,178,296. That means 33 p.c. ;
so, on its present resources, the bank is already
trading up to its full capacity, consistent with
safety, if not a trifle beyond it. The bigger the
proportion of the funds in non-liquid assets the
bigger also the risks. Further, the larger the de-
posits and other liabilities in proportion to share-
holders’ funds, (capital and reserves), the bigger the
shareholders’ risks; and in this case the bank has
just upon £7 of the public’s money for every £1
of its own, and that is a heavy proportion. Yet, in
spite of risks which, so far as figures tell, are un-
usually large, the visible profits are not very large.
Calculated on capital and reserves combined, they
show up as follows:
Sept., 1907.. £6 0 1 p.c. Sept., 1909.. £6 19 3 p.c.
Mar., 1908.. 5 1 5,, Mar., 1910.. 7 0 8,,
Sept., 1908.. 517 2 ~ Sept., 1910.. 619 1 ~
Mar., 1909.. 619 7 ~ Mar., 1911.. 712 6 ~
Nothing definite is, however, to be deduced from
that; a comparatively low return may mean that the
bank is only taking the best business, which nearly
always means a comparatively low rate of interest;
on the other hand, it very often means that the
bank’s assets are fully valued, and that it hasn’t
much internal reserves.

The last line is to some extent approximate only;
it is assumed (1) that directors’ fees and other
exes., to be appropriated presently, will absorb
£200; (2) that the usual 4 p.c. will be paid on
capital; and (3) that the usual 5 p.c. bonus will
be paid on purchases, and that it will absorb £3873.
It is a curiously old-fashioned society this, which
still hangs on to many of the quaint methods of the
early and very unbusinesslike promoters of co-
operative effort. For instance, there are no di-
rectors. There is a “committee.” And the com-
mittee, though it draws fees quite regularly, never
charges them up with other working expenses; it
waits for the general meeting to authorise their ap-
propriation. Also, there are no shareholders. There
are only “members.” And there is no fixed capital;
“members” may withdraw it at any time. The last-
mentioned freak is the only one that matters much.
The shareholders and directors can call themselves
what they like, and it doesn’t matter; but when a
big trading society, with its funds in merchandise,
stock and premises, etc., is liable to be called upon
to pay out all the capital almost on demand, then
it is built on a rotten foundation; and it is liable
to bring the co-operative system into discredit.
Naturally, being liable to be called on to repay
capital (at present £78,468) and loans ( £13,945)
almost on demand, the co. has to keep a large cash
balance lying idle; so the shareholders don’t get
anything like the return they should. But that
doesn’t matter. It is the inherent weakness of the
present system that does matter. A bank that pro-
posed to take people’s money on the same terms,
and with no capital of its own, would be laughed
at. What the management apparently never has
recognised is that all the real capital it has is its
poor little reserves.
Berrima District Farm and Dairy Co.
(N.S.W.):—
Bonus to Written Re-

1908.. 5967 10 „ ~ 99 4240 320 8,454
1909.. 4363 10 ~ „ 99 1465 295 10,958
1910.. 1591 10 „ „ 99 1051 275 11,124
This belated comparison shows a great shrinkage in
the year’s profits. But, it isn’t as alarming as it
looks. This is a co-operative concern, and the crux
of a co-operative constitution is that the profits
should go to the co-operators. It doesn’t matter
much whether they get the profits in the shape of
higher prices when they supply the cow-product to
the factory, or in the shape of bonuses subsequently.
This co. has lately changed its methods a little, and
by reducing the commissions by about 25 p.c., has
given the suppliers higher net prices month by month
—which is better for them than for the co. to pile
up profits and then distribute them in a lump.
“The aim of co-operation,” the directors say,
“should be to ascertain the lowest cost at which
effective marketing can be accomplished, and then
fix the commission charge on that basis” —-which
states the position exactly. Big bonuses, whether
in butter-marketing or in life insurance or anything
else, may be a delusion and a snare. They mean
that you are paying or being paid (as the case may
be) too much in the first place. It is to be hoped
the cow co-operators have been got .to thoroughly
understand that; for no doubt there are plenty of
non-co-operative rivals ready to make capital, if
they can, out of the shrinkage in bonuses. The co’s
position is, as usual, apparently sound. True, lia-
bilities amount to £20,096; but that is mostly for
the last month’s supplies, and on the other side
there were £15,798 of cash, £12,227 of book
debts (most of the customers pay fortnightly or
monthly), and £2235 of stock. The creditors are,
therefore, most amply protected.

This is a trading co., carrying a large stock, valued
in the present accounts at £154,582, which is equal
to more than three-fourths of the capital. Obviously
there is no way of telling whether that stock is
conservatively valued. Yet almost everything depends
upon that valuation. Profit in a concern of this
kind doesn’t consist of the difference between what
the co. paid and what it got for the goods sold,
less working expenses. From the figure thus ar-
rived at has to be deducted the amount by which
the unsold stock may have depreciated, either from
old age and exposure, and mice and worms, or
through a fall in the market value of the goods.
Plainly also the extent of that depreciation is a mat-
ter of estimation only ; so to a great extent one has
to trust the management. If it goes wrong, the in-
vestor is liable to go wrong. No doubt one can form
some kind of opinion from the financial history of
the co. Here, for instance, we see that the manage-
ment has built up visible reserves of over £25,000
in nine years, without increasing the rate of divi-
dend ; and that is a sign that the management aims
at putting the co. into a strong position. These
comparisons, too, are favorable; —
1902. 1909. 1910. 1911.
Capital .. £194,102 £200,000 £200,000 £ 200,000
Overdraft .. 15,466
Other liabilities 36,507 75,774 59,442 57,810
Stock .. .. 146,247 181,347 149,622 154,582
Factories, etc. 76,108 103,005 103,193 104,062
Book debts .. 45,201 56,829' 53,513 53,827
Cash, etc. .. 3,432 4,373 6,761
In 1908 the liabilities were £90,670 ; they are now
£57,810. That is a movement the right way, be-
cause no new capital has been got in meantime.
Stock (book value) has been reduced from £175,486
to £154,582. There may be less stock carried now;
but it is also possible that the reduction is due to
a more stringent valuation. On that point, however,
it can only be repeated that nothing definite can be
said. _ Even the directors themselves may be misled.
Thus in 1902, it will be observed, a very small profit
was shown, and, as the directors explained at the
time, that was “owing to the necessity of making
special provision in respect to stock and book debts
at the Wellington and Christchurch branches, to
meet undue inflation of values, which have only re-
cently been discovered.” 1

“The co.’s collieries have not worked so well as
during the previous half-year, the trade having fallen
off considerably.” This, of course, follows on the
strike. The trade of the previous half-year was,
however, quite abnormal. Stocks had been depleted,
and consumers’ first care was to build them up again.
That half-year, therefore, supplied much more than
the half-year’s consumption; and the profit was much
bloated thereby. Now the pendulum has swung back ;
and it isn’t certain whether the swing in that direc-
tion has finished, even if the Vend gets through all
its troubles. This co., certainly, is unusually well
situated. It has not only got a good trade, and
good men to push and hold the trade —the latter
possession is very important—but it owns a valuable
railway, too. The two mines (the East Greta and
the Stanford Merthyr) are lumped with the railway
property in the published accounts, and there is no
way of telling nowadays how much is written off
their book-value out of profits. In past years it is
certain that large sums were disposed of in that
way ; so that, though the item covering the fixed assets
stands at £189,808, it is doubtful whether much
more than the odd £19,808 represents the mines.
Anyway, as far back as five years ago the two mines
had been written down to £23,613. Assuming that
the railway is good property, the position is, there-
fore, very strong indeed. If that asset is worth
what it stands at in the books, then it seems safe to

Evidently something is in the wind with
regard to the City Bank of Sydney, an institution
which has kept its real position a mystery for very
many years past. Within a month the shares
have jumped from £4 15s. to £5 15s. It is satis-
factory to get this hint that the bank has put itself
into a good position again; but it is a pretty
rotten state of affairs when hints of the sort come
from the Stock Exchange and not from the published
accounts.
“Gwy Some insurance cos. got off the mark
rather too smartly in catching the N.S.W. Workmen’s
Compensation worm. Some wily agents told all
and sundry that they were compelled to take out
accident policies under the Act. Of course, there is
no obligation to insure—if a man likes he can per-
sonally lump a risk as big as a bank, though he
would be a fool to do it. Still, the point is whether
there 'wasn’t false representation, which the law
might regard disrespectfully, no matter how conser-
vative and haughty the co. might be which the agent
represented.
Baker’s Creek, Hillgrove (N.S.W.), crushed
136 tons for 2070 z. gold. The crushing came from
the stopes over No. 14 level, and in the development
of the northern ‘shoot. The latter work is to be con-
tinued by driving No. 13 and No. 15 above and be-
low the present workings. No. 14 has been taken 92
feet to the north, and average assays hold out a
good hope of this block of ground becoming profit-
able. Some experiments have been made with the
cyaniding of tailings, which give an extraction aver-
aging 9s.' 2d. But a larger plant may be required,
and if so shareholders may be asked to fork out a
call to pay for it.

Wylie Creek, one of the New England
(N.S.W.) dredging shows, is still having poor luck.
When things go right with a dredging concern,
the money flows in; but it doesn’t take much to
make them go wrong. Clay beds first, and then
floods, were responsible for the past half-year’s poor
results —a net loss of £43.
“Oof Bird” (W.A. ) looks at the best-
known of the Kalgoorlie mines, and reports as fol-
lows: “Ivanhoe. —From the 1620 ft. down to 2120 ft.
levels, the main shoot shows a decided tendency
to shorten in the south end; but below the latter
point a big slide dipping to the south makes its
appearance, and so far the drive at 2270 ft. indicates
that the shoot is inclined to lengthen again. If it
doesn’t, a considerable reduction in the mine’s ore
reserves is inevitable. Great Boulder. —Unless new
makes of ore are located east of the present main
workings, Boulder’s career as a great mine is ended.
The greenstone, which has come over to the west,
and pushed the backbone lode of the property well
into Golden Horseshoe ground, is the real cause of
the present ominous state of affairs. Golden Horse-
shoe. —This mine has certainly got a nice windfall
in the Boulder boundary lode at 2800 ft. ; but, as The
Bulletin lately mentioned, it will take from three
to four years’ sinking to reach this ore, and there is
not likely to be any material improvement in the
position till then. At present the mine is not
equipped or developed enough to warrant the treat-
ment of more than 25,000 tons per month, and if
the co. tries to do better than this the whole concern
will break down under the strain. Boulder Perse-
verance.—-Generally the workings are looking well,
and a big tonnage of broken ore is lying in the
stopes. Provided no tricks are played with the
mine, there is a good prospect of some more divi-
dends being paid to shareholders, especially from
mining in deeper ground, as the tendency of the ore
bodies coming west gives the Perseverance a chance
of getting something from the Associated at greater
depth. Kalgurli.—Recent drop in the output, and
the inability of the management to say at what
standard future returns are to be maintained, has
made speculators a bit shy of this stock. , The patchy
nature _of the ore certainly makes it hard to even
approximately estimate its worth, but a look through
the workings . would convince any mining • man that
the property is far from being ‘done.’ Development
towards the south end at 200 ft. should reveal big
tonnages of Bdwt. to 9dwt. ore, and, with these large
quantities of even grade ore to draw on, it should
be much easier to regulate the output in future.
But to avoid any further difficulty in this respect,
the co. would be well advised to increase the roast-
ing and crushing capacity of its treatment plant to
allow of an additional 3000 tons a month to be
handled. The best of the bottom levels in this mine
is the sixteenth. Here, from the small beginnings
of a 2ft. lode in the crosscut, the ore widened out in
the first few feeet of driving to a total of between
25ft. and 35ft. So far it has maintained that thick-
ness for a length of over 200 ft. Associated.—The
outlook is simply rotten, and if the new manager
has any reputation, he had better give notice as soon
as he reaches the mine, otherwise he will lose and
probably never regain it.”
An attempt is being made in the West
to form a co. to acquire the Never Never mine, near
Marvel LOch, together with equipment and 12,000
tons of slimes. One-half of The total issue of 40,000
ss. shares are offered to the public, 8000 are to be
annexed by the vendors as part payment, and 12,000
are to be held in reserve. So far mining has been
carried on to a depth of 160 ft., and to date the
treated ore has averaged 21s. Sulphides are showing in
the bottom of the present workings, but, except for
the bald remark that it is not known whether they
will cost more than the oxidised stuff to treat,
nothing is said about them. Silence, however, is
generally preserved when Yilgarn sulphides are
hinted at, and, therefore, it isn’t very remarkable
that few observations concerning them are made in
this case. Available ore is given as 100,000 tons;
but whether this means in sight, almost in sight, or
not in sight at all, is not clear. The fact remains
that, on the treatment of the tonnage mentioned, a
possible gross profit of £50,000 is figured on. In
addition to the share interest, the vendors are to
receive £4500, in monthly instalments of £lOO,
and £5OO has also to be paid to the trustee of a
certain assigned estate. If the flotation comes off
the co. intends to splash up £l5OO in the erection
of a vacuum filter plant, and it proposes to treat
the accumulated slimes right away. From this
asset, which appears to be the best of a doubtful lot,
a profit of £5550 is figured on.
Oroya Links (W.A.) is said to be get-
ting “favorable developments” in its optioned Gimlet
property, at Ora Banda; but, spite of this cheerful
announcement, it will come as a surprise if a deal
is effected. Briefly, the position is this: On the
surface the shoot has a length of 150 ft., and between
surface and 150 ft., where there is a length of 550 ft.
of 335. ore, about 20,000 tons of 40s. ore has been
dug out. Four winzes have been sunk below 150 ft., and
judging by these workings there are good reasons for
believing that the next 100 ft. of ground below 150 ft.
will yield 40,000 tons of 40s. ore. This, taking
costs and losses in residues at 305., would leave a
paper profit of 10s. per ton, or £20,000. This is
all very well as far as it goes; but, then, £25,000
has to be paid for the property, and an extra
£lOO,OOO found for equipment. So, assuming the
shoot continues to maintain its length, width, and
value in depth, it would take all the paper profit
earned from the ore in the mine down to 650 ft., to
pay the purchase price and cost of mine equip-
ment. In view of this, the Links people will pro-
bably make room for some less critical buyer when
the option expires.

The Bulletin respectfully draws atten-
tion to the preliminary particulars concerning the so-
called mining properties which the recently-regis-
tered Australia United Goldfields, Ltd., of London,
is reported to be getting ready for flotation. All
going well, an area of 394 acres, comprising mines
at Bullfinch, Kalgoorlie, Mount Margaret and else-
where, is to be worked by the co., which, if it has
the requisite luck, will have a working capital of
£200,000, less flotation expenses. Of this amount
£lOO,OOO is said to have been underwritten to
enable work to be started at once. Three more or
less venturesome experts have reported on the pro-
perties for the vendors, who, by the way, receive
£20,000 and 525,000 paid-up shares. Altogether
some nine or 10 shows are included in the flotation,
and, although their combined prospects don’t offer
much security, annual net profits are estimated at
£312,000! Perhaps some magician might be able
on a pinch to produce this wealth, but in this present
writer’s opinion a mere mining man has no hope of
doing it. It is even doubtful whether he could get
out enough gold to pay expenses.
Exhibition 00. is the latest of the Yil-
garn boom flotations to liquidate, and several other
similarly-situated concerns are getting ready to fol-
low suit. Exhibition shareholders are to get some
of their money back, but how much is not yet defi-
nitely known.
Farr, chairman of directors of Chaffinch
(W.A.), put through the 55 bags of ore broken from
the much-debated trench. The total yield was 7oz.
16dwt.—20oz. to the ton for five bags, and 6oz. to
the ton for the balance. But, since the co. can’t
find any more of the mysterious deposit, share-
holders won’t get much consolation out of the figures.
Re Randolph Bedford’s defence of his own
attitude in the Bullfinch-Chaffinch business, and an
incidental reference to Gerard Williams’s report on
Mount Elliott (B. 27/4/’ll). Bedford said that
Williams tried to turn down Elliotts at 30s. When
Williams reported on Elliott, The Bulletin is told
that shares stood in the vicinity of 100 s. His report
was to the effect that, though the mine was valuable,
it did not justify the price at which shares were
then standing. On this question Williams says: “It
is true that my estimate for smelting cost has been
somewhat over the mark,' but the reduced value of
present reserves more than compensates for that
error. For the rest, I can only say that I only wish
every report I may write in the future will be so
well borne out in practice.” R. Bedford’s opinion
of G. Williams as a mining engineer is, of course,
Bedford’s own, just as Bedford’s opinion of Bullfinch
was Bedford’s own — The Bulletin’s was quite the
opposite. This paper’s high opinion of Williams’s
qualifications has been already expressed.

Obviously the worst record is that of the Chiltern

tops the list easily, but it has nuggets and it works
shallow ground. The other three claims are vastly
bigger concerns, and they are now panning out wash
yielding thus: Duke and M.L., 465. 7d. per
fathom; Great Southern, 445. 4d.; Chiltern,
Valley, 455. 6d. There is thus not much variation
in gold contents, but there is a great difference in
working costs. This is so because the Duke and
Main Leads has better holding ground than the two
Rutherglen mines, and its workings are much nearer
the shaft. The best outlook appears to be at the
'Great Southern. This mine has had its two or three
years of tribulation like the Chiltern Valley, but a
tributary is meandering in, probably to enrich the
big lead. At the Chiltern Valley a rapid has been
proved in the gutter, a fall of 14ft. having taken
place in the bed of the lead within 472 ft., while
the normal dip should be under 2Aft. This has
meant the impoverishment of the gutter, and an addi-
tion to working costs. Ahead is still the Lock-out
tributary. This should join the gutter below the
rapid.
Another Daylesford prospectus is out. The
New Specimen Hill is to take over Vernon’s shaft
and the equipment, together with what was left in
the mine by the Bull crowd, which worked it as the
Victorian Star. That co. lifted 14,000 tons for an
average of but threw up the sponge, probably
because of the petering out of good stone at the Vic-
torian Cornish. Now W. T. Edwards and R. Smith,
both of whom sign themeslves “late manager,” and
one G. Rowlands, manager, testify that a shoot of
stone, 200 ft. in length, went underfoot at 330 ft.
Edwards writes: “From my personal knowledge of
the property, I believe there is every prospect of
your mine becoming a valuable one, and, if worked
legitimately, should pay dividends for many years.”
That “if worked legitimately” has an odd ring. One
would like to know if there is any hidden significance
in the phrase. The show seems entitled to a trial,
although to predict dividends at Daylesford is as
daring as to declare that a flat make there neces-
sarily brings good gold. The “loading” in the float
is not tough, probably because the market won’t
stand tough loads. The plant on the claim repre-
sents a neat asset.
Gippsland copper and platinum mine, Wal-
halla (Vic.), got into difficulties with its water-
jacket very soon after starting to smelt. The small
quantity of matte produced assayed 49 p.c. copper,
but a leaky furnace prevented a continuous run. The
mine reports speak hopefully of prospects, and the
smelting practice should be a great improvement on
the methods pursued in the ’6o’s. Generally speak-
ing, it is difficult to run a high-grade matte in one
fusion from low-grade ore, for it involves such a
high temperature that the furnace rapidly deteri-
orates.
* * * *
For the first three months or this year
Charters Towers crushed 38,224 tons for £114,497.
Of this £55,697 was from the mills, £28,723 from
cyanide plants, and £30,077 from smelters. Charters
Towers has thus to thank the chemist and metallur-
gist for what prosperity is left it. Day Dawn crush-
ings are the best on the field. Mills’ United averaged
£2 19s. 2d., New Brilliant Freehold £4 9s. 4d., and
East Mexican £5 Is. Bd. Brilliant Extended is ob-
taining better returns from its cyanide plant, and a
better price for its concentrates. It is the only show
on the Towers that gives a plain statement of
monthly profit or loss. Brilliant Deeps is doing some
interesting prospecting in deep levels. Paid-up shares
in this concern rarely come into the market; nearly
all the dealing is in contributing, which are at very
low prices in consequence of calls.
Hampden, Cloneurry (Q.), has been
flooded out. Five inches of rain fell in two hours.
The co.’s dam burst, and the water rushed into No.
2 shaft; the men underground had a narrow escape.
On top a man was swept away and drowned. Things
have, however, been got pretty right again, after
being hung up for a fortnight.
Acorn gold mine is a neighbor of the Oaks,
Oaks rush (Q.), and started its crosscut from the
latter’s boundary. The crosscut cut a formation 7ft.
wide. Assays show 15dwt., and driving is being con-
tinued in the hope of cutting the main ore channel.
These big formations, with leaders through them,
will certainly prove patchy, but it is highly probable
that some payable yields will be got.
The result of the inquiry into alleged
tin-stealing at the Great Northern battery at Her-
berton (Q.) is, as was expected, “not proven.” The
inquiry caused the only stir noticeable for a long
time in stagnant Herberton; and now it’s over, the
“sleepy hollow” will resume its slumbers. A new
broom with a strong hand behind it would, perhaps,
shake the place up; on the other hand, it- might
take the Day of Judgment to do it.
Another of the North Queensland tin
cos., which had a big name, has come to grief.
The Lancelot, out near Herberton, lias closed down.
The co. was lately boring with a diamond driU in
search of the elusive tin, and failed to get it.
Numerous “experts” have been imported from Ger-
many, but they couldn’t make the show pay. The
final collapse was, however, sudden and dramatic.
The German owners put a considerable amount of
cold cash into the affair; and there are people—-
tin experts, too —who say that they might have had
better chances if they had also put in some practical
experience.
Metallurgical works are nowadays adding
largely to the Australian output of gold. Mt. Mor-
gan, Great Cobar, Mt. Lyell, Mt. Elliott, Great Fitz-
roy and Mt. Perry are all helping. In Queensland
nearly one-third of the gold production comes from
copper smelters, and N.S.W. is on the same track.
Mt. Morgan’s monthly production of over 7000 oz. of
gold from copper ore is the largest, but Great Cobar,
which has altered its smelting practice, turns out
from 2000 oz. to 3000 oz. per month. Mt. Elliott (Q.)
is at present producing IOOOoz. per month from
copper ore that contains sdwt. gold per ton. That,
however, is exceptionally rich gold stuff. At Mt.
Lyell the crude ore smelted doesn’t average 15
grains per ton all round; yet the furnaces recover
10,000 oz. of gold and 600,0000 z. of silver for 12
months’ work. Both Mt. Morgan and Mt. Lyell, as
well as several smaller producers, have their gold
and copper separated at the Electrolytic Refining
Co.’s works at Port Kembla,.and that co.’s brand of
copper now ranks with the finest in the world.
* * * *
Blair Atliol colliery, near Clermont (Q.),
sent away 9877 tons of coal for March. The aver-
age is about 8000 tons per month, and the co. within
these limits is doing a profitable trade, and has de-
clared its second dividend. It is lucky in having
a thick, easily-worked seam at a shallow depth;
other mines in the locality have to sink through
hard basalt and sandstone, and then battle with a
heavy flow of water. Electric coal cutters are to be
introduced, though even without them the output
per man is wonderfully good. #
In 1910 the Rand gold output exceeded
£30,000,000, or approximately 34 p.c. of the world’s
total production. Working costs amounted to
£19,000,000, or 62 p.c. of the output. Dividends
were £8,876,085, or 29 p.c. Interest on loans,
capital expenditure, etc., made up the balance.
The stuff treated was .24,432,541 tons, and the
duty per stamp 7.19 tons.
* * * *
“Snell”: Just now, when freedom of contract is a
sacred shibboleth of the Employers’ Federation, it is
interesting to note how the one considerable employ-
ing body that could uphold the gorgeous principle in
its entirety behaves towards its employee. I know
one young man in a bank, who, about 18 months
ago, wanted to marry a nice girl. The bank let him
go so far as the purchase of a house and furniture,
and then said it couldn’t hear of his marrying. The
clerk secured a substantial guarantee that he would
not rob the bank to support his wife and family,
and the financial institution’s reply was the, im-
mediate removal of the clerk to the Backblocks.
That young man is still unmarried. It would appear
that, in the eyes of the lordly employing class, free-
dom of contract is off when the employee wants to
contract marriage. #
The mine manager’s report was being
read at the directors’ meeting. “Driving is now
slower,” it stated, “as we have lost the dig.” The
newly-appointed director straightened himself. “Dis-
graceful : I call it,” he said, indignantly. “I think
he should be written to and cautioned to be more
careful of the company’s property in future.” It
was. another director who was inspecting the mine
one day, and some feet of clean, smooth wall met
his eagle eye. Turning to the men, he said, in
his most grandiose manner: “Men, it’s sheer waste
of time to trim the rock off like that. Later on we
will have it all covered up with timber. No need
whatever to trim it. Don’t do it any more.”
“Leo Lear” : In the course of some defamatory
remarks on the mono-rail and my advocacy thereof,
“Puffing Billy” (B. 27./10/’10) arrives at various
conclusions that need investigation. Brennan
claimed for his mono-rail a pace up to 120 miles
per hour, cheaper construction on account of his
train being able to mount steep grades and get
round sharp curves, and lower working expenses;
and he produced a full-sized car to prove his con-
tentions. “Puffing Billy” raises no objection
to the claim as to speed so that passes as
undebatable. Grades are, as he remarks, largely a
matter of power. Now, power is a matter of cost;
find a cheaper power than steam, and your working
expenses are reduced. Brennan’s loco, is not a
steam-driven contrivance. There being less friction
on one rail than on two, the same power would pull
a heavier load up a hill on the single rail. Also,
going at twice the speed on a down grade, the im-
petus would carry the cars half as far again up the
next one. Each vehicle would not have separate
motors to drive its gyroscopes; they would all be
connected with the main driving-power, and thus
save the space and weight that “P. 8.” says would
eat up profits. The sum of the rolling-stock would
not cost more than the present-day outfit, as, with
the high speed, only half as much would be needed
to do the same work. As to curves, these are built
into a line for the purpose of saving cost of con-
struction, by getting round spurs and into gullies
that would otherwise require cuttings and tunnels,
banks and bridges. A line on a three-chain radius
curve can obviously hug the contour of the land-
scape closer than one on a 10-chain radius. In
Victoria, even with the narrow-gauge two-lines,
there are three-chain radius curves, and has anyone
heard of Weary’s narrow-gauge trucks taking a short
cut? As to stability of trains, I have pointed out
that the gyroscopes continue running after the
power is shut off ; besides, supports fall automati-
cally from the carriages when they come to a halt.
The heaviest part of the modern train is the en-
gine ; one belonging to the Erie railway turns the
scales at 205 tons. So rails are manufactured to
carry the engines, and not the goods, etc., behind
them. An electric mono-loco, of the above tonnage
would pull the planet off its axis, and would be
obviously unnecessary. So the single rail would not
have to be as heavy as the combined weight of two
rails. People object to the mono-rail, and say it
will not replace the existing types, because it is
a novelty. The world has heard the same about
steamships, mono-planes and almost every inven-
tion. Unless, therefore, some better objections are
forthcoming, I will continue to uphold the mono-
rail for works such as our transcontinentals. The
loss of a few hours along these lines may some day
mean the extinction of the earth’s one white con-
tinent.

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Willley.” It is continuous , and
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
moans of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2
