Hurry up !
People want to get home to
their tea. If I’d as much time
as you seem to have to daudle
about, I wouldn’t stand here;
I’d go right away and get a
cup of Robur tea—that’s what
I’d do. “Come, move along
there please!”
A fool once said “a police-
man’s life is not a happy one”
-—that fellow had some sense,
any way, for it isn’t much of
a life except perhaps when
he’s off duty at home playing
with the youngsters.
“'Move on!” “Move on,
please!” What are you looking
for? A place to buy a pound
of tea to take home? Try at
the next grocer’s shop,
and ask for Robur No. i
h
Grade; if
you’ve not
tried it I’m
sure you’ll
like it, and
say it’s the
best tea you
ever tasted for
the money.
The Policeman
QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
A
WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, May 1, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The dead drop from the excitement of
Race week is shadowed by the ghosts of
bills which haunt the fashionable woman in
her morning-tea reverie. The smart cos-
tumiere wonders when she is going to be
paid. Ladies who cheerfully ordered a
hundred pounds’ worth of frocks apiece, won-
der how she is going to be paid. Husbands,
during the present meditation amongst the
tombs of departed junketings, figure as “mean
brutes.” The cold fact that, till he gets
his breath, the professional man with six or
seven hundred a year can’t pay £3O or £4O
for each race week frock is by the way.
The vogue of the evening wedding plunged
Randwick into an epidemic of uniforms last
week. Bridegroom, Dr. James Dick, as
Major of the Australian Army Medical
Corps, had the unchallenged right to sum-
mon to his assistance an armed array of
First Australian Infantry. These warriors,
in conjunction with a galaxy of feminine
charms, brought the wedding guests up to a
grand total of over 700. Governors, million-
aires, etc., will have to hire the Domain, and
switch on a few thousand troops, if they hope
to eclipse the gorgeous goings-on of the
nresent-day Sydney citizen when he enters
for the matrimonial stakes. The bride,
eldest daughter of Mr. and Mrs. George
Wall, of Randwick, was supported in her
triumphal procession to the altar by six
maids.
The smart American woman, in one of
her dress-hunting expeditions to Paris, dis-
covered the witchery of evening garb, allied
to the fascination of large, fluffy, feathery
hats. Australian women, who are getting
themselves talked about for their magnificent
plumage, soon grasped the true inwardness
of the French chapeau garnish as a decora-
tive top to a bare neck.
The Burrowes-Nott orange-blossom display,
in St. Philip’s Church, occurred on the same
evening as Dr. Dick’s excuse for feasting
the Australian infantry. The picturesque
pageant of artificial light, glittering evening
gowns inlaid with (more or less) real gems,
picture, hats, and other emblems of female
war-paint, has knocked the afternoon cere-
mony into a mid-Victorian memory. Ash-
lield,.the home-nest of Miss Mary Nott, who
married Mr. Percy Burrowes, of Melbourne,
under such highly ornamental circumstances,
was defrauded of its local right to hang over
its front fence and criticise the wedding-
guest finery. The party foregathered after
the ceremony at Petty’s Hotel.
The small boy, dressed up like a glorified
rag doll, is one of the minor offences of the
fashionable wedding. Every other bride
now finds it necessary to have an attendant
imp of this white-satin species to hold up
the tail of her skirt. He staggers under a
heavy-weight plumed hat, and figures as “a
page” in social reports. No normal boy of
mud-pie instincts would willingly encase his
nether limbs in white satin breeches. The
abnormal boy, with a feminine love for frip-
pery, ought to be turned loose in a boys’
school, and have the ostrich-feather obses-
sion hand-beaten into something likely to be
more useful to his country.
Hilda, the one daughter of chairman A.
W. Meeks, of the A.M.P. Society and the
Gibbs-Bright firm, became Mrs. Mars Gerald
Cater at St. Mark’s on Wednesday evening.
The Horderns, the Sees and the Meeks fami-
lies are joined by matrimonial links, and
there was a large gathering of this clan,
whose tartan is mostly gold.
The current exhibition at the Town Hall
gives 114 answer's to the riddle of the cen-
tury: “What shall we do with our girls?”
The opening ceremony last week is worth
resuscitating for a moment. Lady Dudley’s
sad voice and wet handkerchief speech was
a swan song of farewell to Australian girls.
Archbishop Wright’s rather smug utterance
dragged in the Bishop of London by the
scruff of his clerical collar; but the work
done by organising sec. Grace Burrows and
the indefatigable young persons who have
been vigorously hammering their thumbs
and chopping tissue-paper into almond
blossom for months, was buried in Wright-
eous silence. The Duchess of Bedford, who
has done as much for the Girls’ Exhibition
as the Gulf of Carpentaria, was, however,
served up as grace before meat.
At the same assembly the Admirable King-
Ilall made a bold descent on the hackneyed
lines, “When pain and anguish,” etc., and
cheerfully misquoted till a watery smile
gave a rainbow effect to a dull platform
gathering. The Anarchist suggests that a
bluejacket who can dance a sailor’s horn-
pipe would be a livelier performer at these
proceedings than an Admiral who can’t make
a speech.
As for the exhibition itself, Mrs. Sophie
Corrie, rising 77 (or thereabouts), is one .of
the demonstrating girls. In shameless de-
fiance of the Psalmist’s time-limit, she goes
on showing the world how to preserve and
bottle fruit. The Civil Ambulance nurses
are on hand, prepared to throw cold water
on hysterics or put a new patch on the tem-
per of any choleric male visitor who feels
that 114 demonstrations are too much for his
nerves.
Lady Dudley lost her heart to an engag-
ing puppy of the bulldog persuasion. “Mary
Gibbs” is her name, and Mrs. Griffin is her
fond owner. When the vice-regal train, with
its attendant A.D.G.’s, was torn away
from the worship of Mary’s front teeth,
her Ex. sent back to ask if the little bulless
were for sale. The owner reluctantly ad-
mitted she wouldn’t part with Mary’s
wrinkled brow for the King’s profile on gold
coins. Queer, how these ugly, lovable brutes
get their teeth into the affections of women
fanciers of dog-flesh!
In the Town Hall basement there is cook-
ing by electricity. A feminine critic said
she thought the puff-paste looked too brown
on one side. Her male escort refused to
pass an opinion, unless he was permitted to
go more deeply into the subject in dispute.
“It’s the taste I go by,” said this Solomon.
Upstairs Mrs. Bennett is also baking by
electricity. Human joints are liable to
get some of their misery cooked out of them
when they lend themselves to this enterpris-
ing dame, who roasts rheumatism on scien-
tific lines.
Mrs. Scott, from Willoughby part,
mothers the Ladies’ Poultry Club section.
She dotes on White Orpingtons. Every
feather of these prize fowls is alleged to
have stood on end with disgust the other day.
A voice from another Exhibition section hur-
riedly demanded a Poultry Club bird to
help in a cooking-by-electricity show. When
the ladies of the Fowl Fancier demonstra-
tion recovered from the shock, they sent an
ambassador with a chilling reply. “The
lady who wanted a fowl to bake could have
it. The price was three guineas!”
Lady Dudley has waved her wand, and
changed the annual Hospice Ball into a con-
cert. H. N. Southwell has ear-marked him-
self for the honorary struggle of seeing the
programme through to an all-star finish. The
Sacred Heart Hospice Ball has been as much
a regular fixture as spring onions, or July
sheep sales. It’s likely to need a good
hard Government House push to tame a
favorite ball into an entertainment that won’t
sprout the promise of a sit-down supper in
one corner of its tickets. June 10 will be
the occasion.
Minister for Education Beeby gave his
official blessing to the Photographic Show,
and opened it with an explanatory speech.
The Royal Art Society’s rooms are respon-
sible for housing these exploits with cameras.
Mrs. G. A. Barton, Miss Aitchison and Miss
Agnes Warburg seemed to be the sole sur-
vivors of their sex in this display of mascu-
line merit. Woman is either too busy an-
nexing the earth or washing up the dishes
to. go in for a photographic competition.
Miss May Moore and Miss Fletcher are mak-
ing headway with their portrait studies in
this city, but in a general sense the camera
and the girl are not on terms of frantic
friendship. Whether the “messy” business
of mixing themselves up with chemicals has
anything to do with this antagonism I can’t
pretend to know. But the determination of
Woman to keep off the photographic grass
is one of the unexpected phases of the show.
F. A. Joyner (S.A.) snares the fancy
with the brilliant sunshine he gets into his
girl and daffodil picture. He’s prone to pose
a well-grown young woman in rustic garb
before his camera, and fill her arms with
flowers or other rural luggage. He loads
his second-best girl with a basket of fruit,
and calls her “Sunjlight.” J. E. Purves
(N.S.W.) faces the domestic problem with
a flat-iron. The girl, labelled “Ironing,” and
the lady with the rolling-pin and paste-
board, catalogued “Baking,” excite a respon-
sive sigh in the merely feminine observer.
These home-grown industries are not so cool
and comfortable as the male photographer
would have you believe, my masters !
The two Sydney girls, Mirrie Solomon and
Jessie Elphingstone, who gave a successful
concert last week, are both pupils of veteran
Kretsclnnann, persistent hunter for musical
prodigies. Kretschmann is, and has been,
the most incurable optimist in sharps and
young semiquavers this city contains.
Some of his juvenile swans may have grown
into very ordinary geese. But he has
scratched in unlikely soil often enough, and
produced clever musical chickens. Two
centuries ago he would have been sent to
join the Celestial Choir in a stained-glass
window —surrounded by grateful Infant Phe-
nomena blowing fat trumpets into his whis-
kers.
Autumn and spring in this city generally
come in with a rush of picture-shows, to
back up the materialistic joys of new mil-
linery. Miss Ethel Stephens will hold an
exhibition of her personal efforts to get a
likeness in oil-painting, and reproduce the
glory of the flower garden, this week. Miss
Stephens’s studio, Kirketon-road, Darling-
hurst, will, therefore, hold art critics (a
much-abused species) and amateurs, friends,
acquaintances, etc., galore; for the Stephens
family, as old residents, know half the popu-
lation (more or less) of Sydney suburbs.
Sketches of Normandy peasant-life—the
aftermath of Miss Stephens’s recent scamper
across Europe—are to be included in the ex-
hibits.
Mrs. Ellis Rowan, whose flower paintings
are now on view in Aldenhoven’s gallery,
glided like a slim silhouette through an oc-
casional Easter festivity. She telils a quaint
story of a Queensland pitcher-plant, which
has an uncanny thirst on teetotal ilines. Says
Mrs. Rowan: “I was once sitting near the
edge of a stream in a northern Queensland
jungle, sketching the pitcher-plant, when I
thought it moved slightly. I watched it
closely, for the movement was almost im-
perceptible. Slowly—very slowly—it bent to-
wards the water’s edge, and gradually dipped
its pitcher-shaped leaf till it was full of
water. Then, with the same slow move-
ment, it regained its former upright position.
I was afraid to tell this incident to any but
my own intimate friends, for a long time,
because it sounded like a fairy tale. But
about two years afterwards, I read the ex-
perience of a South American naturalist
who saw the pitcher-plant do exactly the
same thing on the banks of a stream in
Brazil. After reading that, I felt I might
mention the water-drinking habits of the
pitcher-plant without being looked on as a
botanical visionary.”
It comes as a cold chill to the
better feelings of those who can only
chew the Coronation cud afar off, that
peers’ coronets are made of silver gilt.
This news trots in double harness with the
appalling statement that peeresses will be
entirely responsible for the good behavior
of their velvet robes at George’s June party.
These, and other bald facts connected with
the Royal anointing, snatch the veil of illu-
sion from the divinity that no longer hedges
kings. There is consolation in this thought.
Though peers’ head-pieces are only silver-
gilt, the King’s breeches are to be built of
pure silk, to match his illustrious stockings.
Wasn’t it Earl Spencer’s ancestor who
set the fashion in men]s “dicky-bird” coats,
by having his coat-Ails burnt off as he
warmed his back, in characteristic Cold-land
fashion, on the domestic hearth? This sim-
mers in my mind re the remarks Chamber-
lain Spencer is now hurling, at the hobble-
skirt, which he will not allow to be worn at
Court functions.
The recent O’Connor-Mort marriage has a
special interest to the digger in N.S.W.
pioneer lore. Morts, Laidleys and Harnetts
have taken a useful grip of the soil and
trade in this State since its earlier teething
troubles. Bodalla cheese revolves round
the enterprise of the dairying branch of the
Mort family. Merchandise rears a solid
front before the name of Laidley. Mrs. R.
E. O’Connor, the bridegroom’s mother, is
very tali and broad-chested. The under-
sized woman who gets alongside Mrs. u’Con-
nor at a crowded social corroboree, looks like
an ant negotiating a pyramid. Bride Nea
Mort was secretary to the Rose Bay Golf
Club, a golfer of note, and amazingly popu-
lar with her own sex—the highest certificate
of merit this world wots of.
To genial “Laurie” Harnett belongs the
primary credit of “discovering” Kosciusko as
a potential tourist resort. He incited pho-
tographer Kerry, in February, 1895, to climb
with him up Kosciusko’s bald old head,
plank down his camera, and, during a week’s
camp, photograph every available attrac-
tion. Mr. Kerry admits he was an easy
victim to the fascination of Snowy parts, as
he hailed from the Monaro tableland in the
days when Ryries, Harnetts, Cosgroves and
Campbells were pioneering in wool. “We
took with us,” ruminates the enthusiastic
Kerry, “as guide, Spencer, an old identity of
the Snowy. He used to range with cattle
round about the mountain in summer, and
knew every track. Mr. Harnett loved the
place, and saw its possibilities as a great
tourist resort when Sydney people just knew
it was somewhere on the N.S.W. map.”
From that small beginning, followed by a
Kerry camera descent on Kiandra, then an
isolated community in winter, has evolved
the snug tourist hotel at Kosciusko. This
building, according to the optimistic Hol-
man, is liable to feel new wings sprouting
at any moment.
Akin Higgins, who captains the wealthy
American, tourist team, now spreading their
golden wings for flight across this State,
turns out to be an Australian. Bill Wattle,
who gives me this news, says that Captain
Higgins was the first general-manager for
Cook and Son in Australia.
Mrs. South and Mrs. Smith, the silk-
worm ladies, who come from Queensland,*
are surrounded by inquiring hats. Says
Mrs. South: “The Queensland Government
has been very good to us. It advanced us
£5O, out of which it bought us a machine
costing £2O. This left us about £3O in
hand. It lent us the Tourist Bureau win-
dow in Brisbane to show our silk, and
in countless small ways was kind to us. All
this has been an immense help in getting
Queenslanders interested in work which
means good pocket money for women and
girls.”
Passing through Sydney this week are
Miss Mildred Manifold and brother Bertie,
of the woolly W. T. Manifold family of
Western Victoria. She and her brother are
just back from the land of the early-rising
Jap.
The woolly house of Goldsbrougli, Mort
and Co., the pioneers among Australian com-
panies which finance the budding squatter-
and sometimes also the squatter who is in
full bloom—said good-bye to its chief inspec-
tor the other day. W. E. Moore is sup-
posed to be personally acquainted with more
sheep, and to be on terms of intimacy with
more bullocks, station tanks and boundary
fences, than anybody else in N. S. Wales.
It is 20 years since he began to be the chief
guide, philosopher, etc., of the big firm’s
squatting clients; and a dinner at Paris
House, an address from the staff, and a bulky
cheque from the firm, with other toothsome
trifles, marked the cutting of the old ties.
The knowledgeable and popular man fastens
his own signboard up at Forbes.
Lord Dick Nevill will return to Australia
after the Coronation ceremonies with the
Melba Opera Company. He is a member of
the Opera Syndicate, as well as a personal
friend of Melba’s, and is expected to show
Australians how T grand opera is run, on the
social side, in Old World centres.
The “Francie” Josephs, whose only
daughter Beryl has just been made a Lady
of Jerusalem, or something which sounds
tremendous, were well known in Sydney 20
years ago. Fairwater, the Double Bay man-
sion which the J. O. Fairfaxes now inhabit,
was built by Mr. Joseph in the boom days.
For a decade or more he has been a stock-
broker in London. A sister is married to
Mr. Forbes Angus, of Sydney.
Miss Nona Hay, one of the pretty sisters
of Mrs. Philip Morton, was operated on for
appendicitis at Lewisham Hospital last week.
She is doing well.
Mrs. (Essie Jenyns) Wood) is taking a
hasty look-in at things Australian before
scampering back to her Cold Land moorings.
David Graham Phillips, the American
writer, met a tragic death at the hands of a
man who thought his sister had figured as
“Margaret” in “The Fashionable Adventures
of Joshua Craig.” Phillips dipped his pen
in gall on the feminist question. His last
novel, “The Husband’s Story,” is so bitter
in its denunciation of American women that
one New York critic almost wonders that
the fatal shot wasn’t fired by a woman. Says
Phillips: “The cruellest indictment of the
intellect of woman is the crude, archaic,
futile and unimaginative way in which is
carried on the part of life that is woman’s
peculiar work ... It may be that woman
will some day develop another and higher
sphere for herself. But first she would do
well to fill the sphere she now rattles round
in, like one dry pea in a. 10-gallon can.”
This is almost up to the standard of the
literary dead-cats that we met in that half-
forgotten screed, “Lovely Woman.”
As I close this letter, an unreliable rumor
comes along from the Maheno. It affirms
that Lady Dudley is booked as a passenger
for Auckland, and will have left Sydney be-
fore this reaches you. At date of writing,
this item falls with an unexpected thud. Is
this the inner circumstance which inspired
Lady Dudley’s melancholy note of farewell
when she opened the Girls’ Exhibition?
The Tom Hughes combination, now tour-
ing Europe, sends an informative line to a
friend. Rome, Florence, Buda Pesth and
Vienna form part of their descent into an-
cient history, combined with modern drain-
age. Buda Pesth and Vienna are accused
of being right up to next week in their pro-
gressive municipal schemes for beautifying
plain parts. In Vienna, Mrs. O’Gorman
Hughes joins the Tom Hughes’s personally-
conducted tour. When Mrs. Tom was
Mayoress of this city, her popularity stood
out as a monumental proof of the all-wool,
everlasting wear of “Tact-tact-tact.”
Yours, with a broad nib,
VANDORIAN.

Progress must have its way. And perhaps
the result of the Referendum means that this
country refuses to be stampeded too furiously
even along the right track. Anyhow, if the
Referendum had been as to Progress’s wisest
way, there would have been a unanimous
Yes in favor of the wise way inspired by
that spirit of wisdom, D.C.L., the Whisky of
the Wise.*

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, Mat 1, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie, —
Sassiety women had never worked so hard at a
poll in their lives as they did before the Referen-
dum. Terror of some fearful social upheaval
drove them out; they had been told that hubby’s
best investments were at stake; and every motor
in Toorak and St. Kilda was spinning about
dragging votes along by the hair of the head. In
an ordinary election there are a few personal
friends of the candidates who can be roused to
serious work ; but the moneyed women were got
to believe that this shoe would pinch all of them.
Practically the last of Governor Carmichael’s
rovings in the country took him to the well-to-do
woolly country round about Mansfield. Mrs.
Bon, of Bonnie Doon, the widow of one of the
earliest squatters in the district, was hostess-in-
chief. The Bons arrived on the scene when the
aboriginals were numerous and the air was full of
boomerangs. Mrs. Bon has been a battler for the
rights of our dispossessed black brother for many
years, and is the only woman on the Board that
is supposed to protect the aborigines. She was a
friend of the late King Barak, a really noble
savage and chief of the Yarra tribe, whose domain
extended from the city curve of Melbourne’s over-
worked river to beyond Heidelberg. Melbourne
stands in the centre of dead-and-gone Barak’s
kingdom ; yet in all the statue-studded city there
is no graven image of the old monarch. Barak
had one son called David ; but David died while
still a child, and the succession petered out.
His Ex. Carmichael, while at Bonnie Doon,
visited the Presbyterian church built by Mrs. Bon
wherein are carefully preserved some furnishings
which represent local church history. Some years
ago in Melbourne, tho St. Vincent’s Convent
nuns bought the piece of land on Eastern Hill
opposite the Government Printing Office where
stood the first Presbyterian church. The edifice
contained a richly-carved pulpit from which the
first Presbyterian minister in Melbourne had
preached. On the sale of the property, the Mother
Superior of St. Vincent’s generously presented tho
whole of the valuable furnishings, pulpit and
pews to the kindly old colonist. Mrs. Bon
carried the treasure off to her own corner of the
State, and placed them in the church she had had
built. The building was just completed when
Queen Victoria died, and the first religious
ceremony held there was a memorial service.
At the City of Melbourne stall in the Alfred
Hospital Bazaar, Mayoress Davey has bagged a
fine lot of feathers for the undoing of the buyer.
Amy Castles put on her most fascinating frock,
and sold things on Saturday ; she made an excel-
lent auctioneer. On Thursday afternoon Arca-
dians helped the sale. Miss Gilliam, and
Miss Cavanagh, and Miss O’Connor, and
Charlie Stone beat up the buyers. Mrs.
Brennan was also helped by Miss Gilliam
and other Arcadians at a musical tea-party of
crushing social tonnage. The affair was so big
that it was driven even unto the upper-upper
room of the Vienna. A platform raised
in the big bay windows held the minstrels.
Miss Winifred O’Connor “regretted”—had a
“ throat ” —and her understudy, bright Irish-
Maorilander Florence Quinn, sang instead. Ger-
trude Gilliam chirped through song and dance
with Charlie Stone, and Miss Cavanagh sang
drolleries.
A child at the piano made even Sassiety tea-
drinkers stop chattering at this party. Fedora,
the daughter of Senator St. Ledger, it was—a
child with a beautiful face and an artistic brain.
The April soiree of the Alliance Frangaise was
distinguished by the farewell visit of the Car-
michael lady and the presentation of the decora-
tions conferred upon Mrs. Gay and Mrs. Aarons.
In the absence of the French Consul, John
Madden conferred the ribbons and threw the
appropriate flowers at their feet. Mrs. Gay wore
an apricot-colored gown, and Mrs. Aarons was in
all white. The French decoration consists of a
little bow of purple ribbon about the size of a
thumb-nail, such as distinguished military men
wear on their coat lapels. A large parchment
roll tied with tricolor ribbon accompanies each
bow; but you are not supposed to wear the
parchment, though if run over by a sufficient
number of carts it would make a very fashion-
able hat. The Alliance presented each
lady with two curved silver palm-leaves set
in diamonds. Mrs. Masseran, in a brilliant black
dress, received Lady Carmichael, who wore a
lovely blue and silver cloud, and had niece Ena
with her in a purple cloak. Captain Vaux was in
attendance; but his Ex was unable to attend,
and Mrs. Madden was too ill to be present.
Music and the French drama enlivened the pro-
ceedings, as usual ; and after coffee and cakes, the
guests strolled out to see how the Referenda
voting boards were behaving.
An adjacent man mentioned that he had seen
the little bow conferred on the Emperor of Brazil;
whereupon I immediately demanded further par-
ticulars. It appears that Brazil had an Emperor
about 16 years ago who was not particularly
interested in his job. He was a keen student and
a great scientist, and when people called round on
business he was generally shut up in his labora-
tory watching an experiment. He found his
herd of subjects rather a bore; so, although
he was a lovable monarch, the nation decided
to become a republic. The Emperor was
given a gentle hint, and his attaches conducted
him quietly to Paris. Then the harmless revolu-
tion was started, and concluded without anyone
being hurt. His Majesty contentedly set-to in
Paris to hunt among the archives and spend his
time in the way he liked best; and incidentally
he was rewarded with the little purple bow. It
is an interesting story, which sets one musing on
the advantage or otherwise of being bossed by
hereditary monarchs. Our own royalties and all
their relations are so smothered in butter and for-
a-good-child stories that their humanity is quite
lost. How many of them are like his Majesty of
Brazil, whose position was all the more tolerable
because his ex-subjects made him a handsome
annual allowance ?
Some fortunate friends of mine possess an aged
female retainer who has grafted herself on to the
family tree, and would rather die than give
notice. She is handier with the broom than
the pen ; so when the census papers came along,
the head of the house had to fill in her card. He
left the age line till the very last, then asked in an
apologetic way how many years he should put
down. “Oh, anything over 30,” said the coy
maid of antiquity.
Examples of the art of Charlie Wheeler are
now enjoying the air at Melbourne Guild Hall.
The canvases are mostly of a minor character,
but two or three are the more definite expressions
of a larger energy. Of those particular efforts
one prefers the study of a sweet-looking wonian
at a toilet table. The work is charming, for it is
such a triumph in stage-managed effects. Even
a sort of lamp upon the table seems a self-
conscious “super ”in the tout ensemble. Others
of Wheeler’s bigger items are just as accurately
plotted and arranged. What arrests one strongly
is his taste for the mirror as a pictorial aid. In three
or four works he poses a female before a “glass,”
and paints her reflection to make her feel she is
among relations. In one boudoir study he poses a
girlish nude, and holds the mirror up to Nature
without advertising the latter to any extent. In
another he leaves an elderly lady playing with a
bowl of flowers, no doubt to keep her mind
from brooding on the color in her dress. Also, he
displays pencil drawings, bits of pastel, and
about a mile of landscape paintings. The last
include pieces of the Yarra seen through a dirty
morning or clearer noon ; slices of the country
bashfully hiding in the painter’s atmosphere ; and
a couple of things wherein the artist tries to secure
an effect of frivolity with some nocturnal haze
and paper lanterns.
By the time this letter is in type Alice-sit-by-
the-fire will have left her cosy lounge and retired.
The Melbourne University Dramatic Club selected
J. M. Barrie’s quaintly-named “Page from a
Girl’s Diary,” in three acts, for its second public
performance at the Turn Verein Hall. Professor
Masson, who is a personal friend of Barrie’s,
called on the author during a recent visit to the
Cold Country, and, seeing the MS., secured his
permission to produce the play here. He had to
take a solemn vow that it would be carefully
guarded and promptly returned. Barrie, by the
way, will not allow his plays to be published.
Ellen Terry was the original Alice ; and our Mrs.
Brough would suit the part admirably.
The wedding of Dr. Gerald Sheahan and
Dorothy, daughter of Micky Dawson, of Sher-
wood Park, Warrnambool, drew a joyous train of
guests to St. Pat’s Cathedral. The very young
bride—she is not yet 18 —made her bridegroom, a
young man on the other side of 30, look quite
aged. Lawyer Tom Nolan, who has been best
man to no less than 14 bridegrooms, helped con-
siderably in the management of the function, as
Fred Sheahan, the brother of the bridegroom and
holder of the responsible post, was a novice at the
business. The one bridesmaid was Dr. Sheahan’s
sister Tot, and three small Dawson nieces carried
the billowy train. From the cathedral the party
fled across the city to Menzies’, where the break-
fast waited. Next day the pair left by the Morea
for a round-the-world honeymoon.
A fashionably-squashy velvet hat has been
showing above a familiar bright face. Amy
Castles has just arrived in Melbourne. She is
resting after a long tour—that is to say, she is
hard at work on opera study. And all through
her Maoriland campaign she has been working
away at Italian and German and other wild
beasts.
Rupert Clarke and his new bride, when last
heard* of, had looked at Monte Carlo and Nice ;
and both places had retaliated by looking admir-
ingly at the lady’s dazzling costumes.
The wedding of Harry Musgrove, nephew of
George, to Mrs. Prytz, happened last Wednesday,
The young widow, who is a granddaughter of
Captain Rowan, of Brighton, was Lyn Hills prior
to her first tragically brief marriage. Mr. and
Mrs. Musgrove have gone to Sydney, where they
are to live.
On Thursday, Ethel Henderson, the physical
culturist, said “ I will ” when she was asked if
she would have Ronald Buchan, son of Collins-
street auctioneer John Buchan. The bride took
up the foils relinquished by Mrs. Hargreaves
when that lady deserted the gym. for matrimony.
Now there is a chance for another woman to don
the fetching costume of the fencing mistress.
The latest girl to exhibit an engagement half-
hoop of diamonds is Jane Webb, daughter of the
fine old doctor who spent his life hunting for a
cancer cure. She will marry Ernest Pohlman, a
Toorak young man, a few weeks hence.
The Chester Manifolds are going to give a
gorgeous ball at Warrnambool to celebrate the
first official plunge into Society of their daughter,
fair-haired Marian.
The wedding happened last Monday week, and
the church was smothered for the occasion in
daisies. Bert Burton was the happy man who
claimed the white-clad and daisy-crowned bride.
Two bridesmaids, with red-brown hair, wore white
gowns that shaded into autumn tints at the foot.
The clergyman, tired apparently of the conven-
tional blessing, loudly wished them both “ Good
luck, in the name of the Lord.” Mrs. Burton
departed for the honeymoon in a green coat and
skirt, with a dream of a green feather blowing
from her black velvet hat, and a fine muffling of
black fox furs.
Willie Kelly, who had one of the first cars in
the early days of motoring, is bent on being the
first Australian amateur airman. To this end he
has ordered a machine with which to go through
our air pockets and pursue the astonished eagle.
A line from London tells me that that vilely-
treated young Australian, Ella Caspers, is getting
back to her old place in the musical swim, and
lately has been chief vocalist at various Society
“At Homes.” When the last mail left she was
singing before Princess Louise of Schleswig-
Holstein, the Duke of Norfolk, Countess of Radnor,
and other people whose names are “ on the door ”
of the upper circle.
A lovable woman who will be mourned by many
died last week at the age of 85. Elizabeth Cham-
pion had seen Melbourne grow up, and, as a sort
of right,' she mothered all its inhabitants who
•came within her sphere. Her sons are well-known
professional and business men in Melbourne and
Ballarat, and the baby of the family, Floss
Champion, is one of our prettiest girls.
Henry Bracy, at Her Majesty’s, is engaged in
the nerve-shattering business of trying voices for
the Melba grand opera season. In his search for
a champion chorus, the experienced stage-general
finds about six suitable singers per day. And he
tries scores! Needless to add, they all try him.
The Swastika Club has evolved a literary
circle, which sits round once a month and dis-
cusses some author. Last Thursday clanged with
Kipling. During an unexpected interval, caused
by the late arrival of a Personage, Mrs. Vice-
President Moore showed unsuspected talent in
recitation, and kept the ring or combine from
becoming , bored. Later on, librarian De Soir
read a paper, and Maud Grantham sang a Kipling
song and spoke a piece. Tea was served in the
brown yoom. Widow Staughton rested gracefully
after her energetic campaigning in the sacred—or
scared —cause of No-No. The Wrixon lady
beamed amiably through the inevitable political
talk. Mrs. Squatter Davidson spread a special
table for her guests. Mrs. Watson, of Queens-
land, who was until recently Mrs. Fisher, and
Florence Quinn, the blue-eyed Maorilander who
is understudying Miss O’Connor, were among the
visiting group. Mrs. Gregory, looking impressive
in a full cape of ermine, arrived with Mrs. Barag-
wanath, thrice Mayoress of South Melbourne.
Thursday’s function was the second meeting of the
literary Swastikas. The first discussion was
dedicated to Burns, and you were expected to
come armed with a quotation even if your name
didn’t begin with Mac, M’ or Me.
Another literary society, called the X Rays, gave
a unique send-off, the other day, to one of its
members who is deserting to get married. Daisy
Price was the offender, and her sisters-in-literature
presented her with a classical fairy tale, ex-
quisitely illustrated. The joyous gathering took
the form of a fancy-dress frivol, for which the
guests were sworn not to spend more than six-
pence-ha’penny on a costume. Some managed
with hobble skirts constructed of butter muslin at
2|d. per yard, double-width; and I hear that
there were Pasha confections made of crinkly
paper. One man bought a bundle of old maga-
zines for sixpence and combined them with the
previous day’s paper. However, the committee
counted him out because of the expense of the
gum.
The marine At Home on the new Aberdeen
boat Themistocles promised to be a great success;
but Neptune—who was not invited—brewed, a
gale, which delayed the steamer’s arrival, and
postponement cards had to be sent flying to all
points of the compass. The ship 3teamed up the
bay only a few hours before the time fixed for the
revel; and, having only just concluded its fierce
argument with the sea, was not in a suitable frame
of mind to entertain visitors.
“The Merry Widow,” now nearing the end of
her widowhood at Her Majesty’s, is drawing the
crowd despite Higginson’s absence from the
cast. Florence Young, as Sonia, wears a poem of
a frock: it is of dull white crepe v and clings
closely to her fine figure ; crystal embroideries
give the right finishing touch. Although the
company is complaining of colds, no huskiness is
noticeable on the critic’s side of the footlights.
The Widow has been gyrating since 1908, and
will have something like 500 performances in
Australia and Maoriland to her credit by the end
of the present Melbourne run. There can be no
doubt about the value of treacly music after this.
A gaily twinkling star at present shining in
Melbourne Sassiety is the daughter of Chief
Justice Parker, of Westralia. She came with
father on a short visit to Melbourne, but liked it
so much that she demanded more; and now her
bonneting is compelling the Block to turn its head
to have another look. The pretty Westralian
Dolly is being mothered by one or tWo care-
fully-selected Toorak matrons.
' The Harry De Littles, lately mentioned as of
Caramut, have taken Ottington, the St. Kilda-
road mansion once tenanted by the king of bridge
players, Baron Hedestrom.
Staniforth Smith, lately resurrected from
Papuan deserts, evidently places faith in the good
old motto, “Better late than never?’ He has
just sent his friends belated Christmas wishes on
cards which had been printed before the sender
was advertised for in the lost-or-swallowed column,
Staniforth's father, who still lives in Victoria,
was naturally among those who got the overdue
good-wishes.
The Loretto Convent, at Albert Park, which has
“finished ” so many Melbourne girls, is thinking
of organising a Past Pupils’ Association. The
big public schools have their Old Boys’ reunions ;
but you don’t catch the other sex having gather-
ings of Old Girls. If they manage to get toge-
ther, it will be as Past Pupils—which is a
non-committal way of putting it. A meeting to
discuss the project iff to be held at the convent on
Sunday afternoon, May 14.
Florence Challis, singer of the historic “Old
Bull and Bush,” now selling “Sweet Lavender”
at Melbourne Opera House, has a pair of beauti-
ful feet, and is therefore fully justified in singing
a barefooted song. The management consider-
ately lays down a few yards of carpet for the
item. Florence used to fling sprays of lavender
to the audience ; but in this part of the world the
real stuff is too expensive to throw away over the
footlights. The present trip is a final pick-me-up
after a serious illness, and for that reason
she restricts herself to songs which do not require
much action. Some of her dresses are dazzling,
notably the Court Presentation gown, in which
she endeavors to rouse enthusiasm for George
No. 5. In private life Miss Challis is Mrs. Reed.
The husband is secretary at the Palladium, the
luxurious London theatre which was opened at
the end of last year.
From my Adelaide correspondent:—
The Dandies made their last bow of the season on
Saturday night. The audience was huge and vociferous,
and manager Dampier was dragged on by Loud Cheers to
say good-bye for the co. His pretty wife is slowly toiling
up the rocky path of convalescence. She’s been sadly
missed by patrons of the show.
Mrs. Powis Stewart is determined to teach us our Bard.
Under her influence the Shakspeare Theatre is distinctly
moving. T’other night Rabbi Boas lectured there con-
cerning the Bard’s views on marriage, and his learned
remarks were followed next night by an abridged version
of “Romeo and Juliet.”
We’re indulging in a perfect orgy of racing. Tatter-
sail’s occurred last Saturday, and once more the weather
authorities smiled. Result —a record crowd, in its new
autumn clothes, despite the fact that the air smacked
more of summer than of winter. We take new fashions
slowly here ; but we take them (or a caricature of them)
in the end. On Saturday quite a number of women moved
slowly and with difficulty in the garments which have
been warned off the Social course at ’Ome. Some tren-
chant male comments were almost justifiable.
The “ season’s” really come. Cards are out for tea and
gossip on the Government House lawns on May 9, and a
couple of smart “charity” balls are well under weigh.
The Empire Ball (whose proceeds are to further mis-
Sionise seamen) is billed for the barn-like Exhibition
Building on May 30, and it should be a gorgeous show.
Each set is to represent some division of the Empire,
and the air is thick with discussions of color schemes,
decorative effect and the rest. Usually in these sets man
is the stumbling block, for he arrays himself in up-to-
date evening clothes, to dance with Crusade ladies, and
can’t be brought to see that he looks more than ever like
a waiter.
There is much marrying and giving in marriage in
this village at present. The Verco-Mills wedding was a
quiet affair ; the bridegroom was Dr. Joseph Verco, who
leads the profession here. His friends had never contem-
plated his doing anything so frivolous, and his engage-
ment came as a big surprise.
There has been many a wet eye at the Children’s Hospital,
Usually May-day is given special celebration there, and
all who can go along with a gift of some sort—anythin!?
from a ha’porth of sweets to a side of beef. This year"
owing to some epidemic, the authorities, plagiarising
the absent Wallington, murmured “ Better not.” It’s a
pity, because the place does excellent work, and deserves
all the help it can get.
On Tuesday Australia’s present to H.M.S. Common-
wealth was unveiled in the Mayor’s parlor. To Mrs. ’Siah
Symon is due whatever credit there is in the small idea,
and she did most of the hard work also of executing it.’
The lady does better service as presiding genius of the
Poetry League ; in that capacity she has done much to
help on promising amateurs, and (a no less necessary
thing) to deal gently but firmly with those who’d be
obviously better employed in darning socks than wasting
good paper.
Sunday picture people are jubilant. The usual “ tech-
nical flaw” enables them to continue their godless prac-
tice of exhibiting Biblical pictures on Sunday nights—for
the present. What’ll happen when Parliament meets re-
mains to be seen, for Premier Verran is a local preacher
and has our morals at heart. It seems an open question
which is the greater evil—for damsels of 16 to parade the
streets and loiter in the squares, or for them to sit and
look at moving pictures of Moses crawling out of the
bulrushes.
Sincerely yours,
JOHANNA.

Another Severe Case of In-
digestion Cured,
Again the tonic treatment has been suc-
cessful in curing severe indigestion. Mrs.

Townsville, reports that she has recovered
from a bad attack of Indigestion. The tonic
treatment is surely the simplest and sanest
for the complaint. Indigestion occurs when
the stomach becomes too weak to digest food
and the tonic treatment aims at restoring
strength to the digestive organs. In all the
cases where the newspapers have recorded
remarkable cures of Indigestion the to^ lc
taken has been Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills.
They have been found to have excellent re-
sults in even the severest cases, as this cure
shows: —
“I enjoyed good health till about two
years since, when I began to suffer from In-
digestion,” said Mrs. Smith. “I suffered
much from acid and flatulence and pain in
the stomach, especially after eating. I felt
a great oppression about my heart. I telt
much difficulty in breathing, especially when
lying down, and frequently had to get up
out of bed and go outside so as to catch my
breath. I felt a great weight at the pit of
my stomach. I gradually lost my appetite
and became very thin. I had a smothering
feeling about my heart, and my stomach used
to swell to a great extent at times. I„ use “
to feel quite giddy in my head, and I suffered
much from want of sleep. No matter what
kind of food I ate it alwavs disagreed with
me. I had been suffering agonies for about
twelve months when I read a testimonial in
the Townsville Bulletin of a cure per-
formed by Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Phe
case described was very much like my own,
so I decided on giving them a trial and
bought a box and started taking them, one
after each meal. The first box did not do
me much good, but during the time I was
taking the second box I found a wonderful
improvement. I continued the use of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills till I found myself thor-
oughly restored to good health again. lam
now as strong and healthy as I ever was,

POLITICAL
POINTS
Now, more than ever, the prayer of the true
Laborite is shortened to “Give us this day our
daily.”
Said Sydney Evening News the day after
the taking of the Referenda
The “Noes” won yesterday by a most gratifying
majority, and with them triumphed the cause of Demo-
cracy.
The fat little journal means £ S. Democracy !
After all, there are some compensations in
the victory of the “No-Noes.” We won’t have to
hang Bruce Smith now as a revolutionary. It
would have been a shocking loss to have had to
hang Bruce Smith. He makes such a beautiful
Norman Lindsay bear.
The S. Australian “ Liberal ” Union was
quite frank about it. Its President, Yardon, was
cn route to England when the Referenda vote was
taken, and to him wired the executive :
Noes lead Australia by hundred thousand.
It put the position exactly : Noes 1, Australia 2.
THE PARTNERS.
Mr. Holman’s speech is the vindication of the
Liberals. So far from holding up his hands in holy
horror at our alleged extravagance, his party has
already increased the loan expenditure for this year
to the highest on record for this State. — Ex-Premier
Wade, on Holman’s Policy Speech.
N. S. Wales Works Minister Griffith, who
is full of ideas, has been poking into the Backblocks
lately, and has scarcely been able to move half a
mile without being asked for a railway. As the
State can’t build half the lines that are wanted,
Griffith has hatched a scheme for giving settlers
railway rebates in proportion to the road-haulage
they have to do to reach a station—lo miles, 5 per
cent. ;20 miles, 10 per cent., and so on. It is a
well-meaning scheme; but what this country
wants is not so much to get settlers to crawl back
25 or 50 miles from the railways as to make the
people who hold land right alongside railways put
it to fuller use. That 25-mile haulage, to a rail-
way station is a horrible waste, whether a man
pays for it himself or gets the State to pay for it.
Hamilton Wickes, who was the Board of
Trade Commissioner to Australia, has lectured
the Australian Chamber of Commerce in London,
the fussy body which spends its time, when it has
any time, decrying Australian Protective duties
and land legislation. Wickes laid much stress on
a statement that the Germans have annexed the
export trade in metal ores from Australia, and
have squeezed British merchants out almost
entirely. The figures were: Exports of ore to
Germany and Belgium in 1909, £1,065,682; to
United Kingdom, £38,946. Whereupon Wickes
almost whispered: “Wake up; you're losing
business.” But while it was Wickes’s job to
whisper the warning into J. Bull’s ear, it ought
to be somebodv’s job to shout it into Austra-
lia’s Whether the ore goes to Belgium or Britain
it goes out of Australia, and it is about time Aus-
tralia if it supplies the world with metal, re-
tired also to be allowed to supply it with the
labor. Just at present it is merely helping to
build up a nation that sooner or later may hit it
behind the ear with its own hardware.
Summary of Chinese produce in manifest
of steamer lately arrived at London :
5141 carcases frozen pigs, 9414 cases frozen produce,
150 cases grease, 28 cases vegetable oil, 1098 casks lard,
200 tons salmon.
John Bull gets his tucker out of queer pantries
now that his own lands are largely turned into
golf links and “game ” butcheries.
What is sauce for the goose is not sauce
for the gander. At the very moment the Sydney
papers were soundly rating Attorney-General
Hughes for breaking the City Council’s by-laws
by speaking in Moore-street and blocking the
traffic, they were smashing the same law (as they
do every election) by erecting election hoardings.
Hughes may have been wise or foolish ; but, any-
way, he didn’t block traffic to one-tenth the
extent that the hypocritical Herald and the
Telegraph did.
One of the amusing features of the llefer-
enda vote was the triumphant heading :
MELBOURNE DOMINATION REFUSED!
This appeared in one of the narrow-minded
little Sydney papers the morning the result was
announced. As a simple matter of figures, the
Victorian negative vote was the largest in the
Commonwealth ! Hence, according to the Syd-
ney howling dervish, “Melbourne,” instead of
struggling and scheming for “ domination,” re-
fused “domination ” to itself !
Said the S. M. Herald during the Refer-
enda campaign :
Federation has taught us to think as a continent. It
has broadened our old provincial views, and though
these die hard, especially with the older generations,
there is growing up a distinctly Australian sentiment in
place of the former colonial view.
Apparently the Herald is now a humorous pro-
duction. Anyway, it was in the recent contest
the chief defender of the narrowest of provincial
creeds ; and it never had one continental thought
in its leading columns, nor cared, apparently, one
continental curse for Australian sentiment.
The Argus burst out into a beautiful
scheme the other day. First of all it discovered
that the State electorate of Essendon now con-
tains 18,211 voters, Collingwood 13,526, and Carlton
13,183, while Castlemaine has 7163 and Korong
only 6656; and, as it reckoned that a redistribu-
tion of seats was coming, it got in early with the
announcement that, no matter how the population
stood, the new scheme would provide the country
with 40 seats and the metropolis with 20. This
would mean that every man in the country would
make a noise in Parliament about four times
louder than the city man. Further boiled down,
it signifies that the big landlord, having mostly
proved a curse, should be rewarded for being a
curse with special Parliamentary concessions.
Even with the Weary-Watt Government in
power, that cock won’t fight.
Premier Weary’s locum tenens, Treasurer
Willie Watt, saw that the gun was loaded, and
climbed down from his refusal to gazette the
determination of the Carters and Drivers’ Wages
Board. Acting on the advice of Chief Factories
Inspector Murphy, Watt declared certain of the
Board’s findings ultra vires. The Board, how-
ever, refused to amend them; it said . that
neither Watt nor his inspector had any locus
standi, and if it was wrong there was the Supreme
Court to go to. Whereupon Watt persuaded it
to “ alter the phraseology ” of some of its findings,
and promised an amending Factories Act next
session to legalise others. So the recent difficulty
is smoothed over, though the episode again
instances the growing tendency of State Insects
to arrogate powers that belong, or should belong,
solely to the properly appointed Courts.
“ W.R.W.,” on a prophet:—
“S.S.”(B. 27/4/’ll) asserts that “Australian Socialists
have all taken Nietzsche to their bosoms, and quote the
‘inspired German’ exuberantly.” As the writer of the
article on “Nietzsche and Jesus ’’(lnternational
Socialist, April 8), let me say that I don’t take Nietzsche
to my bosom, and I don’t know any Australian Socialist
who does. We repudiate him with the Duke of Argyle
and the Rev. Malthus. In the article, which “S.S.” has
evidently misread, I gave an impartial outline of
Nietzsche’s views, stated his opposition to Socialism, and
then said : “ Briefly, it may be said that the argument
against Socialism fails, because Socialism is not based on
sentiment. It is based on justice, and Socialists claim
that no system can stand the test of time which is not
based on justice.” I concluded by saying : “Socialism,
based as it is upon absolute justice, must in the end
triumph over both Christianity and Nietzscheism,”mean-
ing thereby, as I had attempted to show, that Chris-
tianity and Nietzscheism are both based on injustice, and
are hence anti-Socialistic. “ S.S.” says: “ Nietzsche was,
. indeed, a greater Anti-Sosh than George Reid, and I won-
der at the Socialists’ delight in him.” We, on our side,
wonder where such “delight” exists outside the fertile
imagination of “ S.S.”
State Frights seems a permanent disease
now in N. S. Wales. It began with Carruthers,
whose mission was to worry the Commonwealth
Government, snarl at it and bite it from behind.
When he saw a chance of an electioneering coup
he seized some wire-netting out of bond and
defied the Commonwealth. Instead of having
Carruthers dragged up at the Water Police Court
and fined five bob or the rising of the Court, and
ordered to pay for the constable’s uniform, the
Federal Government took the matter tragically
and sent it to the higher Courts, where Carru-
thers had his ears pulled at great expense. Then
Wade succeeded, and, being a mere monkey-
imitator of Carruthers, he went on the pin-
pricking policy, being backed up therein by the
daily press of Sydney. The precedent was estab-
lished that, in order to be a N.S.W. statesman,
you had to be chipping at the Commonwealth in
a hostile way all the time. Then came the
MacGowlman Ministry, which, for want of ideas
of its own, imitated the tactics of the Carruthers-
Wade crowd, and when it had a chance to do
mischief to the Commonwealth, did it out of the
habit that N.S.W. States-men have got into.
The other morning the Crookwell Potato
Co. (N.S.W.) wept into the Sydney papers
because it had lately been “ receiving potatoes at
.Redfern that had been eight days on the way
from Crookwell ”; and in its blind agony the
potato co. got its stupidest tuber to produce this
moan: —
If this is the best we can expect from departmentalised
State control, goodness only knows what things will be
like when the Commonwealth takes over the contract of
controlling ; the whole lot, that is, if such a calamity
should ever happen.
A less stupid spud would know what things
might be like, anyhow. For instance, the Crook-
well potatoes took 200 hours to travel 172 miles
because a vast quantity of other goods are dragged
unnecessarily over the same line, and a vast
quantity more goods are dragged unnecessary
distances over other lines, and all because the
State railways are run, in the first place, in the
interests of Sydney. If Australia controlled the
railway business, the railways would be run in the
interests of the users, and goods wouldn’t be
hauled unnecessary hundreds of miles so that one
bloated city might have a peck at them. And
that would abolish the congestion and the short-
age of railway stock, which pinched the Crook-
well Potato Co. and led it to make a fool of itself
in print.
Before the Referenda vote was taken, the
daily papers once every 24 hours told the man
who usually votes Labor that of course this wasn u
a party matter —lie could be quite sure of that
from the fact that both McGowen and Holman
were against the proposals. After the vote was
taken the same papers had the gall to ask what
the Labor party was going to do about its defeat
—which ought to be enough to open the eyes of
the Labor No-ers to the fact that a confidence
trick was played on them.
THE RIGHT STAGE AT LAST.
THE ORIGINAL SAIREY: "Ullo , ’ullo, Betsy
Age! You beginnin’ t' see ‘cm TOO, eh?"
The Referendum verdict has been given,
the railways remain wholly under State control,
and by all the rules Private Enterprise should be
perfectly happy. It isn’t, though. Two days
after the vote this message from Lithgow
(N.S.W.) got into the Sydney D.T. :
It is reported that the electrolytic works of the Great
Cobar, Ltd., are to close down, as the Railway Depart-
ment refuses to grant the concession on the carriage of
copper from Cobar to Lithgow. It is stated that the
same rate is charged to Lithgow as to Sydney.
To take the copper to Sydney means dragging it
95 miles further ; but it is part of Ma’s creed that
things ought to go to Sydney, and if they refuse
to go to Sydney they don’t deserve any considera-
tion. Johnson already has the weevily wheat of
Southern Riverina on his mind ; the potato people
say it takes eight days to rail Murphy and his
missus 200 miles ; and now there is this copper
trouble. If large concerns threaten to shut up
because of Ma’s remarkable system of rates, the
No-Noes may begin to wonder if the Common-
wealth could possibly run things worse.
As illustrating how poisonously the Syd-
ney daily press colors its news records with its
political prejudices, quote this from the S. M.
Herald (22/4/’ll) :
MINERS WON’T WASTE MONEY.
Newcastle, Thursday. —At to-day’s meeting of the
delegate Board of the' Colliery Employees’ Federation
an appeal for funds to assist in the referendum campaign
was received from the Sydney Labor Council. The
Board decided to decline with regret, as they had no
money to spend in this direction.
The Herald knows well—-nobody knows better—
the poverty, individual and corporate, of the
Newcastle miners of to-day. Yet, despite this,
and despite the fact that it is plainly sqt forth in
the telegram that the Miners’ Board declined
“ with regret,” the Herald says in big letters—
“MlNEßS WON’T WASTE MONEY,”
thus insinuating, in a way to catch the readers
eye, that it is not the workers’ poverty, but their
will, which forbids a contribution. No wonder
money is being hoarded up for a Labor daily.
Interesting, now that the dust has cleared,
to examine some of the projectiles hurled by the
Liberal dailies in the last frenzied hours before
the taking of the Referenda vote. Here is one of
Sydney Telegraph’s :
The N.S.W. Labor Ministry also means to increase the
expenditure; the South Australian Labor Ministry has
already done so. These Ministries have no knowledge of
economy. To spend wholesale they consider is to bid
or popularity. The COST OF LIVING is going steadily
un and must go higher, and they will make Aus-
tralia THE DEAREST COUNTRY IN THE WORLD TO
LIVE IN, AS WELL AS THE MOST HEAVILY TAXED.
Then, instead of urging people to vote to remove
some of the power from these Governments which
“ have no knowledge of economy,” the Liberal
press fought to retain for them all their dreadful
capacity for evil—and it succeeded. Blue ruin
should therefore now be imminent. The cost,of
living should go up like an aeroplane ; and an
avalanche of taxes may be expected any minute.
If these things happen, it will have to be remem-
bered that the “Liberal ’’ press conspired to bring
them about. If they don ’.t,,the “Liberal” press,
will have been proved once more to be a false
prophet and unworthy of attention.
There is no satisfying the Australian Fat-
man any way. Awhile back he was girding at
Labor for seeking power without responsibility,
and he gibed at Deakin for granting its demands
with: “Yes, Mr. Watson.” Now, having got
power. Labor asked for responsibility, and the
Fatman lied his hardest to stop it from getting
what it was prepared and anxious to accept.
Norman Bayles, M.L.A. for Toorak
(Vic.), has given up hope of ever be-
coming Premier. It was the Re-
ferenda campaign that did it. A
night or two before the voting, a
common gardener person attended a
meeting at which Norman was the
oratorical star. Norman had been
talking lovingly about Wages Boards,
and their impending “destruction ” if
the Referendum proposals were
passed ; whereupon the gardener per-
son spoke up and said: “I’m a
gardener. We’ve been trying to get
a Wages Board for a long time, and
we can't. How’d you advise me to
vote at the Referendum, so as to get
a Wages Board ? ” There was a
death-like silence on the part of the
chief orator, the while he rummaged
for a reply—any old sort of reply.
At last: “ Oh, I dunno !” he groaned
and subsided. It would be too much
of a horrible bore altogether to be
Premier in a country where gardener-
persons put horrid questions like
that.
N. S. W. Minister Griffith
started his State brickworks the
other day ; and there doesn’t seem
to be more capital leaving the
country than usual. In fact, two or
three days after the fires were lit, one
of Australia’s biggest trading con-
cerns—Burns, Philp and Co. —an-
nounced in Sydney that they were
asking to increase their nominal
capital from half a million to two
millions, and their paid capital from £300,000 to
£750,000, in view of the improved outlook for
larger trade and bigger profits.
A few days before the Referenda vote was
taken, the Liberal party flooded the country with
this allegation :
If the powers sought for are conferred, the Federal
Government will have the power to prohibit the carrying
on of business at all.
The State Government has every power which the
Federal Government asked for; so the people
who voted “No” will now have time to reflect
that, since the power was not transferred to the
Commonwealth, the State Government can forth-
with shut up businesses and declare the wearing of
straw hats illegal.
“CLASS CONSCIENCE.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There is a movement in Victoria to hold a conference of
churches for the purpose of considering what steps may
be taken to combat the growing “ class conscience.”
“ Class conscience ” is a wicked thing ;
It makes the working man aspire
To have the tender liver wing,
The motor-car and diamond ring,
The pretty wife, the fine attire,
And all the things the Fats desire.
The boss had viands rich and choice,
The “ man ” had rags, a hunk, a bone;
And then the gentle pastor’s voice
In poverty bade him rejoice,
And all was well from zone to zone—
“ Class conscience ” was a thing unknown.
The working man has been to school;
A vicious change has come to pass—
He sees the payment of the fool
Is scraps and tatters, as a rule.
Now self-help, practised by the mass
Becomes “antipathy of class.”
When “ master ” got the prize of toil,
And all the lavish land’s increase,
And labor got the drips of oil,
And never dared to raise a broil,
None cried to have “ class conscience ” cease—
Dear brethren, that was “perfect peace.”
VICTORIA.
EDDY SON.
SUPPORT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Argus abuses Hughes for his appeal in favor of a
Labor daily. It concludes with a lofty air : “ Let the
party amend its ways, abandon its class hatreds, adopt a
patriotic policy of conciliation and co-operation for the
advancement of Australia, and it will not lack press
support.”
If it will kneel, in gratitude,
The Age will bless its attitude;
And if ’twould hang, there’s plenty scope—
The Argus will provide a rope.
’Tis quite a piquant paradox :
The “ Liberal ” papers come, in flocks,
Support to give the Labor clown,
If he will take it lying down !
VICTORIA.
