SPORTING
NOTIONS
The finish of Sydney First Grade Cricket
Competition was a close and interesting thing. In
the history of the premiership there has bean no-
thing more exciting, and anything exciting about
cricket is remembered for centuries. At the begin-
ning of the last round Gordon was just ahead on
points, with Redfern hovering horribly on the hori-
zon. If Redfern beat University and Burw’ood
downed Gordon, the fat was in the fire, and a defeat
of both would have hashed things up gloriously.
But Redfern got University down, and fell upon it
to the tune of 224 on the first innings. This made
the Syrian suburb very hopeful, and it inquired
loudly if anyone had heard how Gordon w r as dealing
with Burwood. Gordon was dealing with Burwood
with a vengeance; Burwood had smacked up 281 in
the first innings, and Gordon had responded with
287 runs for two wickets (Macartney, 107, not out;
Trumper, 79; Iredale, 36; Johnston. 56, not out;
sundries, nine). So Gordon won by eight wickets
and six runs on the first innings—a fearful hiding,
the father of a dog-walloping. Gordon thus won the
premiership with 16 points; Redfern was runner-up
with 14 ; then University, nine; Petersham, eight;
North Sydney, three; Burwood, two. After that the
score ran from one point by Central Cumberland, to
Waverley’s 11 below zero. Cricket may not be de-
cadent, but some of the clubs evidently are.
Altogether Saturday was a great day. Cotter
got loose and played the Triton among the minnows,
clean-bowling seven Sydney batsmen for 39 runs,
and performing the hat trick. Folkard smashed up
six Cumberlanders for 40, and Hazlitt, in return,
took six Balmaniacs for 45. These were only the
cream of the performances; there were plenty of
others of excellent quality. But, in spite of this
fearful slaughter, the bat also got loose, and LiP um "
phreys’s 114, in a total of 207, Macartney’s.'lo7,
Roughley’s 109, and Johnston’s 116 made 'a ; track
of glory across the afternoon. And below; these
were Dean’s 90 and Harvey’s 85 (not out).
The Sydney Second Graders also ended
up their little flutter. Marrickville, in the first at-
tempt, took the premiership with one point to spare
from University 11. .In the Third Grade, Norjm
Sydney gained its sixth success in seven
mighty record, indeed. i :
J. N. Crawford has received a formal
notice from Surrey County that it has rescinded its
own resolution of August, 1909, which kicked him
out. It seems to have been a silly business, anyway.
Surrey had beaten the Australian XI., and Crawford,
who was asked to act in the captain’s absence for
the return match, thought they could do; the trick
again if he had all the best material available 1 . The
committee, however, left out one or two, profes-
sionals he thought invaluable. He protested- Both
sides said more than they meant; and eventually
St. Peter’s College, Adelaide, , got a new junior
master, and S.A. got a chance —-which it promptly
took —of winning the Sheffield Shield. Surrey’s pre-
sent move may be merely doing a decent action, or
may have an eye to getting J.N.C. back. He is
just giving, up schoolmastering, with the idea of
going where all young South Aus. goes now —on the
Land.
Crowds lined up at tlie Victorian League
matches on Saturday, showing that the public isn’t
as disgusted as it might be at the “demoralisation
of ’oofball. The East Melbourne ground, in par-
ticular, was packed as for a semi-final. Here Essen-
don and Carlton collided with some force, but with-
out animosity ; in fact, all the day’s games passed oft
with an absence of brawls. The ’Dons were faster
and lasted better, but their lamentable shooting
robbed them of victory. At half-time they had put
on 16 points—one goal 10 behinds —to Carlton s 30
—four goals six behinds. In the second spell, how-
ever they improved their aim, and had just equalised
matters —45 points all —when the bell rang. First
draw of the season, after a strenuous and cheerful
but not over-scientific game. At Victoria Park, the
premiers, ’Wood, met Richmond, which had been
assuring its supporters that it was the pea. The
yellow-and-blacks worked with certainty, and had a
few good kickers; but the combination that ensures
success wasn’t there. The Magpies, on the contrary,
marked vilely, but their concerted play rattled Rich-
mond, and the premiers rubbed it in to the tune of
94 points to 65. Souths, with only one recruit to
the team that lost the semi-final last year, took the
field against the Students, whom cireumstanoes an-
nually rob of many of their best ’bailers. They had
six novices on Saturday, also they were short of prac-
tice and void of combination; consequently Souths
established, a lead of 45 points to six in the first
quarter, and increased it to 102 to 40 by 5 o’clock.
The big Metropolitan C. G. opened its gates to a
very large crowd, the M.C.O. having decided to pro-
vide football tickets (at 55.) to those who take no
stock in cricket, picture-shows and the like. In con-
sequence, that team now boasts a body of barrackers,
who urged it to bog in and demolish Fitzroy. ’Roy
is suffering from an internal upheaval, and played
nine new men, who hustled to such purpose that
they led throughout, and were only overwhelmed in
the closing minutes, losing by two points—-70 to
Melbourne’s 72. Geelong, which uprose like Jonahs
gourd towards the close of last season, came up and
tackled St. Hilda. The subsequent disaster was re-
presented by the figures—Saints, 66 points; Sleepy
Hollow, 57. # *
The chief Ass. episode was the colli-
sion of Brunswick and Port. It was expected to be
rather easy for the Brickies; but the others have
acquired two ex-League tiptoppers, who have been
licking the erst-uproarious Portsmen into shape, with
the result that they played the game instead of the
goat, and won by 62 points to 48. Brighton con-
ducted a genteel discussion with P’ran, in which the
thick end of the stick was always held by the shop-
keeping suburb. P’ran, 107 points; Brighton, 32.
This controversy saw the only serious casualty of the
day, a dislocated shoulder. Loving neighbors ’Scray
and ’Town got into holts on ’Town’s ground, the
locals scoring by 21 to ’Scray’s 50. Their passing,
marking and kicking were all poor ; so if they don’t
play for the love of it, like real amateurs, Heaven
knows what they do it for at all. North put up a
mountain of points, viz., 148, to Preston’s 50; and
Essendon made an even holier show of Northcote by
scoring 118 to 18. But Preston and ’Cote are used
to it.
* * * *
N.S.W. Rugby League opened its First
Grade competition on Saturday last in ideal weather
and before huge crowds. Play was strenuous, but
two of the matches had a runaway flavor about them
that detracted from the quality somewhat. South
Sydney scrunched North Sydney by 25 points to five;
which was bad. But the fate of Annandale was even
worse, for Eastern Suburbs rubbed its nose in the
gutter to the tune of 30 points to three. The Glebe
v. Western Suburbs was more even, Glebe winning
by eight to nil. Western Suburbs failed through
the weakness of the forward crowd, especially in the
scrums. Its back-machine was all gold, and worked
like a machine should work—faultlessly. Excellent
kicking kept the Glebe penned up in its own terri-
tory till it became a forward game, and then Glebe
raged like the heathen. It wasn’t so much that
Glebe triumphed as that Western Suburbs failed.
As regards the other two matches, in the North and
South Sydney cmeute, it was the superb passing of
the southern backs that did the mischief. Their
work in this respect was really superb, and, conse-
quently, their charges were irresistible. In addition,
they showed a solid front in defence that broke the
hearts of the Northerners, who simply withered
away. As for Eastern Suburbs and Annandale, the
defeated side was simply out of it —an army of
veterans against raw militia.
The Rugby Union contented itself with
four practice matches, and the quality of the play
showed how serious the stampede has been. The
Union clubs are hard hit, indeed. But the new ma-
terial is promising.
Union Rugby kicks off on Saturday. Al-
though about to engage in a fight for its very life,
it is still amazingly strong in clubs, eight in the
first grade, 14 in the second and 10 in the third
grade. This paper does not regard the position of
the Union as hopeless. All it wants, even now,
is good management to pull it out of the mud. But
whether it has that management about the premises,
only time will reveal.
Sydney University tennis team had a good
time in Adelaide, beating Melbourne (previous holders
of the inter-University challenge-shield) and Ade-
laide comfortably. Cricketer Barbour and Rugby
footballer Metcalfe were in the Sydney six, and the
latter won the champion singles which followed,
beating in the final Herring, who lately played as
one of Victoria’s best six in the regular inter-State
match. The University doubles champions proved
to be Utz and Roxburgh, also of Sydney, Nott and
Bayliss, of Adelaide, being runners-up.
The Mars Buckley Cup, the utensil which
causes an annual commotion among the inter-State
lawn tennis players, is to remain in the Ma State
another year, owing to the Victorians being de-
feated by the N.S.W. reps, by 10 rubbers to two.
Bananaland was anxious to fight for the mug, but the
challenge came too late, and the Northerners were
choked off for another year.
The 47th Inter-State tennis match between
N.S.W. and Victoria was celebrated last week at
Double Bay (Sydney). The men’s singles and
doubles saw N.S.W. on top with 20 rubbers, 40 sets,
and 290 games, against Victoria’s one rubber, nine
sets, 176 games. The solitary win for Victoria went
to Campbell and Blair over Parker and Rice. The
ladies’ side of the game provided a closer finish, the
Ma State being in the ascendancy with six rubbers,
15 sets, 121 games, to Victoria’s six rubbers, 12
sets, 121 games. Up to that point everything in the
garden was lovely; but then the N.S.W. Lawn
Tennis Association began to feel bad. The gate was
a shocking failure, yielding only £32 14s, 9d., a
drop of over £6O compared with last year’s total.
If the State championships turn out no greater draw
than the inter-State matches, the Association will
find itself in a queer position.
Unless the , N. S. Wales Lawn Tennis Associa-
tion can rake the money out of the public its case
is hopeless. More complaints of meanness are laid
against tennis players than any others. At the
courts, there are scouting boys who bring back the
balls that are driven hither and thither, and who
are generally regarded as caddies are at golf. But
while your golfer always pays his caddies, and usu-
ally pays them well, the rule in tennis seems to be
“Do the boys if you can.” At one of the biggest
suburban courts last Saturday, writer saw a small
boy scouting for a dozen players from 1.30 till 5
p.m., and, as he was kept going all the time, they
were very glad to have him. Yet those dozen mis-
erable “sports” slunk off without offering the boy
even a penny. And that’s the snag Lawn Tennis is
up against—it has too many of that sort among
its members.
* * * *
The Inter-State eight-oar race on the Par-
ramatta (N.S.W.) next Saturday is to be all-
Australian, as every State will be well re-
presented, and the Westralians are, at time
of writing, just as sanguine as the Ma
Staters. The Ma State crew has fairly lived on the
water the last few weeks, and slept in camp close be-
side it; and if careful preparation means anything,
they will be a formidable boat-load. And usually
careful preparation means a lot. The Australian Single
Sculls should also make for excitement when Cecil
McVilly, of Tasmania, defends his title against the
assault of J. R. Towns (N.S.W.) and H. E. Stevens
(Vic.). Rowing is in a good way just now, the only
beetle in the soup being the wail of the Ma Associa-
tion that there are no funds to give the visitors a
good time.
The Great Public Schools Regatta on the
Parramatta (N.S.W.) last week provided a rare tit-
bit in the “Championship Eights.” From end to
end there was never more than a couple of lengths
between the four entrants, and when St. Joseph’s
College struggled through with no more than 2ft. to
spare from Sydney Grammar School, with the other
boats almost in a'line, the welkin split. The “fours”
also produced a fine race, which St. Ignatius won,
with the others in a bunch a length away. Alto-
gether it was a Saints’ day.
* * * *
N.S.W. Bowling Association got into the
soup over the question of a paid secretary. The
committee recently came to the conclusion that
the affairs of the Association had now grown to a
size which practically demanded the whole time of
at least one man. To provide the salary, the com-
mittee recommended that a-capitation tax of 3s. per
annum be levied on metropolitan members, and Is.
on those who dwell and play in the country. It
seemed reasonable enough, Heaven knows; but the
members went cantankerous, and kicked the proposal
into limbo. Whereupon the -president, secretary, and
the rest of the officers went on strike and left the
Association dumbfounded-
Henry Moses, of the Commercial Bank of
Sydney, who was about the hardest man in his day
to dig out of the N.S.W. Inter-State Cricket Team
they were the days of Sammy Jones, Percy Mac-
Donnell, Turner and Ferris —has now won the N.S.W.
Bowls championship. Moses left the cricket field
in his prime, because he couldn’t afford the neces-
sary time for practice. He at once took to bowls,
and was an instant success at it.
The spread of bowls in S. Aus. is best
shown by the rise of the suburban clubs. The
District system not being enforced, the first-estab-
lished Adelaide and Oval clubs keep the premiership
between them; but they are always liable to get a
shaking-up from an outside club, and this year the
individual-best player is declared to be Bednall, of
the Semaphore. “Champion of champions” is the
modest • title that goes with this success. Also, the
same club has annexed the single-rink-of-four pre-
miership, with a team which, by some curious ar-
rangement (or lack of it), did not include its own
champion.
The most extravagantly foolish of all
the English attempts to belittle George Gray’s bil-
liards is the suggestion advanced by the London
Sportsman —-ordinarily a sane paper —that his prac-
tice of “allowing his cue to rest on the cloth after
completing a stroke, .and making a lateral movement
with? the tip, raises the nap sufficiently to check the
red ball on the desired spot.” From this it might
be supposed that Gray’s losing hazards from baulk
were always made into the same pocket. Besides, if
his cue affected the surface of the cloth from the
very outset of a long break (assuming, of course,
that he confined his play to one pocket only), he
would build up a sort of barricade by the time he
got to his 500th consecutive hazard. In which
case the red ball would finish its career down the
table by resting against the ridge of nap on the
far side, and Gray’s own ball would need to do a
kangaroo act in order to hit. the red. The inven-
tion of such a theory is a lovely compliment to
Gray’s achievement. His play is so accurate that
a marvellous explanation is offered to the British
public, who, in days of long ago, were gravely told
that the quick movement of Ned Hanlan’s racing
skiff between strokes might be due to secret mechan-
ism underneath it.
“Spot”: Looks as though billiards will
have to be re-organised and the red bail eliminated
in some way. Consider how the red ball kills in-
terest in the game. At the match with A. E. Wil-
liams, at Sydney, last week, Fred Lindrum made
breaks of 706, 541, 53S and other huge numbers,
and thereby won a 14,000 game by 5057. _ Why,
when George Gray returns and challenges Lindrum
for the championship, the game will degenerate into
an endurance tussle, which will bring even club-
swinger Tom Burrows to tears.
Australian cyclists are still battering awav
at Leon Meredith’s 100-mile record, but with little
effect. The nearest approach was that of J.
McSweeney, of Westralia, who ran over the distance
in shr. 26min. lOsec. ; but between that and the
4hr. 52min. 51 4-ssee. of the Englishman, there
is fixed a great gulf. Many records and reputations
are, however, buried in Australia, and there is every
probability that the record of Leon Meredith will
find a grave in the same soil within the next few
months.
•w * * w
N.S.W. Amateur Athletic Association has
taken the only way to secure a' ground, by entering
into an agreement for the use of the Sydney Sports
Ground throughout the summer. The Rugby League
has the same area for the winter; so the space is
occupied from the kick-off till the drawing of stumps.
Amateur athletics boom in Victoria. The
opening of the cross-country season last week saw
429 start in the combined run, representing 18
clubs. “Sydney for pleasure,” Melbourne for the
strenuous life.
* * * *
“ J.P.”: In a recent Bulletin (16/3/T1)
a Christchurch (M.L.) cricket record was noted.
Auckland has just provided another. A softgoods
drummer, named Rowntree, knocked 50 runs off two
overs —five fours and a six, followed by six fours.
The bowler’s name is mercifully withheld. His alias
is Mud.
* * * *
They last well, some of these crack rifle-
shots. “Carbine Jimmy” Ross, who had won the
Mapriland Carbine belt in 1884, 1886 and 1888 (he
got it then for keeps), was still plugging with great
accuracy at last year’s M.L. rifle meeting; tied for
first place in the first stage of the “King’s,” and
finished third for the championship belt. But the
conditions under which Jimmy did the great things
have at last been changed; the M.L. defence man-
agers have come to a sensible decision to ban the old
fixed bull’s-eye target from all ranges, and replace
it with objects more in the nature of service con-
ditions.
* * * *
The Sydney hockey-players are great on
nomenclature. Consider the names of their clubs:
Bandits, Barbarians, Corinthians, Corona, Pilgrims,
Ramblers, Rovers and Trojans. The season opens
on the 22nd, and the programme has been arranged
up till July 1, after which the champions will fight
out another round among themselves, to decide
which of them will kiss Polly at the end of the
season.
A queer case was tried before Judge
Jameson at the Croydon (Q.) District Court the
other day. Last July a match for £5O a-side was
run between two local horses —Deerfoot and Velox.
There was a very close finish, but the judge reckoned
that Velox had won bys a head. The owner
Deerfoot protested; but the protest was dismissed
by the committee of the local racing club. (The
match took place on a race day, and the committee
had control of it.) The next day the stakes were
paid over to the owner of Velox ; but the owner of
the other nag told the stakeholder that he paid at
his own risk, and started an action to recover his
£5O. Judge Jameson said there was no doubt
that Velox had won; nevertheless, he was bound to
give a verdict for the plaintiff, under an old English
law which declares that if one of the parties to a
wager expresses dissent before the money is paid
over, he may recover it from the stake-hoider. He
admitted that he didn’t like deciding that way, and
would be glad to state a case for the Full Court.
And finally he rapped out that he wouldn’t allow
Costs, anyway.
*** * ’ ’
Adelaide put up two records of a sort on
April 8. One was on the city racecourse, where
Steelbit, by Lord Grenville (dam unknown), a six-
year-old, with 9st. 51b. up, did the two-mile
"steeplechase in 3min. 52sec. (The obstacles _ at
Victoria Park are not trifles, either.) The previous
record was by Findowie, at Caulfield, 3min. _s2jsec.
There may, however, be trouble over this new
time. The electric Clock, which “can’t lie,” is
started when the gong sounds. But the gong went
late, and, as the electric ticker was obviously going
to lie, it was not used. The time given was taken
by the hon. timekeeper, on his own watch, from
the fall of the flag. The other record is only a
“probably”—6min. 44sec. on a 3i-h.p. Triumph
motor-cycle, for 4 miles 148 yards up the Greenhill-
road, which is about the nastiest of the hill-climbing
tests that are scattered around Adelaide’s back door.
The bookies at Scarsdale (Vic.) went on
strike the other day because the judge declared the
favorite, New Moon, had won, when the ring was
quite convinced in its own bag that Royal Lady had
won. In high dudgeon the bookmakers resolutely
refused to take any more of the people’s money for
the rest of that day. In a roundabout way it re-
calls the pathetic outburst of a well-known Mel-
bourne penciller: “The bleedin’ horse won, after
all! Tell you what it is, there’ll be no certainty
in racing till the exclamation ring rides the con-
taminated horses itself.”
* * *
Although the Rugby Leaguers entered the
field last Saturday, winter from the athletic stand-
point does not really begin till next Saturday, when
Lacrosse, Union Rugby, Australian and Soccer will
all burst simultaneously into flood. The cricket sea-
son is a sad time for the professional barrackers, and
the arrival of football opens the lungs and brings
new life to the howlers. What the barrackers would
do if football were abolished only the Lord knows,
and because He knows, presumably He will not allow
football to come to naught.
Broken Hill has a Ladies’ Cricket Asso-
ciation, which ends its season with Miss Reidy at
the top of the batting' averages, with 28. Her 62
wickets, at an average of under 4, might reason-
ably be expected to put her head of the bowling
also. But it didn’t. Miss Stephens took 116 at
an average cost of 31.
One Percy Thellusson, who carries a cour-
tesy “Hon.” in his carpet-bag, has put up a record
in M.L trout-fishing at one of the bays on Lake
Taupo. His catch for 11 days was 370 fish, weigh-
ing 33751 b. ; and the Maoris and pakehas of little
Taupo have had small meat-bills for a fortnight.
“Martinmas”: The average fighting man
hasn’t much idea of time whilst he is in the ring.
When young Jim Armstrong fought Mehegan in Ade-
laide he lasted 10 rounds, but confessed that he re-
members nothing after round four. At the local
Stadium, not long ago, a boxer went back to his
corner after the sixth round, and calmly said:
“That’s the second round gone, ain’t it?” When
Tom Dunn and Ernest Waddy fought a fearful mix-
up, after the second round Waddy asked how many
rounds had been fought. When he was told, he was
amazed. He thought he’d been hitting the other
fellow half an hour.
Talking at the Sydney Journalists’ In-
stitute the other night, aviator Hammond said he
was one of the original 12 “flying men” of whom
eight are now in Heaven. He was, he believes, the
first representative of the Pan-Britannic nations to
take to the business —or gamble—whichever you
like. That he is now among the most skilful of the
world’s aviators is a feather in the cap of Austral-
asia, which bred him. He is a Maorilander.
*'***
The battle of Kid McCoy versus Billy
Wenand, fought at Melb. Athletic Club’s Hall, on
Eight-Hours morning, was a trial of stoush between a
game, clever, small boy and an equally clever lad
with about 141 b. of beef to the good. Weight told,
but little Wenand, the Maori bantam, made a rat-
tling good scrap of it as far as it went. Billy’s wild
career ended abruptly in the seventh, when McCoy
had his opponent finished, and the Maorilander wisely
“flew the rag.” McCoy is just about a good enough
proposition for any lightweight in Australia, and it
was not a fair deal to give the bright little bantling
from over the water so tough a nut for his first
crack in Australia.
“Silas Suell”: Clarry Weber does not
seem too precipitate about meeting even that bulky
ancient, Mike Williams, and is making demands on the
boxer ridiculously at variance with his published in-
tention of providing a demonstration of the super-
iority of wrestling as a means of self-defence. It
is now claimed by Weber’s people that Mike must
wear a stout, loose jacket, and be prepared to box
on a wrestling-mat. Of course, he must also muffle
his punch with two or three ounces of padding. The
jacket would greatly help the “rassler,” and seriously
impede the boxer’s arms. The mat would clog the
latter’s feet. There is really no call whatever for a
practical demonstration of the fact that a freC,
powerful 14st. wrestler is too good for a 12st.
boxer blindfolded, hobbled, and having his hands tied
behind him. We’re all willing to take that much
for granted.
**. * *
Mike Williams, the pug., evidently thinks
no bread is better than half a loaf. When he turned
up at Bendigo, recently, to fight Doran, the ex;
amateur heavy-weight champion, he found a house
worth only £22 10s., and immediately made anxious
calculations as to the disposal of this magnificent
sum. The referee was to get £3, and the manage-
ment 30 per cent. ; which left only a possible
£l2 15s. for Michael. So he decided the money
was not worth the risk of the stoushing he would
either get or give, and refused to scrap. Some
people felt sore about it, but Mike stoutly
maintains that he was justified in refusing to serve
out battery for such a small purse. It was all right
for Doran, he says, Doran being a new arrival from
the amateur ranks, but it was no good for Michael.
Now people loudly affirm that it was as much fear
of what Doran would serve out to him as the paucity
of the house that caused him to come to such a de-
cision. But that’s quite absurd; Mike may not be
much of a bruiser, but his courage is indisputable.
* * * *
One of the qualifications of a gentleman
who trains bruisers nowadays is the ability to in-
vent sonorous sobriquets for his proteges. At this
business Tahmmy Burns is a past master, so
this unknown packet, Jack Lester, who has
come to Australia under the great little
fighter’s auspices, is billed as “The Fight-
ing Demon.” Why, only Heaven and Tahmmy
know. Other people guess—but cannot be sure, of
course —that it is all part of the advertising scheme.
When those Yankees were here we found gentlemen
labelled “Cyclone,” who didn’t amount to even a
whirlwind, though they certainly blew for all they
were worth. The night that Papke jigged round
the ring with Dave Smith in pursuit, someone
who wandered in, under the impression that he was
in a picture show, inquired who Papke was. “The
Illinois Thunderbolt!” said someone. “Oh!” re-
plied the other. “I can see the bolt all right;
but where’s the thunder?” All the same, this Jack
Lester is a promising-looking animal enough, and
his uppercut is said by competent judges to be a
thing of beauty to look at, but an obscene thing to
feel. At present he weighs 14J stone, and, as he
is only sft. 9in. high, it can be gathered that he is
of the solid build of which born fighters are made.
He is a Canadian, but that fact will not prevent
the Yankee press speaking of him as “American” —
unless he is defeated, in which case he will be
Canadian, and Canadian only.

Legal Notice.
r I 10 LANGDON MORCOM or other per-
JL sons claiming his share in the Estate
of ELLEN SI BELLA BILLING, deceased.
Pursuant to an Order of the Chancery
Division of the High Court of Justice dated
the 27th February, 1911, made in the Mat-
ter of the Estate of Ellen Sybella Billing de-
ceased in an Action wherein Harry Fitch
was Plaintiff and Caroline Gilbert (Widow)
Defendant (1911. B. 399). Whereby it
was Ordered that the following enquiry
should be made that is to say :
“1. An Enquiry whether Langdon Mor-
com a nephew of the Testatrix is living or
dead and if dead when he died and if he pre-
deceased the Testatrix whether he left any
and what issue who survived her and if he
survived the Testatrix who by devise descent
or otherwise are or is entitled to the moiety
of the two one-fifth shares of the Testatrix’s
real estate which descended to him and who
is his legal personal representative” the said
LANGDON MORCOM or the person or
persons claiming by devise descent or other-
wise to be entitled to the moiety of the said
shares of the Testatrix’s real estate or to be
t Personal representative of the said
LANGDON MORCOM or otherwise to be
interested under the said enquiry are by
their Solicitors on or before the Ist day of
July 1911 to come in and prove their claims
at Chambers of Mr. Justice Warrington
and Mr. Justice Parker, Royal Courts of Jus-
tice, London, England (Room No. 252) or
in default thereof they will be peremptorily
excluded from the benefit of the said Order.
Tuesday, the 4th day of July 1911 at 12
o clock at noon at the said Chambers is ap-
pointed for hearing and adjudicating upon
the said claims.
Dated this 20tli day of March 1911.
R. T. WATKIN WILLIAMS,
Master of the Supreme Court.
NOTE.—The said Ellen Sibella Billing
died on the 28th September 1910. The said
Langdon .Morcom left England in a Mer-
chant Ship in or about the year 1862 and
was seen in England again in the course of
the year 1867. He is believed to have left
ms ship in Australia in 1868 or 1869 and
was last heard of in the latter year

Street, London, England,

Forest Devil.
HOW A LADY WORKED
THE “DEVIL.”
Messrs. J. COOPER & SONS,
Melbourne.
PANTON HILL,
17th October, 1910.
Dear Sirs,—Just a few lines to let you know how we are getting along with the Bunyip
•Forest Devil father bought from you three weeks ago. Father says it is the iwu.
MACHINE HE HAS EVER USED, and he has seen and used a good many, and is very
pleased with it. I must let you know a little what we have done with it. Cur trees are
mostly white gum, box, and messmate. We have uprooted trees that were 4ft. "
diameter, or so thick that the anchor rope was just big enough to go round themwithi only
father and myself on the lever. There was one giant that, when 1 said to pull it, father
seemed verv doubtful, and told me to wait until my brothers came home from school , but i
said, “ Put'the ropes on ” ; and both of us got on the lever, and in a short time,. jut came the
monster with roots in the air, and made a hole not less than sft. deep and 12ft wide, we
had not seen a tree pulled .like it. Father would not have the hole filled up, but lets it stop
there so people can see WHAT A POWERFUL MACHINE THE BUNY '•
have PULLED TREES MYSELF, about 2ft. in diameter, without any help. Some of our
trees are dead about five years, and the>'P
soil, but it is all the same. THE GREEN ONES COME OUT JUST AS EASY AS IHE
DEAD ONES. There is NO STOPPING when once the “ Devil catches hold of them
THEY HAVE TO COME WITH THE BUNYIP. Wishing yfiu every suwess in the
fuTure, yours truly, (Signed) MARIA NOTSCHALL.
John Cooper 8 Sons,
287-9 Elizabeth Street (opp. St.
Francis’s Church), MELBOURNE.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will he
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
Far Back: Thanks for the idea. But,
you see, a lot of these fellows don’t warnt to be

MEMORIES.
Within a stylish mansion sat a noted wealthy squire,
Brooding o’er past memories before a ruddy fire;
He recalled as he sadly pondered his first unhappy
Wife
| He had deserted and left to die in the blossoms of
her life; ,
He was interrupted by a servant who informed him
she had found
Nestled upon the doorstep A child sleeping sound ;
He pressed the waif into his bosom and as he gazed
upon her face
The image of his departed wife the gloomy man did
trace; '
Bowed by grief and memories he knelt in sad Des-
pair
And offered up to the great Supreme a long repent-
ful prayer.

PORT ARTHUR, TASMANIA.
Behold those ruined walls and falling bricks!
That relics of a shameless bygone hell
That still must tell in silent words
Some mysteries of the darksome killing cell.
Steaming up the harbor from the south
The Penitentiary braves its rotting wall,
And looks so calmly o’er a calmer bay
As if to contradict the hell it was withall.
But look beyond, upon the hill above
The hospital still holds up its ruined bust
And shows its sandstone pillars to a rising sun
Which fails to brighten where to dust.
So many wretches were painfully reduced
That many hosts have passed through that black door
Beyond which lies the flogging yard
Whose triangles and “cats” are now no more.
We descend across the English oak-lined creek
By terraced metal slopes and reach at last
The Asylum whose rusting clock still chimes
The hours—much shorter now than in the past.

I decided not to call for the census papers that
day.
“Albatross” has varied luck :
I w’as sitting on the cliffs with my legs hanging over
the edge. Beneath my dangling feet stretched the in-
finite Ocean. It was morning; a kindly sun was
shining dowm from an altitude of about 15 degrees, and
his rays had fashioned a silver motor track on the ocean
highway. The sea was blue —the bluest I had ever seen
—and the silence could not have been more profound. I
was very happy ; and no wonder ! I had got a letter
from my owners saying they were satisfied I had not
been to blame for the loss of my last ship, and had
appointed me to the command of a fine new vessel at
£4OO a year. Moreover, they proposed to make me some
recompense for the six months I had been idle ! With a
wild singing in my heart, I looked out across the inviting
sea. As I did so, I saw- a steamer’s smoke on the horizon.
The silence was so intense that I could distinctly'hear
the throbbing of her engines and the thrashing of her
propeller even before her hull was visible. Onward she
came, and I saw that she was heading for the cliffs.
What was the skipper doing? Was he mad? I longed
to shout out a warning, hut my tongue cleaved to m v
mouth. I knew she was rushing to her destruction, and
I could not warn her ! She was close in now', and here
was I sitting helpless, speechless ! Ah !... I struggled
and then I covered m.y ears with my hands to shutout
the crash.. .. Will that scene ever be effaced from my
memory? With a shriek from her siren that my hands
could not shut out, the steamer disappeared ! In my
agony I rolled off the seat. I had missed that d d
train again !

The “Number One ” Chicago
HAND HORSE CLIPPING MACHINE.
*
It’s easy to see why clipping- a horse improves his health,
prolongs his life and increases his value.
“ Just think,” says a leading authority on horse breed-
ing, “how it would feel to be on your bed with clothes
wringing wet with gummy sweat. Too often, after sweat-
ing freely, a horse stands all night wet to the very skin,
using up, to dry his thick hair, all the heat that should
be going to recuperate his tired body. Thus he loses
strength steadily. Whereas, if the clippers should be run
over him, he would dry off in 20 minutes, and stand warm
and comfortable all night. But that isn’t all. When a
horse takes a chill from sleeping in his wet coat, rheu-
matism, pneumonia and kidney disease result, and the
horse is ruined.”
That’s why you need a “ Number One ” Hand Horse
Clipping Machine.

THE BANK OF ADELAIDE.
FORTY-FIFTH ANNUAL REPORT OF THE DIRECTORS TO THE SHAREHOLDERS.
The Directors have pleasure in submitting herewith for the information of Shareholders the Balance-
sheet of the Bank as on the 27th March, 1911; also statements of Profit and Loss Account and the Reserve
Fund on that date.
The balance to credit of Profit and Loss is .. .. .. .. ..£76,485 13 9
which it is proposed to appropriate as follows:
To Reserve Fund (making it £430,000) .. ... .. .. £30,000
To Dividend at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum . . .. 25,000
■ 55,000 0 0 ,
Leaving a balance of .. .. .. .. .. .. .. .. £ 21,485 13 9
to be carried forward.
An Interim Dividend at the rate of 10 per cent, per annum was paid in November last.
The 25,000 new Shares, the issue of which was authorised by the Shareholders on Ist June last,
were offered to the Shareholders at £6 per Share (£4 being Capital and £2 Premium). The Shares
have all been taken up and paid for, and the £4 per Share—£loo,ooo —has been added to the Bank’s
Capital, making it £500,000; and the Premium of £2 per Share—£so,ooo —-has been credited to
the Reserve Fund, making it £400,000.
Since last Report a Branch: of the Bank has been opened at Waikerie, and Agencies opened at
Blanchetown, Bowhill, Halbury, Monarto South, and Purnong, and the Agency at Meningie has been closed.
The General Annual Meeting will be held at No. 25 Waymouth Street, Adelaide, on Wednesday j
3rd May, 1911, at 11 o’clock a.m., at which the Shareholders will be called upon to elect Two Directors
in place of the Hon. Sir Jenkin Coles, K.C.M.G., M.P., and Arthur Waterhouse, Esq., who retire by
rotation, but, being eligible, offer themselves for re-election.
The Shareholders will also have to elect Two Auditors for the ensuing year, and to fix the
remuneration of the retiring Auditors. By order of the Boar(1 of DirectorS)
R. S. YOUNG, Assistant Manager.

The National Bank of Australasia Limited.
1. The Balance-Sheet of the Bank as on 31st March last, and the Profit and Loss Account for the Half-Year which
then terminated, are now submitted to the shareholders.

3. During the half-year Branches were opened at Lalbert and Nyah, in Victoria ; Tumby Bay, in Soutli Australia ■
Corowain New South Wales ; and Wongan Hills and Wyalcatchem, in Western Australia. The office at Lindenow
was continued as an agency. An agency was opened at Raywood, in Victoria.
4. The Ordinary General Annual Meeting will be held at the Head Office of the Bank, Collins Street, Melbourne
on Wednesday, 3rd May, at noon. ’
5. The shareholders will be called on to elect two directors on that occasion, in lieu of Sir William Austin Zeal
K.C. M.G., and the Honorable Joseph Major Pratt, who retire by rotation. There are no other candidates. They will
also be required to appoint two auditors.
By order of the Board of Directors,
The National Bank of Australasia Limited.

MEDLOW BATH, Blue Mountains, N.S.W.
MAJESTIC HOTEL.
COMPLETE arrangements have been made by the new proprietor (Mr. W. H. Burgess), late of the Grand
Hotel, Melbourne, for the comfort of visitors during the forthcoming season.
Medlow Bath is an ideal resort. The crisp mountain air is a delight, and the mountains, in their
Spring garb, appeal to lovers of the picturesque. Arrangements can be made at the Hotel for motors,
carriages and riding tours.
Motor parties can be arranged any time to start from the City Office.—or your own residence—direct
to the Majestic Hotel.
To pedestrians Medlow offers ever-varying attractions. The views in the mountains are superb, and
hours can be spent in walks through the Hotel grounds.
Hot-air pipes are laid in many rooms. Daily Motor trips are made from the Hotel to Jenolan Oaves.
Further particulars can be obtained from the Hotel, or from the Sydney Offices, 42 Oastlereagh Street.
W. H. BURGESS, Proprietor.

THROW AWAY YOUR PIPE
AND
SMOKE MY CIGARS
Cf\auw t
I’VE knocked around a bit over Australasia in
my time—been in the back parts of pretty
well every State and New Zealand.
I’ve mixed with shearers and musterers and
rouseabouts at different sheds; been at cattle
camps in Queensland; up among the snow
country of Monaro- in the jarrah forests of
Westralia; in mining camps all over the Com-
monwealth; and everywhere I ve seen the boys
smoking their pipes—and seldom a sign of a
Cigar.
Then, I've pulled out my case and handed
some DIADEMAS around. And after ail this,
people say to me—“Oh, Australians won’t smoke
Cigars I'*
ħ
I do just get riled when I
hear this, I tell you that
nearly every pipe-smoker,
once he gets the taste of my
DIADEMAS Cigars, swears
by them. They knock pipe-
smoking hollow!
There’s nothing but To-
bacco in them—and the best
Cigar Tobacco that is grown
—the wrapper, finest Java
leaf; and the inside filling,
all clean, straight,long Brazil.
They draw easily and burn
freely, for they are hand-
rolled by as good Cigar-
makers as there are any-
where in the world.
The boys out-back I’ve
given them to, as I said, just
swear by my Cigars, Men
in the Cigar trade here, too
- —men with whom Cigar-
smoking is a life habit—come
to me and say—“ Look here,
those Cigars of yours are
better than any we can get
in the city. Let us have a
box as a favor.”
Now, I want you to try
my Cigars —at my ex-
pense.
I want to convert you from
the pipe to Cigars—to my
C igars. All I want is for you
to buy my Cigars as against
your pipe and favorite to-
bacco —and I know you'll be
a regular customer of mine.
I’ll post a box of 100 DIADEMAS right to
your address for 22/6. Smoke 5 or 6of them,
and if they're not as good as the best 6d. Cigar
you can buy send the rest of the Cigars back,
and I’ll return every penny of your money. I'll
make no deduction for the Cigars you’ve smoked
There’s no funny business about this offer,
mind. I'll keep to every word of it, and there’ll
be no “back answers” if you want your money
back. If you want to know the kind of man
you’re dealing with, write to the General
Manager, the Commercial Bank, Melbourne,
and he’ll tell you I’m a clean, straight chap in all
my dealings.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN

“ Bulletin.”
IN BANANA LAND
The Goomerri (Burnett district) land
sales indicate the intensity of the land fever
in Queensland. Competitors came from all
over Australia to get a “cut in” at - this
private estate of 32,000 acres, surveyed off
into farms and township. One quarter-acre
town lot brought £440, and farm land sold
fiP to £l3 an acre. It is further alleged
that over 50 per cent, of the sales made were
effected with a view to speculation impure
and un-simple.
Recently 35 Macka.v sugar cockies, their
wives and families, steamed off to see how
kings are crowned. Another batch left for
Tasmania, Maoriland and Sydney. In 1897,
when the diamond jubilee was celebrated,
and later, in 1901, when Edward had an ap-
pointment with his hatter, the sugar cockies
were not represented at the London shivoo —
they were too busy grappling with the mort-
gage, and wondering where Tommy Tanna’s
half-yearly wages were to come from. This
white labor is a devastating curse all right.
“The Prodigal”: Last week’s election to
fill the late Speaker Joey Bell’s seat in the
Assembly, recalls Joey’s own first public
election. It was for the presidency of the
Cambridge University debating society. The
candidates were Joey and the student who
nowadays is the important statesman, Aus-
ten Chamberlain, M.P. The Queenslander
won by 12 votes, and Chamberlain had to
be content with the vice-presidency. Reg-i
inald McKenna (First Lord of the Admir-.
alty) was a member of Bell’s committee,
which included several future members of
Parliament of considerable tonnage. McKenna,
at the' age of 21, was anxious to secure a
seat in the Commons, and he entered the lists
against Leonard (now Lord) Courtney in
Cornwall. McKenna took the youthful Bell
with him, and the pair put up a desperate
fight against the old veteran. Bell, in this
contest, distinguished himself as a platform
orator, and especially as a master of repartee.
Colin Bell, one of Western Queensland’s
most popular squatters, was asked to contest
the late brother’s seat, but preferred to stick
to sheep.
Donald Hyndman, now chief steward of
the Rupara, had a similar position on the
Yongala for six years, and changed with
Chief Steward Mawby just before the last
voyage.
Northern Queensland, the most fertile and
most vulnerable section of this continent, is
getting “settled” at a great rate! Govern-
ment Statistician Weedon finds that the
great increases in the State’s population
have all been in the districts which were al-
ready the most thickly settled, while the dis-
trict of Somerset (which includes Thursday
Island and York’s Peninsula) shows 700
persons less than at the last census. The
Denham Government is, apparently, becom-
ing alive to tlm necessity for action, and is
remedying matters in this violent fashion:
“Arrived last week, ship Osterley, with 96
immigrants for Brisbane, five for Rock-
hampton and one for Lucinda Point, North
Queensland.”

Enthusiasm for ‘ Yes-Yes” saved the life
of Bamford, M.FI.R. He had been advising
Mackay to vote ‘Yes” on the 26th, and had
intended to go on by the Yongala; but at
th <; eleventh hour he found that there were
a ew others he wanted to talk to, so he de-
( ided to travel by the Cooma, which was sail-
iQ S some hours l later.
Mackay has an aboriginal bellman and
town crier. For some "time after the pre-
vious official passed out, the town manage#
without one, until a theatrical company
showed the way by engaging a horde of small
boys for the work. Their voices, however,
were too shrill; someone with a thousand-
fathoms-deep contralto was wanted. So
Paddy, a full-blooded aboriginal, applied for
the job and got it.
THE QUEEN THAT IS TO COME.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Cookery is now taught the renior girls in Queens-
land State schools —an innovation that is likely to be
immensely useful. —Daily Press Item.
Y O more we’ll sit and shiver at the mention
of the pie, v
JLI No more we’ll think profanely-as the pud-
ding wanders by ;
A happy day’s a-coming when a laughing lass and
clean \ .
Will hover like a goddess o’er the goods of our
cuisine;
The hash that once saluted with a flavor all its own,
The grievous chop that smote us till we left it with
a groan,
Shall all be blown to Hades by this new reforming
blast,
And their memory dark be buried in the limbo of
the past!
The chicken of the future shall be tasted in the
broth,
The dinner-beer shall greet us with a plcntitude of
froth ;
There shall be no more cinders on the margin of the
toast,
And all shall scent the Artist in the serving of the
roast;
The kitchen shall be purified of Chow and Japanee,
And she who tends the oven shall a maid of cunning
be;
And we who once grew pallid when the stew was to
the fore,
Shall hail its coming gladly and shall clamor—
“ Give us morel”
The cook who once was “greasy ” shall have perished
in her grease;
She served us most unkindly, but we only wish her
peace;
Her weakly tea will vanish, and (he grisly egg she
fried
Will follow with her mutton chop artistically dried,
And in her place a laughing witch of fricassee shall
rise,
With promise fair of luscious dish, and dainty, in
her eyes;
And we shall fill and fatten, who were one time grim
and lean,
In tribute to the maiden who is Queen of our
Cuisine.

QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, April 29, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The marrying of George Gray, the much-
sought-after bachelor son of the Hon. G. W.,
was accomplished on Thursday last with
becoming eclat. The bride was Miss Muriel
Appel, daughter of the Home Sec., and the
event was brought off at the home of the
bridegroom, with Father O’Flynn as referee.
After the guests had sipped yellow wine out
of exquisite Venetian glasses, brought out
some years ago by Mr. and Mrs. Gray,
breakfast was served with warm speech at
Appelville. Miss Muriel discarded the
usual satin and orange blossoms for a simple
swathing of net over soft silk, topped by a
black satin hat, in which white plumes
nodded. She acquired a long string of
plaited pearls from the bridegroom, and
many diamonds and an emerald or so from
the family. The moon will shine beside the
breakers at the Tweed, in your territory.
Miss Joan Hart bestowed her future upon
the gentleman of her choice at St. John’s
Cathedral the same day.. She said “Cer-
tainly” in a frock of satin and gauze, which
was the admiration of many guests at the
proceedings. The honeymooners went south.
Miss Gore, who is on the eve of a European
tour, held the bride’s “bokay” while she was
otherwise engaged, and Mr. Crouch gave
first-aid to the bridegroom.
The Government candidate, Mr. Vowles, with
the assistance of the whole Ministerial band,
won the Dalby seat, with about 100 votes
to the good, and thousands to the bad.
Premier Denham puts the meagre majority
down to defective organisation and a short-
age of polling booths, which appears to be
a vote of censure moved by Mr. Denham
against himself.
After a floating Cabinet meeting, the
Ministry came ashore again on Friday, and
Premier Denham immediately announced
the expenditure of £lOOO for the purpose of
locating the wreck of the Yongala. He also
foreshadowed a Government cold store, to
be erected upon a Government wharfalso,
he proposes to fling a -sop to the Wowser
by a stoppage of the sale of liquor in the
sugar districts. The erection of sugar
mills, as proposed by the Sugar Commis-
sion, will be given effect to. The last
item of the programme that came floating
home is that the much-needed pilot boats for
Maryborough, Rockhampton, Townsville
and Cairns are to be seen to. I hear they
are to be good solid vessels of the latest
pattern.
Talking of boats, the Otter, with the
Home Sec. aboard, will leave for Carpentaria
Gulf on Sunday. Mrs. Appel and a care-
fully selected party go along also; but
daughter and her new bridegroom have
jibbqd./ ;
There was a long table of tea drinkers on
Friday at the Eschenhagen Cafe, where Mrs.
Gore said farewell to a host of friends prior
to stepping aboard the Orient liner for Eng-
land. Another little lot was saying fare-
well to a girl who has decided to go cooking
for a man on a coffee plantation up north.
She tells me that the only food-preparers
thereabouts are saffron-tinted, so she has
been learning the culinafy art, and means to
keep her kitchen white.
The private assembly dances are to be
started in a big way at the end of May,
and Miss Denham is setting her hand- to the
lever. The Commercial Rowing Club boys
are first in the field, and their hardy annual
will open out on May 9.
The Mary fund is to close this week, I
believe, and the promoters are making the
best of a bad job.
Lange Powell, the only Queenslander to
have a canvas hung at this year’s Royal
Academy Show, was married last Wednes-
day week to Miss Moore, of Victoria. He
is an architect for his meals, and intends to
raise his own humpy in this city.
Ernie Baynes is surrounded these days
with the congratulations of horsey men
for having collected the Cup at the' Royal
Agricultural Show in Sydney, and brought it
back with him. His horse, Comet, which at
our own show did such a sensational “leap”
over the gate, secured the crockery.
The faculty has decided to hold an inaug-
ural ceremony in connection with the
Queensland University, when the undergrads,
will have their first chance in this State of
misbehaving themselves. The procession is
to be headed by the Beadle and Wee Mac-
gregor, who will put ’em all on for the oc-
casion. And how he does blossom out at
times, to be sure!
Miss Chauvel, who has been for two years
at European art schools, will be in Sydney
ere this reaches you. She has had some
nice things said of her sketches in the Cold
World.
Yours affectionately,
MARIO.
There are gathering clouds whichever way
Dig-his-own-Grave Denham looks. At Dalby
the other day, Victor Drury, solicitor and
ex-Mayor, who used to be a supporter of late
Speaker Joey Bell’s, opened one of the La-
bor candidate’s meetings with the remark
that Bell’s followers were not Denham’s,
and that, as'for himself, rather than sup-
port a Philp-Denham Government, he was
stepping in behind Labor.
Although some parts of Queensland have
been putting up rainfall records, others are
having a tough drought. On Glengyle sta-
tion, near Bedourie, there is not a blade of
grass, and, for months past, cattle have been
dropping, out from starvation. Now the
mail contractor from Boulia to Birdsville
has applied to the G.P.O. to make Bedourie
the terminus of his journey, owing to the
parched state of the country between
Bedourie and Birdsville.
“B20S” on the sugar cocky:—
There are two kinds of sugar cocky-—one who
takes up land himself, and another who buys
land from a large owner, or co. The one is equiva-
lent to a “free house,” the other to a “tied house.”
A “free house” can sell anybody’s beer it chooses,
so a free cocky, i.e., a selector, can make what use
he likes of his land. But a “tied house” must only
sell a certain brand of beer, and a “tied cocky”
must do as the large owner or co. says. When a
cocky buys land from a large owner or co., the latter
says: “We will sell you the land, provided that you
grow cane, and nothing but cane, for 10 years (or
any other stated period, but it is usually 10), and
that you sell us your cane at our price.” The pros-
pective buyer asks: “What about growing a bit of
maize and lucerne for horsefeed?” “Buy them,” is
the answer he receives. “What about a few vege-
tables ?” is his next query, to which he receives
the same answer. When a sale is made on the
foregoing terms, the cocky is not told what kinds
of cane he must grow, but he is told what kinds he
must not grow—which amounts to the same thing
at the end -of the season. Does he offer cash he is
met with, “Oh, dear no; you must buy on terms,” so
that the owner may have a hold on him. ■ Do
“Boro-Belle” and other writers see now why the
cane cocky “worships” cane, and grows nothing else?

The Inky Way.
“Condot”: Concerning ancient newspapers (B.
6/4/’ll), I have two older than those mentioned.
One is “A Perfect Diurnal of Some Passages in Par-
liament from Munday the 22. of January till Mun-
ilay the 29. of January 1649.” This reports very in-
adequately the “Tryall of Charles Stuart.” Charles
liad a hit of a quarrel with the Lord President of
the Court at the outset, and declined to plead. The
Lord President replied in a style suggestive of John-
son snubbing Boswell: “Sir, you are not to dispute
our Authority. You are told it again by the Court.
Sir, it will be taken notice of that you stand in con-
tempt of the Court.” At the conclusion of the hear-
ing the Lord President made a “large speech,” prov-
ing by law “how kings were accountable to their
people and the law, which was their superiour, and
producing severall instances of kings being deposed
and imprisoned by their subjects, especially in his
own native kingdom of Scotland, where, of 109
kings most were deposed, imprisoned or proceeded
again for misgovernment, and his owne Grand
Mother removed, and his Father, an infant, crown-
ed.” Which last is a bit puzzling, and looks as if
his Honor had been misreported. After this the
“dark” read the sentence —That he, the said Charles
Stuart, as a Tyrant, Traytor, Murtherer, and
Publique Enemy, shall be put to death by the sever-
ing of his head from his body.” An advertisement
records the publication of an early system of short-
hand —“Zeiglographra, or a new arte of short writ-
ing, more easie, exact, speedy, and short than any
heretofore, invented by Thomas Shelton, being his
last 30 years’ study.”
“Blue Blazes”: “B. de D.” (B. 6/4/’ll) whoops
for the better treatment of the “casual three-half-
pence-a-line-men,” who, he says, are in a worse
position than the rankest junior. Also he remarks:
“A casual may find himself down for an engagement.
He attends, but if no copy results no pay results.”
Neither statement is much more than quarter right.
Some linesmen may be Worse off than the rankest
junior, but not many. As a matter of fact, the
bulk of the Sydney linesmen are a long way better
off than the juniors of even the big offices. There
are linesmen in the city making even more than
senior staffsmen, though I grant they wouldn’t form
a battalion or even a cricket eleven. As for “B. de
D.’s” second statement, I can’t speak for all the
offices in all the cities, but I know that on one of
the Sydney morning papers the rule is that when a
linesman is put on the duty book he is paid 10s.
for the day and nothing for the copy.
The latest Woman’s paper (Mrs. Bull, London)
has a column of engagements, marriages and divorces.
They are published under the heading of “The Matri-
monial Egg,” thus: —-

“Bookware”: Monypenny’s new authorised “Life
of Disraeli,” Vol. 1., based on a complete run of all
the Hughenden papers, clears up the mystery of
Murray’s daily newspaper, the Representative. As
usual "the explanation is the simplest. Disraeli, then
only 20, was the potent man in the early stages, but
suddenly dropped out; and that was the puzzle.
Murray, Disraeli, sen., and Powles agreed to start
the Representative. Murray had a half share; the
other two a quarter each. Young Disraeli was’ the
splendid manager in the preliminaries; he went and
interviewed Walter Scott and Lockhart at Abbots-
ford, and actually persuaded Lockhart to take the
editorship, though Scott choked him off it. Dizzy
also engaged a lot of foreign correspondents, and did
numerous other miracles for a young man of 20.
Then he suddenly vanished out of the concern. The
explanation is that Murray found old Dizzy and
Powles had not a realisable bean. Murray, having
gone so far, ventured alone, and lost £ 26,000 in
six months. It recalls Mortimer Franklyn, the
Yankee barber of genius, who got Herbert Henty, of
Melbourne, to pour out £90,000 over the World,
Federal Australian and Victorian Review while
Henry Henty, Herbert’s partner, was away. There
might have' been a fortune in it; but Henry shut
down. Remember Tichborne Castro’s saying: “Them
as has money and no brains is made for them as
has brains and no money.”
“Scribe”: Anent the jarring between Wilson and
Mackinnon of the Aiigus (B. 23/3/’ll). Wilson
was farming at Dandenong when his indignation was
roused by the proceedings of Governor Latrobe.
He wrote a letter on the subject to the Argus,
signed “lota,” and it made such an impression that
he followed it up with others. Then he and John-
ston bought the Argus ; Wilson wrote most of the
leaders, and the Radical bias was palpable. Thus
he helped to flog up the Eureka Stockade—whereat
he drew in his horns, terrified, and gave the young
Age its chance to live. Wilson was, nevertheless,
still fully charged with Liberal sentiments, such as
had prompted his standing advertisement: “Wanted
a Governor.—Apply to the People of Victoria.”
When Wilson gave up he handed the reins to the
man of his heart, Higinbotham, who would infallibly
have blocked Synie and the Age. However, the
Mackinnon money and Fatman came in, and Higin-
botham held on for only nine months. In after years
he had the sweet revenge of “quodding” the Argus
publisher.
THE CRAFTSMAN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
For thrippence or fourpenee a golden line the poet
is plying his trade.
His coat is off, and his sleeves rolled up, lest his
laurels wither and fade;
His mind is rich with a jingle of rhymes, and his
verses bump and roll —
Oh, happy the ink and glad the pen that interpret
a poet’s soul!
Sing, happy maiden, ’neath the roses,
Blink, happy starlight, to the moon.
Every little dewdrop that he knows is
Making bread-and-butter in a tune.
There are wrongs to be righted, and roads to blaze
through the trackless forest of days,
There are foes to be smitten, and deaths to die, and
beautiful ladies to praise;
And the singer is willing to tackle the lot with his
frolicsome flutter of rhyme.
He is grinding his verses to beautiful form for
thrippence or fourpenee a time.
Dance, gleaming waves, in happy laughter.
Nod, happy flower, upon the stem,
Live in pleasant song for ever after —
Bread-and-butter shines in every gem.
There are exquisite kisses on beautiful lips, and
wonderful eyes and hair,
And slippers that flutter like small black mice at
the top of the bathroom stair.
There are kids that are shouting, and birds that
sing, and shadows that flicker and fade,
And marvellous sunsets that nobody dreams are the
tools of the rhymster’s trade.
Shine, happy sun, upon a nation,
Lie, happy statesman, long and hard ;
Every blooming thing in all creation
Turns to bread-and-butter for the bard!
N.S.W.

WHAT IS CATARRH ?
CATARRH is inflammation of the lining
membrane of the nose and adjoining
passages. If this inflammation is not ar-
rested it invades the passages which lead
from the nose to the head, ears, throat and
lungs. It injures the sight and hearing,
destroys the sense of taste and smell, ren-
ders the breath offensive, breaks down
the affected tissues, and consumes the
nasal cartilages. The discharge, passing
through the lungs and stomach, causes
dyspepsia, also consumption. Do you
want relief and cure? If so, try our
great remedy.

BibenduuTs Tyre Lectures — No. 5.
This is why the
Square Tread
is shaped as it is:
Because after exhaustive experimental work, and a long series of
practical tests, the manufacturers have ascertained that only a cover of
this particular shape can, when inflated and supporting the weight of the
car, give the best results in the wearing of the tread.
Because it augments the strength as well as the resiliency of the
rubber, besides giving it a tendency to close up any cut sustained, rather
than permitting it to gape open and provide ingress for injurious foreign
bodies. Thus it is that the Michelin Square Tread has won its pre-eminence
among all-rubber covers.

Matchless for the Complexion.
Sweethearts Still.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
SO, Sweetheart Once, you thought our
love was but a passing phase
A transient thing that would not stay
to haunt us all our days.
You thought that living love like ours, that
never knew alloy,
Could fade and die because we quaffed the
wanton wine of joy 1
Can eyes that saw the rainbow tints —and
saw them, oh ! so plain—
Turn from the lights that lit our land to
Darkness once again V
Can feet that trod the Primrose Path —no
matter where it led —
Go back to trudge the Proper Way amongst
the living dead?
Can arms that stretched imploringly, that
passionately clung,
Droop by your sides like leaden limbs while
still your heart is young V
Can burning kisses, that your lips imprinted
on mine own,
Forgotten be because the chance to kiss
again has flown?
I went my way—the same old way that
pagans went before —
Because I knew to linger meant to love you
more and more!
Because the flow’r that Youth has plucked
must only droop and die,
l said not “Au revoir,” my dear, but “Once
for all, good-bye.”
The tears were shining on your cheeks; my
heart had turned to lead —
But oh! the star that bade me go shone
brightly overhead!
You snuggle in another’s arms; but ever in
your dreams
That wanton, wayward, wand’ring star like
Flmo’s beacon gleams !
We had our loves, our early loves, that
faded with the past,
But when we met, the love we knew might
well have been our last.
For through the later love its burns, and
sears them with its flame,
Till all your wedding robes become the scar-
let cloaks of shame !
But go your way, who loved me once, and
take this for a sign
That though our muffled voices call, and all
the Fates combine
To draw us each to each again in dark for-
bidden ways,
I shall in silence pay the debt the Haunter
ever pays—-
The awful debt of loneliness, when in an-
other’s arms,
The love I had and flung away expends its
sacred charms.
’Twere better had we never met —and yet
that little while
Was worth a million years of love in Ortho-
doxy’s style!
’Twas worth what we have suffered since,
in silence, and alone!
’Twas worth what we shall suffer yet—to
all but us unknown!
'Twas worth it all—ten score of times ! And
yet—and yet, to-day,
I almost wish the Fateful roads had turned
some other way!
No, Mine of Old, our love was not a lightly-
passing phase—•
A transient thing that could not stay to
haunt us all our days.
We loved —and oh ! such love as ours would
never brook alloy,
Nor fade and die because we quaffed the
wanton wine of joy.
Ah, Sweetheart Once, your memory pro-
vokes the old mad thrill. . . .
You’ll go your way, and I’ll go mine— but
we are sweethearts still!
N.S.W.
GILROONEY.
The Babe of Tauakira.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I HAD more than a suspicion that I was
bushed. Hartigan had said I should
come out on a ridge by 8, and here it
was close upon 4, and I was still struggling
through dense scrub. Hills, sky, everything,
were shut out by the impenetrable foliage.
Suddenly I broke through a scrub wall,
and found myself in a clearing, and when my
blinking eyes had got accustomed to the
glare of the sunlight, I saw a small slab
cottage at the far end of it. In front was
the wreck of a garden, and right against me
a cherry tree laden with fruit.
No smoke rose from the chimney of slabs
and clay. All around were signs of the re-
turn of the wilderness, and over all brooded
the sombre stillness of the Tauakira bush.
The deserted home had a sinister atmosphere,
and a sense of eeriness made me grip the
billhook a little tighter in my hand as I ad-
vanced towards it.
The ruined door was partly open. I
knocked, but was answered only by the echo
of an empty house. I stepped inside —and
stepped out again more quickly.
For on the floor three skulls and some scat-
tered bones lay in the sunlight which streamed
through the glassless gap of the window.
One skull was little, evidently that of a
child. It made my teeth rattle, and my
knees shook. A feeling of intense depression
swept over me. That little skull was pa-
thetic.
Looking, round I saw that the walls were
riddled with bullet-holes, and on the slab
floor, littered, over with cartridge case heads,
were still discernible dark, wicked-looking
stains. And I picked up a faded red bootee.
I looked round, and the slanting sunrays
called me back to the unpleasantness of my
own position. But the hills rose to my
right, and, far away, just discernible in the
dancing haze, the white flag that marked
Hartigan’s position gleamed like silver
against the purple, of the bush.
• t°°k my bearings, and hurried off, for
night comes down quickly in the bush of
Maoriland’s North Island. Through the clear-
? n ?, ,r s P e< ?> Past some tumble-down out-
buildings, into the bush. There, bearing
always to the north-east, I found the rising
ground, and after an hour’s anxious scram-
bling over slippery roots I made the ridge.
Through the trunks of the open mountain
busli gleamed the fitful flashes of Hartigan’s
fire, and 20 minutes later I swung into
camp.
“Thought you was bushed,’ lie said, as I
threw down my load and reached for the
pannikin.
“We’ll go down and bury them in the
morning,” he said, when I had told the story.
“Fancy ’em lying there all these years! Ter-
rible, ain’t it? I remember the story now,
but I never knew it ended just that way.
“It was when the soldiers was at Pipiriki
that Brown and Monty Piper came up and
bought that land from the Maoris. There
was, of course, Mrs. Brown and the young
boy—he’s the little one, I expect.
“There were more white people about here
then than now, and it was common talk that
Piper and Mrs. Brown were more than
friends. The place fairly hummed with
gossip. Only Brown said nothing—every-
body else talked their heads off. Brown took
no notice. He was a quiet, slow-thinking
sort of chap, but when the last baby arrived
he took Monty outside and suggested it was
time for him to get away. He was afraid
that in about another week he’d lose his
temper, especially if Monty stayed. Monty
looked at him and said nothing, but he got
inside, packed his swag, and left without
saying good-bye.
“When he got into Pipiriki he camped
close to the fort and kept himself to himself.
He gave no explanation why he had left
Brown, but, of course, none was needed.
“The second week he was there came the
Big Rising—came like a thunderclap, in the
form of an attack on the, town by a crowd
of howling Hauliaus, who nearly carried the |
place with the first rush. What a scrap it
was ! The whites used the bayonet when
they had time to fix it; but most of them
just swung the heavy end of the gun, and
after about 30 of the liveliest minutes I
ever saw —and I’ve seen some rough ones —
the niggers were belted out, and what
couldn’t walk were dropped into the river.
“Monty was in the middle of that scrap,
and when it was over he begged the Colonel to
give him just 10 men to go out and save his
baby. The Colonel wouldn’t hear of it. lie
felt certain that every white within 40 miles
had been killed and eaten. So he forbade
Monty to leave the place—he wanted all
good fighters, he said by way of compliment.
“That night Monty was on sentry-duty
with me, right up against the Ilauhau line.
And it was just when the darkness was
about at its worst that he said to me: ‘I
think I can hear someone stirring over
there. I’ll just work a ring on him.’
“And with that he drops down on all fours
and waddles off, looking for all the world
like a big black beetle.
“I waited an hour or more, and then
called to him. No answer. So I crept
out myself and saw some large individual
lying full length under a bit of a bush.
“I plunked a bullet into him without a
word, and when the patrol came scurrying
up we found he was the biggest Maori I'vi
ever seen. He had his throat cut, and wa;
lying stark naked on the grass. His mat-
had all gone, and he had been scalped. But
there was no sign of Piper. Then I took a
tumble.
“He’d gone to see what had happened at
the farm. My hair rose at the thought. I
saw the whole dodge. He was dark, and
only wanted a Maori’s curling hair and
feathers and mats to look a real Maori.
Somewhere there was rushing through the
bush, as hard as he could go, a brown-facet,
white man, wearing the scalp and mats of
a Maori warrior—whew !
“Ten minutes later it was found that
the bush behind the Maoris’ main position
was on fire, and it staved off any further
attack that night. Then a mist fell, and it
began to drizzle —the sort of drizzle that
chills a Maori to the bone and makes him
keep under cover or doubles him up with
rheumatism if he doesn’t.
“When the business was reported to the
Colonel, he said it was desertion, but that
Monty was a mighty brave deserter. The
baby, he was sure, had been eaten at least
24 hours before, and now Monty was in a
fair way to be eaten, too.”
Then the old bushman knocked the ashes
out of his pipe, and said something about
turning in.
“But what happened—how did it end?”
“Oh, it ended all right. We was relieved
and the rebels chased to glory.”
“But how did Piper get on?”
“From that day to this I’ve never heard
of him.”
And there ended Hartigan’s story of the
Babe of Tauakira.
A little later I was running an agency
business in wet, windy Wellington, and
living in a pub, where we sat round the
fire o’ nights and spun yarns. I
told of the Babe of Tauakira, and
produced the bootee. Two lady board-
ers who were present wept. One of
them did it gracefully and the other did it
any old way. Two days later a newspaper
man called on me. He got the yarn, and in
due time it appeared, with touching al-
lusions to the bootee.
A few weeks later I was sitting dejectedly
in my little office, wondering if I was ever
going to do any more busI’ness, 1 ’ness, when there
came a tap at the door, followed by the ap-
pearance of a brown face, fringed with giey
whiskers and gemmed with two very dark
brown eyes—or, rather, eyes of a faded
black. He came in and looked at me
quizzically.
“What can I show you?” I inquired.
“That bootee the yarn in the paper was
about?”
“With pleasure!” I remarked, thinking he
was some curious individual who wanted to
test the truth of the things newspapers
printed, and I gave it to him.
“How much?” he inquired.
“Not for sale. Why do you want it?”
“But I’ll give you any price within rea-
son. I’ll give you £lO, if you like.”
“Well, I don’t like, and I won’t have it.
There’s romance about that bootee.”
“Much more for me than for you !” he an-
swered. “Name your price, man. I’m
Montague Piper.”
And after that there was a hiatus, chieflv
filled in with drinks.
* * * *
“I had been watching the Maori scout for
about an hour,” said Montague Piper. “He
couldn’t scout for nuts. I crept along just
(Continued on next page.)
