in his track, for I had got behind him. My
trouble was that some of his gang might be
tailing up my course. When he got where
I wanted him, I spoke in Maori. He turned
with a start, and met a blade. He never
spoke. .He had no time. I made sure of
him and scalped him, took his mats, and
pulled his great scalp over my skull cap.
I hurried down to the river, where I threw
my clothes into the water, and donned the
mats. In that disguise I could pass through
the Maori lines without much trouble.
“It took me less than 20 minutes to pass
through them and reach the track that led
to the farm. To make things easy I set
fire to the bush, and tickled up the besiegers
a little.
“But the bush was full of packs of howl-
ing Hauhaus, coming in with plunder from
all quarters, or merely hurrying to take part
in the sack of Pipiriki. Of course they made
a devil of a row to scare the Taipo, so by
the time they got close I was under cover.
I had a great temptation to blaze among
them, for I felt almost certain that some
of them had helped to murder my boy, and if
they had murdered him I knew they had
eaten him. Still there was a chance, so I
saved my cartridges. It was only six miles
to the farm, but, what with the darkness,
the slippery going, and dodging the mobs of
Hauhaus that came tearing through the bush,
it took me three, hours to do it.
“I got mighty cautious as I approached
the farm, and carefully avoided anything like
a track; and at last I stood on the edge of
the clearing, just concealed by the scrub,
and saw about a dozen Maoris feasting, in
the light of a blazing fire.
“I knew what they were feasting on. An-
other crowd stalked in, and were invited to
take part in the feast. There was plenty
for all. I listened, wondering if they would
give any hint of how many they had killed.
As soon as the newcomers had settled down
I worked round to the back of the house, and
made a dash for it. I got in safely, but
there was no living person there. I got
out the way I had come, and, climbing a
tree, waited for the niggers to gorge them-
selves with their feast. Right beneath was
the stable, and, as I sat there watching the
Maoris dropping off to sleep one by one, I
thought I heard a faint wail —mufiled some-
what, but insistent.
“Like a flash I was down the tree and into
the stable, where I was greeted with a
muffled “Yah!” I groped to • the manger,
plunged my hand in and found straw, and
underneath it was the babe, with his clothes
piled on top of him. His bottle was beside
him, and, to my delight, it was still half
lull. I placed the mouthpiece where it was
wanted most,. and then I watched the gang
through the opening in the slabs, and tried
to think out a plan of escape. I had not
much hope of Pipiriki holding out, as both
ammunition and food were short. On the
uther hand it was 45 miles as the bird flew
to safety, and nearer 90 the way I would
have to go. But I made up my mind there
was nothing for it but to work through the
bush and strike the Wanganui lower down
at night-time, steal a canoe, and make a dash
for it. But before we went I wanted —oh,
I badly wanted —to kill some of those swine.
“Round the fire they were all asleep now,
save one big brute, who sat with his gun
across his knees, on guard, and every hair of
his head alert.
“And while I wondered how to do for him
he rose, looked round, and loped into the
house. He came out the back way, and pro-
ceeded to have a look round the various out-
houses. At last he came towards the stable.
“I was behind the door. He thrust his
light inside cautiously and looked round.
Then he stepped inside. He never spoke.
He was the deadest Maori you ever saw.
Then I stole out to the fire, and in that
light I reckon I looked as good a Maori as
any' of them. Anyhow they were full of
food, and half-full of drink. I sneaked away
their guns, and then commenced to kill.
Three who were sleeping apart I wiped out
with the cold steel, and then as one sat up
I stepped behind a tree and blew a hole
through him. As fast as I could snatch up
the guns I shot them down.
“They were sluggish and scared, bewild-
ered by drink and noise. and smoke, unable
to see in the semi-darkness (for the fire had
burned low). They thought a regiment was
upon them, and fled howling, dashing into
trees and stumps as they ran One ran to-
wards the stable where the child was. 1
ran with him, and stabbed him as we
bounded along.
“So I got the boy, and, wrapping him in
the shoulder mat of one of the dead Maoris,
I fled into the bush, and soon put a good
couple of miles between me and the farm.
Then I stopped to rest. Hardly was I seated
when I heard a twig crack in the distance.
So I wasn’t alone.
“I found a Huge rata, with a great hole
in its trunk. Into this I slipped slowly,
taking the babe with me, and found myself
in a space as large as a small cave.
“There I lay, and, in spite of all my ef-
forts, went to sleep. When I woke the light
was shining faintly through the opening in
the side of the rata. The boy was still
slumbering; I almost feared he was dead. I
waited to hear sounds from without, but
none came.
“Then the child commenced to cry, whim-
pering weakly, and I realised. he had to be
fed somehow or he would die. So I ven-
tured out prepared to kill him if there were
Maoris about. But no one was in sight.
“I tramped on dofircedly, the babe whim-
pering and hbwling in turns, and then
relapsing into fitful sleep. About o’clock
I looked down from the end of the spur on
to the Maori town of Ivoriniti, which was
swarming with natives to such .an extent
that I knew there had been a big increase in
population. As there was no sign of destruc :
tion I guessed that the people of Koriniti
had fraternised with the rebels, if they had
not actually joined them. My chief object
in life was a cow. I had washed the bottle
as well as I could at a creek, and the babe
was ravenous. But there wasn’t a vestige
of a sign of a cow.
“I tramped on another couple of miles,
keeping off the track and struggling through
undergrowth and manuka scrub, with the
child wrapped up in my mat to keep him
from being scratched. The strap of my
rifle chafed my naked shoulders, and the
flies and the heat were awful. Suddenly I
came to a wall of thick manuka. Down be-
low on the track I could hear the noise of
many voices, but I struggled on through the
scrub till I suddenly blundered into a con-
siderable clearing, containing about 40 cows
—all the cow property of the Koriniti dis-
trict, in fact. Evidently the Konniti people
didn’t trust their friends overmuch. A'Maori
woman suddenly rose from under a detached
bush, and looked at me and the babe in
questioning, wide-eyed wonder. On her
back was the little bundle that told she
Was a mother in being. I put it to her in
her own language that if ever there was a
chance for her to do a good deed it was
right now. , ,
“Well, she took the boy from me, fondled
him, smiled archly, and next moment he
was too busy to yell. As he dined she mo-
tioned me to follow her, and leading the wa\
she pulled up in another clearing large
enough only to stretch ones legs in. There
she sat and suckled the babe until he went
right off to sleep. Then she gave him back
to me and vanished. I sat there wondering
if she would bring an armed party, but in-
stead she brought a part of a pigeon, a piece
of sucking pig, some camp-oven bread, and a
billy of milk. When 1 had eaten, she led me
to a cave, which made a home of a sort.
“I lived three days in that cave, and the
Maori woman brought me victuals and took
the babe to the little clearing for fresh air
and food, and brought him back scrubbed
and freshened up. ,
“At the end of the third day she came
after dark, and leading me down to a se-
cluded nook by the river, showed me two
“ ‘Take the smaller one and go, she said.
‘Once you get to Makirikiri you are sate.
They are all Queen Wickitoris men there.
Tell* them not to kill us when they come, for
the women of Ivoriniti have sent this pake-
ha babe and you as a peace offering.’
“It was a night of darkness and driz-
zling rain. The river was high and ran fast,
and with hard paddling I made good pro-
gress. Passing villages I just drifted so as
not to start the dogs barking. The babe
slept under the mat, with his bottle, heedless
of the gamble in which he was taking so
prominent a part. ’, , ~ .
“The east was just flushing when suddenly
round the bend came a great boat, propelled
by oars. Rifle barrels flashed out, and 1
was watched suspiciously. Then with a
bound the boat came alongside, and a white
officer looked at me inquiringly. I dropned
the paddle, and, grasning the babe, banded
him aboard the other boat. Ihen I tell
overboard. , , ,
“I was rescued promptly enough, and when
1 woke up I was in the camp at Makirikiri,
where the force for the relief of Pipiriki was
being gathered. I gave them the message of
the Maori woman of Koriniti, and hearing
that the child was well, turned over and
Went to sleep. , ..
“Oh, ves, he’s still well. And it was a
good speculation for the people of Koriniti.
It didn’t seem to be clearly proved that they
had done anvthing very serious, and they
got the benefit of the doubt. I think they
also got some cows that the rebels left be-
hind them in their retreat. Yes, the babe
paid a big dividend at Koriniti. ’
C. A. JEFFRIES.
The Song of the Little Men.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A GLEAM of green on a spinning sphere
Half-lit by the sun’s white rays,
And the great Earth wheels to, the
forming year
Down the silent starry ways ;
From the swarming hives of her human
freight—
Palace, and shrine, and den —•
A song streams up to the gods’ high gate,
The Song of the Little Men.
“O Brethren, now is the end of quests,
Life and desires are vain; .
Fold hands, fold hands on your sinless
And back to your sleep again!
We have cleared our hearts of all vice ab-
horred,
Save little deceits and mean;
We can front the stroke of the Future s
sword, ,
With our bloodless souls washed clean!
“We have strangled Ilate, and his vile corpse
sways
With Passion, a prey for birds,
We have pecked at Love with our milk-meek
phrase
Till we slew him with love-like words ;
We have chilled each soul, and its springing
Quenched deep ’neath a prayer-bleak
Yea, purged our life of its fierce desires
And all that the old gods gave.
“Our earth is freed of the sword and lance.
Where now are the heroes born
Who once sprang swift to the peril s chance,
With a joyous laugh of scorn?
They are gone, they are gone, with their
fighting hands,
And breasts that braved the gales,
As they urged their steeds to the unknown
lands — .
To the unknown deeps, their sails; _ >
They are gone, who forced on Oppression s
throngs '
Their stern unyielding will. .
Who made the deeds, and who voiced the
songs
That echo, echo still.
“No voice of old may revive our caste,
No call of the Hero-roll:
Did the phantom legions thunder past,
They could not wake our soul.
‘The meek inherit the. darts,
Brothers, can touch our reign l
Fold hands, fold hands, o’er your sinless
hearts,
And back to your sleep again !
A gleam of green on a spinning sphere,
Half-lit by the sun’s white rays, .
And the great Earth wheels to the growing
year,
Down the silent, starry ways ;
The great Earth wheels through the day
and night,
To spaces beyond our ken;
And deep at her heart is a spreading Might—
The Song of the Little Men.
N.S.W.
HUGH McKAY.
